THE RED PAGE.
The Other Kendall.
Henry clarence kendall has
been variously set before the world, by
biographers more or less competent'; but
always as one who, save for some adven-
titious or involuntary moments of lucidity and
reason, needed to be apologised for. There was
another Kendall, who, it seemed to me, did not
require or favour apology in any sort whatever ;
who was very much disposed to take the world
fighting; whose lapses were adventitious or
involuntary; and whose actual self, otherwise
established and might have stood out
and been remembered, courageous and strong. I
had a letter from that Kendall some five-and-
twenty years ago. He was at Camden Haven
then; I up above Tumberumba, where diggers
fossicked, cattle duffers lurked, and the polite
world seemed remote. Kendall’s letter was not all
polite. It harmonised with a good many things,
living and lying about.
I envy you your camp under Kosciusko ; you ought to
do something up there, at least you will have a better
chance than in the bitter, bad Bohemia of either Sydney
or Melbourne. And if you eschew Bohemia and get
amongst high-class journalism, as you say (I never found
it), the people to whom you sell yourself will emasculate
you. If you do,anything virile and they accept, it will
duly appear an eunuch.... You think you will find com*
panionship, do you ? /And with congenial surroundings a
man may live a cleanly life and still keep his inspirations
warm. I tell you you were never so lonely in 27 deg.
South-West as you will bein Collins or Pitt street. If you
have got any’ genius, or if any genius has got you, keep
away ! Take it with you to another place of being.
Genius is worse used in the cities of Australia than ever
was pearl by’ swine, and the man in whom it lurks, as a
disease, is just broken yip like oyster-shell and pitched on
to the middens. Did not your friend, Marcus Clarke,
tell you that? He published the fact in even plainer
terms, and yet is himself crook-backed and broken-kneed
with bowing to Rimmon. And yet the Jews and the
bailiffs are with him, and will be till the end.
Kendall, by tlie way, did not like Clarke;
could never even discover excellence in that good
bit of genuine Australian prose, the introduction
to Gordon’s poems.
When we met in Sydney a year or two later
Kendall’s residence was still Camden Haven ;
his occupation school-teaching and measuring up
timber for the sawyers. The only regular literary
work he was “ permitted to do ” took the shape of
odd paragraphs in “The Meddler” column of The
Sydney Mail. I told a political friend, a pretty
good fellow, too, of Kendall’s expected arrival.
“ Last time I saw him,” said he, “ he was coming
out of that building at the corner of Pitt and
O’Connell-streets which looks like a penitentiary
for the correction of literary aspirants. His coat
was green with wear, and split down the back.’
“ Did you take him round to David Jones or do
the St. Martin business on his behalf ? ” The
political gentleman went away with a snort.
When I met Kendall he was not comely. There
was nothing about him physically or sartorially to
beget desire; but once he began to talk none but
a fool could affect contempt. The Lord God had
put a soul in him, and that soul burned on his
tongue and lightened through his eyes.
Swinburne’s “ Thalassius ” was just out then.
I had reviewed it, and was a bit bewitched per-
haps with the wild waves roaring, and tall cliffs
ringing, and the “indomitable soul” which posed
just a little in the frequent climaxes. Kendall
would have none of it. None either of that stuff
which seems to be echoed in some of his own
work (not the best). “Swinburne began,” he
said, “with Atalanta- in Calydon, and -ought
either to have finished there or built accurately
and harmoniously on that foundation. Nothing
in English like the ‘Atalanta.’ If you make
proper allowance for the inferior vehicle of the
thought, I doubt if there is anything in Greek
beyond it. It is virile as a fragment broken off
the Iliad, relentless as (Edipus, musical, sweet,
bitter true—aye :
The gods very subtly fashion
Madness with sadness upon earth ;
Not knowing in any wise compassion
And holding pity in little worth."
“Browning did Greek into English pretty
well,” I ventured.
“Browning! I hate ninety lines out of every
hundred Browning wrote. He only held a panni-
kin full of English, and was always stirring it up
to confusion and murkiness to make people think
it was a vast, mysterious ocean. When he lets
it flow freely, at times there is a rivulet of verse,
clear as crystal. His big things ! You might as
well look for a vision of Paradise in a fortune-
teller’s glass, or for the form and colour of
a thunder-storm in a painted pudding-basin, as for
anything actually great in them. I like his
‘Count Gismond/but it lacks grace. -If Long-
fellow, now, had let himself go—given the bit of
devil that was in him a chance —he would have
gone high.” .
“ Browning seems to express that sort of thing
very well. You will remember the passage—
The sin I impute to each frustrate ghost
Is the ungirt loin and the unlit lamp...”
“ Excellent Browning, yes ! Excellent text for
an excellent sermon. You have quoted it before,
and you worry me. You know nothing of this
city or this people. Isaiah might walk amongst
them as he walked through Egypt for a wonder
and a sign, and they would discover no more than
Egypt did ; you will preach and quote till the
abomination of the desolation gets into the marrow
of your own bones, and then ”
“ What then?”
“Shape your own ‘then.’ Go under the rod!
Hang on to the staff of the dominant conven-
tionalities ! Or. drink, drug, dice, drab! What
does it matter?”
The furrows of his face hardened like crinkles
in molten metal set while in motion. Kendall
had the hardest-lined face I ever saw. It was
like the “old Hickory” of Abraham Lincoln, or the
chiselled granite of Carlyle. But all the hell he
had endured left no bale-fire in his eyes. They
showed clear and kindly always, as did Jonathan
Swift’s, in the worst of his madness.
It was difficult to get him to talk about himself
or his work unless the aggressive bitterness within
drove him to assertion rather than complaining.
“ A man has to do his duty in those places wherein
it pleases this generation to place him. I hey
“ pound ” him generally, as the wise men of Gotham,
U.S., did Pegasus. But there is always a
treadmill in the pound, and he has no wings. I
have to do my six hours daily up such steps as
two score of bush infants provide. And, by way
of variety, take a turn with measuring tape and
pencil over a few thousand logs and planks of
cedar. And, why don’t I do something really
big ? It is practicable, of course, and easy.
Charles Badham would make or maintain a repu-
tion for scholarship if he had to serve six hours
a day as amanuensis to Henry Parkes.”
We turned, or tried to turn, towards the recom-
penses, the consolations. He was hard, far-
reaching, and absolutely logical. “Only fools,
and people who have not been there, talk about
consolation and recompense. An angel only
comes to you to impose a task. He flashes the
inspiration, the thought which begets obligation.
And he will swing a knotted scourge later if you
do not take that obligation up.
“‘Thoughts in hours of insight willed ’ is the
text of the proper discourse, is it not ? Only in
the context, if Matthew Arnold had been scorched
a bit hotter, he would have written ‘ must ’ be,
not ‘may.’ ‘Must be through days of gloom
fulfilled.’ Woe be to you if fulfilment fails ! ”
“ But with fulfilment, achievement ? *
Heopened hiseyeswide. “Oh, yes,lhavefulfilled
and achieved. I wrote a cantata for the Exhibi-
tion, and half f tdfilled some obligations in the way
of boots and store bills. And another poem of
occasion brought in another hundred pounds.
And they published me in the papers ; and a few
folks would have had me out to their houses on
show. Oh, yes, there are rewards for fulfilment
and achievement. You may almost hope to be
allowed to lick the fleshpots with scullions per-
manently if—you succeed 1”
He was in a bitter mood. It was difficult to
find him earnest, communicative, and otherwise.
One day, a spring day, at Manly (it was before
the best friend we ever had went into .Norman-
Swiss architecture on the middle hill) he came
momentarily into accord with his own best verse,
and spoke out of his own best nature. There were
perfectly delightful dreamy afternoons in the
Manly of those days. The shadows lay motionless
on the grass. The water on the hither side was
still as tue sky above, and the sea lark scarce
twittering about the farther rocks. Light, large
and glad, and all things germinal of whatever
seed of grace lay buried in one’s conscious being.
His eyes grew soft and limpid. The querulous
speech died in a long silence ; and those rare tears
that are the outward and visible sign of the
blossoming of a strangely quickened soul, formed
arid fell. I think it was rather in self-commune
than conversation that he spoke.
“The soft, white feet of afternoon” were all
about us, and he quoted from the poem wherein
that line shines:
Yea, in my dream of fall and brook,
By far sweet forests furled
1 see the light for which I look
In vain through all the world ...
The world is round me with its heat
And toil and cares that tire ;
1 cannot with my feeble feet
Climb after my desire.
Cannot! Will not, says the world; shall not,
Fate. . . “Sir,” said Dr. Johnson, “ let us take a
walk down Fleet-street! ”
After that day I saw Kendall no more. He
went back to Camden Haven; he went out
on that well-meant but fatal appointment as
chief forester; he ' came back to Sydney, and
died, and was buried.
Ah ! let him sleep ! Surely he takes his fill
Of deep and liquid rest, forgetful of all ill.
So W-B.D. ordered the inscription on his monu-
ment. ’ Some others of us would have had it: “He
began to interpret the parable of Australia.” But
that also is with things which do not matter much.
What really did, and does, and will tor ever
matter, is the mistake made about him his
little life long. Even the Bohemians of to-day
know little of the bitter loneliness of that life.
In Sydney or Melbourne then there was no
literary or artistic companionship of any sort
whatever. A few eminent people toyed with art
and literature in a dilettante style; but their
patronage was only to be won by subservient
respectability. The press did nothing. TillGresley
Lukin got hold of The Queenslander, Kendall or
Gordon or Brunton Stephens would not have been
offered payment for contributions in verse to any
weekly paper in Australia ; and The Queenslander
did not long uphold its unfamiliar departure. A
good deal of genius was stifled in those days, and
it may be that a few in whom the true passion was
strong choked it out, as resolute men do constantly
with a drink, drug or gambling habit. It made a
worse wreck of some lives than any of those habits.
Various developments have made the literary
and artistic life just a little better nowadays.
Kendall was unfortunate, too, in his failure to
grip on with the few really able men of the time.
Charles Badham was chief amongst them. I went
with others to beard that old lion in his den, at a
time when a project was afoot to commemorate
the Australian poet by a something in connection
with the Australian University. He was not with
us in that particular way. He did not seem to
think that an Australian Muse had been, or for a
few generations would be born. An annual
Kendall prize would be fitting enough ; but, for
the doing of a bit of Horace or Virgil into
English ! The old professor came down to the big
meeting whereat a good many people bore belated
testimony, and we hoped that he might repeat for
us the old “Uncouth, unkenn’d, unkissed.” But
it was not so. “ Dando ! dando ! dando ! ” said he
in conclusion, “Give ! give! give! but not the
laurel.” He would gather up anything ever
Australia could produce in that way and lay it in
reverence on those old Italian tombs. If they
meet (the master and the poet) in that pleasant
limbo of Dante’s, outside the Inferno’s first circle
(where it would be natural to seek both) —who will
apologise ?
F.M.

Phil Mav died on August 5. London papers
say that he had suffered from consumption for several
years, and for months it had been evident that the end
was near. Critical notices of May’s work are pitched in
the highest key. “By Phil May’s death,” says Ihe St.
James’s Budget, “ the world of art has suffered an
irreparable loss. It is no exaggeration to say that ne
revolutionised line drawing, and no artist ever had more
slavish imitators. ... No one has ever been a greater
master of light and shade, and his technique was perfect.
As a line worker he stood on a pinnacle by himself, and a
certain well-known art critic spoke truthfully when he
said : ‘ Black and White is summed up in two words,
The Academy unkindly says that it was when May’s
powers began to wane that Punch discovered him. IM
Athenaeum says his death at the early age of thirty-nine
was not unexpected, “ but is no less a loss to the worm,
since he was unequalled in his own style.” And the praise
of The Athenaeum is always conspicuously cautious.
New Conceptions in Science, by Carl Snyder. A
book all the libraries should buy from Our Advertiser: it
is not worth 9s. to an individual. Snyder reviews the
very latest discoveries in a series of articles, some °i
which have appeared in the magazines. Incidentally,
one may see the psychology of the schoolmen miore
definitely exploded, and David Syme’s soul sent flying
still further into the mist of anthropocentric fflythus. An
interesting and stimulating book, that should be sold at
about three-and-sixpenoe.
"War Is Hell.”
“ "f'kRITISH victory in the Soudan !
i-C The enemy clung obstinately to the
I 9 trenches and were bayoneted in
them.
Nothing could have been finer than the behaviour
of the troops/’
Nothing finer, indeed !
White Christian soldiers, three thousand miles
from home,, in the pay of white Christian
bondholders, bayoneting black Mohamme-
dans for defending their native land, and
setting the example of bloodshed to brown
Mohammedans whom they had already trained
to slaughter.
Good God, is it too much to hope tha.t the day
may come when every sane man will shrink
from running a bayonet into a fellow creature
as he would now shrink from murdering a
baby? .
We look back with pity, contempt, and detestation
on the times of the rack and wheel and fagot
—we who are still in the thick of the Dark
Ages ourselves.
A t rousandfold better to be a true Mussulman
Dervish fighting for his home than one of
these Christian hypocrites emphasising their
barbarian butcheries with chaplains and
crosses and Te Deums and every kind of
shameless lie and blasphemy.
«£*
How they buzzed round about the fires of Smith-
field,
The biacK, perverse, froward, reverend clergy !
Like June beetles round the hall lamp,
Teaching the Gospel and knowing not the first
word of it —
More cruel, revengeful, bloodthirsty than the
ignorant mob they instructed—
Blind, malignant, pompous leaders of the blind.
And so to-day round the fires of war—the flash of
artillery .and glance of bayonets
(But at safe distance impotent)
Again the dismal brood swarms, bysterioal,
smirking, grimacing—
Still as oblivious of all their Master taught,
Still going further than the thoughtless populace
in their lust and frenzy,
Still impious, blasphemous, sacrilegious, profane—
Gloating like harpies over the nation’s sins.
&
Hail to the hero 1
Decked out in blue, red, and gilt, as in war-
paint—
Rejoicing like a savage in a long head-feather and
gold shoulder fringes—
Proud to commit with these adornments all the
crimes for which he would be disgraced and
punished as a felon without them ;
Modestly bearing on his breast a star and ribbon
which say, “I am a hero,” as plainly as the
beggar’s placard says “ I am blind.”
Followed by a brass band and bass drum, which
screw up his courage at a pinch like the war
dance and the tom-tom of the Central African
and redskin;
Vain of his manliness in the field while indulging
in effeminate quarreling over the honours, at
the rate of a month’s quarreling to a half-
hour’s fighting ;
Admitting that he obeys orders without thinking,
and thus proclaiming his complete abdication
of conscience and intellect;
Rushing home from the fray to advertise himself
in the newspapers—
Hail to the hero !
O shade of Cervantes !
Come back and draw for us another Don Quixote.
Prick this bubble of militarism as you pricked
that other bubble of knight-errantry.
Come and depict the hero !
1 . ' ''
(From the German of Bodenstedt.)
Talk, if you will, of hero deed,
Of clash of arms and battle wonders ;
But prate not of your Christian creed
Preached by the cannon’s murderous thunders.
And if your courage needs a test.
Copy the pagan’s fierce behaviour ;
Revel in bloodshed east and west,
But speak not with it of the Saviour.
The Turk may wage a righteous war
In honour of his martial Allah :
But Thor and Odin live no more—
Dead are the gods in our Valhalla.
Be what you will, entire and free,
Christian or warrior—each can please us ;
But not the rank hypocrisy
Of warlike followers of Jesus.
Take up the White Man’s burden,
Send forth ypur sturdy kin,
And load them down with Bibles,
And cannon-balls and gin.
Throw in a few diseases
To spread the tropic climes,
For there the healthy niggers
Are quite behind the times....
Take up the White Man’s burden,
And if you write in verse,
Flatter your nation’s vices,
And strive to make them worse.
Then learn that if with pious words
You ornament each phrase,
In a world of canting hypocrites
This kind of business pays.
— Ernest Crosby, in Swords and Plowshares
(Lond., Grant Richards, 1903).
POTT'S POINT & THE PRISON
“ The British naval authorities have build a
prison on Garden Island, opposite Potts Point.
jr ABY SYDNEY VEBE DE VERE,
B You set the fashion of the town :
m You dance with haughty aides-de-camp;
* Your Paris dresses ivin renown ;
But when the prison opposite
Brings family skeletons to view,
My poor “ colonial aristocrat,”
What will you do 1 what will you do f
2 hose sheer-legs. Lady
Vere de Vere,
Already souvenirs evoked:
Not yet a hundred years
have flown
Since your progenitrix was
choked.
Oh your sweet eyes, your
low replies:
A social leader you may be;
But there was that , around
her throat
Which you had scarcely
cared to see.
Lady Sydney Vere de Vere,
We know from what base loins you come :
Your naughty g.g.-sire prigged spoons,
Your grand-sire made his pile in rum ;
And yet your nose is classical,
You thrill us with patrician charms:
—Since you defy the stud-book thus
What is, the use of coats-of-arms ?
Trust me, Sydney
Vere de Vere,
From yon blue hea-
vens above us bent
The London cut-purse
and his Moll
Smile at the claims of
long descent:
Howe’er it be, it seems
to me
’ Tis only noble to have
. cash; ' <
Bank shares are more
than coronets,
I And real estate than Norman dash.
Sydney, Sydney Vere de Vere,
There stands a spectre in your hall;
Then why resuscitate its twin
To haunt you on a prison wall'{'
Oh, teach your ancestors to rest
Where you have put ’em, lying low:
And tell the foolish A dmiralty
To let the foolish prison go.

VOU need to take particular care at this time of year
. against colds and chills, more especially when throw-
ing off Winter Underclothing for lighter Summer wear,
and the only material which may be relied upon as ar
absolute safeguard from the effects of sudden climat „
changes is VERTEX CELLULAR.
When one wears underclothing of this fabric, he or he
is safe from “ taking a chill” as, owing to its p. .uliar
structure, it operates to retard a too rapid evaj ration
of the body’s natural heat in its insensible vapour—the
cause of the “ chill ” —allowing simply any excess of over-
heated vapour to disperse gradually and imperceptibly.

epidermis and acts, in fact, as a supplementary skin, and
for this reason so many medical men order it for invalids
and delicate children, and recommend it to all their
patients for general wear all the year round.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Fast.
We dug him a grave by the sea-shore,
We covered him over with sand,
We hid him away for ever,
There, where the wave wooes the land.
We wandered to distant countries,
Tossed on the ocean wave ;
His very name was forgotten
A s he slept in his sandy grave.
Once more in the gathering twilight
We stood on that wave-kissed shore,
And we gazed on the crested billows
As oft in the days of yore.
And there, on the shell-strewn sea-shore,
He came to us once again—
Came through the shifting sandhills,
Came from the grave where he’d lain.
So we bury our past in silence
And live through the rushing years—
Forgetting its grave in laughter,
Forgetting its grave in tears.
Till some day we stand in the twilight,
By the edge of the curling wave,
And find that the sand has shifted,
And brought it back from the grave.
N.s.w.
J.A.C.
The Sea.
I ever loved the sea—all soft sea beaches—
There’s something wooing in a quiet shore ;
One fancies that the waves make little speeches
Or sing lost songs we knew in days of yore,
Or hum some missing air which straightway
reaches
A long-sealed brain-cell, where, in time before,
It clambered back again and sank to rest,
Like the lost bride in that old oaken chest.
V.
G.G.McC.
Song.
We have this life, this love only—
Kiss mo on the mouth, my own !
Dust we’ll soon be through the ages,
And who’ll reck when we are gone ?
Let us take what love can give us ;
We’ll find naught more sweet and true
In this life-time and this love-time,
In Time’s dreamland, I and you.
What is after’s so uncertain,
Love’s the one thing Life has known ;
And, while we have its dream in us,
Kiss me on the mouth, my own !
N.s.w.
R. CRAWFORD.
At Eve.
There is a gentle ghost that flits
At eve about the home ;
SI e sometimes softly comes and sits
Beside me in the gloam.
I see her sitting silent there
In her accustomed seat;
A vision that is wondrous fine
And marvellously sweet.
Her dear eyes smile into my own,
So real the vision seems;
So life-like have my fancies grown,
I cannot call them dreams.
Sometimes she whispers in my ear
Of days long since gone by,
And then the glitter of a tear
Shines, sometimes, in her eye.
I know you cannot hear her speak—
Her voice is but for me—
And though through all the house you seek,
Her form you will not see.
But still she comes, and this I feel—
Some day she’ll clasp my hand,
And then together we will steal
Into the unseen land.
S.A,
S.T.
Wert Thou But Dead!
Wert thou but dead,
That yearning of the arms that clasp the dark
In long night vigils, when the soul doth hark
For Memory’s whispers—even that agony
Were sweet, if Memory still could comfort me ;
But Memory’s sweetness is for ever fled.
Wert thou but dead !
Wert thou but dead,
Some golden rod from thy fair hair might grow;
A wild blush-rose from thy cold cheek might blow,
And all the fragrance of thy grave would steal
Athwart my heart, and make my senses reel
With past delight—till present pain were sped.
Wert thou but dead !
Wert thou bi t dead,
Ah, then, mayhap, I should no longer crave
The sensuous sweetness of thy grassy grave ;
But—all my passion purified—should feel
Divinest love my anguished spirit seal;
For then from Heaven my starving soul were fed.
Wert thou but dead !
N.S.W.
MAGGIE SULLIVAN.

I AYNE’S EXPECTORANT
fcURES THE. WORSTCOUGHS.


A Champion Athßete
PAYS THE
Penalty for Continuous Training
And Finds a Welcome Antidote.
The Case of Mr. 8. MAHALM.
(FOR THE LEICHHARDT “STANDARD.”)
To judge from the numerous successes i«
all kinds of sport which have been placed
to the credit of Australians it appears that,
as a people, we possess the necessary
stamina and skill required to excel in various
departments of athletics; but, as Mr.
Samuel Mahalm, of Augustus street, Leich-
hardt, told our reporter, it is quite possible
to overtax one’s constitution, and then
trouble ensues.
“In your palmy days as an athlete -you
did not think that so much training and
racing was likely to do you harm, I
suppose ? ” remarked a Standard reporter.
“I did not give it a thought, to tell you
the truth,” replied Mr. Mahalm. “I was
too enthusiastic in matters of sport to
trouble about the future, but now that I am
all right again I do not regret having in-
dulged in exercise so heavily.”
“It was in rowing that you principally
excelled, was it not ? ”
“Well, yes; I did meet with a fair
amount of success in that line, as I won the
champion sculls for youths under 21 three
years in succession, and was pitted against
some of the old-time champions besides. In
running, rowing, and sailing I won twenty
races out of twenty-seven starts, so I have
not much to complain about in that respect,
although, when my health broke down, I
thought the game was not worth the candle,
as the saying goes. It is an awful strain on
one’s system to keep at such heavy work
continuously, and when I got very ill I
eonsidered that my success was dearly
bought.”
“ What was the first sign you had that
your health was breaking down ? ”
“ Nervousness. The frequent strains
upon my system had a bad effect on my
nerves, for they became so unstrung that I
could not keep them steady, and the most
simple thing in the world could give me a
start and make me excited. I went on like
this for a little while, thinking that I would
soon be all right again, when I was greatly
surprised to find that I was suffering from
indigestion. That was an every-day sort of
complaint, I thought, from which some
people suffered severely through not treat-
ing it promptly, and, making up my mind
that I would not make the same mistake, I
started taking medicine for it."
“ A very sensible procedure,” ejaculated
the scribe.
“It would have been had I known the
proper medicine to take, but I had had no
previous experience, so I took whatever I'
saw advertised, and when one thing did me
no good I tried another, thinking that I
would sure to strike the right one in the
end. Sure enough I did, but it was not till
after enduring several years’intense torture
that a friend advised me to give a trial to
Clements Tonic, but by that time my health
was completely wrecked. The food I per-
suaded myself to take was harder to digest
than ever, and the after effects were much
more trying. A heavy pain settled on my
chest, which gave me great trouble in
getting my breath, especially at night time,
when I often had to sit up in bed, because
I felt as if I would smother if I lay there
any longer, and paroxysms of pain were
caused in my stomach through wind arising
from the decayed food. By these and other
means I was prevented from obtaining any-
thing like a requisite amount of sleep, the
consequence being that when 1 ought to have
been leaving my bed I was too dead tired to
. s . O- S°b so miserably weak and low-
spirited that I felt as if I could not speak to
anybody, whilst the mere thought of doing
a little work seemed preposterous, because I
had no vitality in me to do it. For another
reason I did not like exerting myself. That
was because my heart palpitated and pained
me when I put my body in motion, and I
grew so anxious on that score that I resolved
to take the greatest care of myself and not
throw any chances away. But at last I got
so disheartened that I thought I would never
be well again, a sharp pain catching me
between the shoulders and across the lower
part of my back, while sometimes I could
scarcely see out of my eyes for the terrible
headaches I had. .My tongue was in a dis-
agreeable state, being almost white, and
everything I tasted seemed bitter ; not that
I tasted much, as I had no appetite at all.
I could not have sat in a wager boat for the
life of me, as I had turns of giddiness,
which inconvenienced me even when stand-
ing on my feet. What had once been hard,
solid flesh became nearly all skin and bone,
so that there was no light task in front of
Clements Tonic when I started taking it.
Another circumstance which I should not
overlook was that I used to have very un-
pleasant dreams on any of those rare
occasions when exhausted nature claimed a
couple of hours’ sleep, so you see what a
perpetual drain there was on my system
through want of proper rest ; and nothing
could ease my pains sufficiently to allow me
to sleep, save Clements Tonic, which gave
me a new feeling of content before the first
week was out. It seemed a marvellous
thing that Clements Tonic could have such
a desirable effect upon me so quhkly, con-
sidering that I had been suffering so long ;
and further use of that medicine showed
that all others were outclassed. The lazy
sense of drowsiness which had made my life
so dreary was given place to a feeling of
exhilaration and activity as a result of using
Clements Tonic, whilst that medicine’s
action on my nervous system was more
beneficial than I had ever deemed possible to
be obtained. My nerves became toned to the
state of perfection they were in when I
could go over a three-mile course at a ding-
dong pace ; and I have such a high opinion
of the strengthening qualities of Clements
Tonic that if I were young enough to start
racing again I would take that medicine for
the purpose of supplying the vitality which
is lost whilst training. For putting my in-
ternal organs into a healthy and machine-
like condition, and driving away headaches,
giddiness and chest pains my acknowledge-
ments go to Clements Tonic, which also
claimed my gratitude for remedying the
palpitations and heart pains. With each
bottle of Clements Tonic I came nearer the
end of my afflictions, for I was eating well
and could digest all manner of food. Windy
spasms and back pains were done away
with by the same medicine, and there was
not an ache left in me. I can frankly
admit that my liver and nerve troubles
were quite cured by Clements Tonic, and
you can publish all I have said in any way
you think advisable.”
“ Thank you,” replied our representative,
“ but it is probable some of your old friends
already know this experience of yours.”
“ Some do, I know; but I can’t tell all of
them, as I know so many through living in
this suburb twenty years, while for the last
five years I have been proprietor of the
Leichhardt Swimming Baths.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Samusi Mahalm, of Augusfcus-street, Leich-
hardt, Sydney, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that’ I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting’’, of four folios, and
consecutively numbered from one to four, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements
Tonic, and also contains my full permission to pub-
lish in any way my statements—which I give
voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and
I make this solemn declaration conscientiously
believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the
provisions of the Oaths Aot, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Leichhardt, this twenty
fourth day of March, one thousand nine hundred
and two, before me,
W. B. WRAGGE, J.P.

THE PRESENT POSITION OF AN AUSTRALIAN PRIME MINISTER.
It was at the command of State Premier Irvine that Premier Barton excluded railway servants from the operation of the Arbitration Bill, and
thus practically wrecked his Ministry. The bear is shown waiting for further instructions.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 17, 1903.
The Results of Grovelling 1 to Irvine.
The Barton Government reached about its lowest
depth last week, and there is now every sign that
public contempt will shortly bring about its down-
fall. Kingston, the strong, honest democrat, has
left it. O’Connor, the strong man whose political
honesty is not at all certain, but who was none
the less a tower of strength to the combination,
has practically left it. Turner, the safest, most
honest and most capable financier in Australia, is
still there, but Turner nowadays sticks very
closely to his own department, and keeps himself
to a great extent aloof from the wobblings of the
other departments. Probably he would go too
if he could tear himself away from his be-
loved cash box ; but if the Ministry lasts
there is a Banking Bill ahead, also a great Debt
Consolidation matter to.be attended to, and there
are stormy days in view for the States in the loan
market, and there is a great row in sight when the
States at last send in their bills for properties
handed over, and there is a Federal capital to find
the money for. Turner, who loves financial
troubles, probably can’t bear to part with these.
But away from the room where the one remaining
strong man pores over his solitary cash-book and
tries to make 2s. do the work of 2s. 6d., there is
nothing but a weak and futile Ministry trying to
drift with the strongest current, and without
sense enough to find out which is the strongest
current.
* * * *
Premier Barton’s abject servility to Victorian
State Premier Irvine over the Federal Arbitration
Bill was the cause of the latest and worst disgrace.
Under the Constitution, the Australian Parliament
is entrusted with full power to make laws dealing
with industrial disputes which extend to more
than one State. That Constitution embodied the
country’s instructions to Premier Barton, and he
should have kept his eye on those instructions and
on nothing else. There is no hint in the Constitu-
tion about giving the benefits of arbitration to one
section of Australians and not to another. There
is no authority whatever for depriving any Aus-
tralian of the privilege which another Australian
enjoys. The fact that, by bringing State em-
ployees under the Act, the calculations and the
sweating projects of State Treasurers might be
upset has nothing to do with the case; whether the
man whose calculations are based on sweating
is Treasurer of a State or of a ham shop, it
is all one, so far as the Constitution says.
When the Constitution authorised the Federal
courts to arbitrate in all industrial quarrels
extending to more than one State it must be
taken as having meant just what it said. And
of all the disputes which are liable to spread to
more than one State, railway disputes are likely
to be the most disastrous. They are liable to affect
the largest number of men, and to cause the
largest amount of loss. They are calculated to
paralyse more industries than any other kind of
dispute can do. They are the only disputes which
will almost inevitably spread to more than one
State, for the railways form a network which takes
i i S.A., Victoria, N.S.W. and Queensland. If
the Arbitration clause in the Australian Constitu-
tion meant anything at all, it was certainly
intended to include railway employees; when they
are eliminated there is hardly enough Federal
arbitration left to be worth talking about.
* * * *
The Arbitration Bill, as originally presented to
the Australian Parliament, did cover State rail-
way employees; so far the Barton Government
recognised its duty. Kingston was part of the
Barton Government then, and while that strong,
honest democrat was there it was something very
different from the spineless show it has since
become. But Kingston retired, and Turner
locked himself up more and more from his un-
congenial colleagues, with his cash-box for com-
Eany. Also State Premier Irvine of Victoria
egan to issue orders. That truculent person
pointed out that he and Bent couldn’t kick
those of the Commonwealth’s subjects that were
in their employ to his and Bent’s complete satis-
faction unless the said Commonwealth subjects
were expressly excluded from the privileges other
subjects enjoyed. He explained that Bent’s
roar and denunciation would be interfered with.
He demanded that, though the Commonwealth
Parliament represented all Australia and was
supposed to do equal justice to all Australia, it
shouldn’t do equal justice if thereby he and Bent
would be interfered with. He didn’t say that
Arbitration was anything save a fair and just
idea; he merely said, dr implied, that he and
Bent couldn’t get along comfortably when fair-
ness and justice were on deck. Irvine’s arrogant
demand that a large section of the Common-
wealth’s subjects should be denied the benefits of
the Commonwealth’s law, and handed over to him
to be kicked with impunity, would have roused a
real MAN to say something very unpleasant to
Irvine, especially as Irvine was the person who
not long since grossly insulted the Common-
wealth Government by introducing a Bill which
purported to give any Victorian policeman power
to arrest the Federal Postmaster-General if he
circulated through the mails papers that Irvine
disapproved of. A real man would have made
Irvine apologise for that foul and bumptious
insult before holding any further communication
with him. But Barton, who seems to be a good
deal less than halt a man, took his orders respect-
fully from Irvine, and altered his Bill accord-
ingly.
* * * *
Premier Barton, the easy-going time-server,
the opportunist, the man who is always looking
for the line of least resistance, promptly agreed to
exempt State railway servants from the operation
of the Federal Arbitration Act because he
fancied the voice of the people went that
way. Premier Barton knows so little of the
voice of the people that if he found it tied
to the decanter in his club with a label on it, he
probably wouldn’t recognise it even then. Much
lounging in clubs, and much association with the
habitues thereof, have created in his mind an
erroneous impression that the voice of the people
is something in a top-hat and a frock-coat, and a
large waistcoat with jewellery on it. The great
mass of the people outside clubs lies outside of his
life. But the House of Representatives viewed
the matter differently. It held that Australia had
not supplied Premier Barton with a Parliament
and Ministerial colleagues in order that he might
go outside them and take orders from petty State
functionaries. Even if State Premier Irvine re-
presents Victoria in this matter, which is not at
all certain, it objected to Victoria running the
Commonwealth. It was resolved that Australian
legislation should not be shaped by the importers
that Premier Barton meets at his club. In a
House wherein 61 members and the chairman
were accounted for out of a total of 75, it was
resolved by a majority of five that the Arbitration
Act must include railway employees. And, bad
as was the Government’s defeat, it would have
been much worse had not such Oppositionists
as fanatical - individualist Conroy and Tory
M‘Millan, whose votes only go with the Barton
Ministry when it is doing something exceptionally
foolish, been for this once on its side.
• • •- •
The Barton Government has not resigned ; it
has decided to totter along with ignominy. Having
first included the railway servants in the Arbi-
tration Bill, and then excluded them at the
bidding of an arrogant State Bumble, it
hadn’t the courage to turn another somersault
and include them again. Therefore it dropped
the measure, just as Premier Reid dropped his
Local Government Bill in N.S.W. under exactly
similar circumstances. This line of conduct
has completed the breach between it and its
democratic supporters, and it can only hope to
live now on the forbearance of its enemies till it
reaches the end of the session and gets a chance
to argue the point with the country. The country
is not likely to treat it with much respect in the
matter, for the country isn’t Irvine, nor is it
Bent. Whatever view the country takes on the
main question at issue, the fact that the Barton
Government got into its present position through
its servility to a mere State Premier, and
through taking its orders from Irvine instead of
from the Australian Parliament, makes it look
small, pitiable and contemptible. In the division
all the K.’sC.M.G. (Bonython, Fysh, Barton,
Quick, Turner, Forrest, M‘Millan, Lyne and
M‘Eacharn) were on the beaten side. For a
Government which professes to administer the
most democratic constitution in the world to get
the support of nine K.’sC.M.G. at a crisis is
a disastrous symptom. No alleged democratic
Australian Government can stand the support of
so much K.C.M.G. as that.
The Australian Parliament.
The first Australian Parliament closes its life in
a blaze of excitement. In its youth and adult age
it pursued a humdrum course, arousing no great
glow of enthusiasm, shining certainly as a
bright deed in a bad world when compared with
the State Parliaments—but devoting itself under
Premier Barton’s leadership, to a strenuous
avoidance of energetic virtue. Wherever an easy
path was possible, it was taken. The spirit of
compromise was ever hovering over the grandiose
building which housed the Parliament—that hall
of stucco ornamentation and lavish gilding, a
sermon in gaudily-colored mud on the extrava-
gance, the sickly “ loyalty” and the shoddy
taste of boom-time Victoria. But in its
old age a fever came into the blood
of Parliament and in om? short week the
whole fabric of laboriously buttressed compromise
and ingeniously designed humbug was swept
aside. On Sept. 7th the Barton Government
was jogging along in its ambling gait of com-
promise, the Labor party following, a little
sullenly, but with no hint of revolt. The Reid
Opposition was toiling painfully in the far rear,
the sharp yap-yap of Representative Conroy re-
sounding always, like the cry of an angry bullooky
with a bogged team. The only suggestion of
trouble was in the spectacle of Kingston sitting
in grumbling hostility on the cross-benches.
That lie would burst out with reproaches one
day and definitely declare against Barton
was certain, but no one imagined that the
outbreak would be dangerous, would even seri-
ously disturb the gentle, ruminative gait of the
Ministry. On September 10 Parliament adjourned,
with the Government defeated in both Houses
and holding office only by the grace of its oppo-
nents; with Kingston in more or less open alli-
ance with Reid, and the Labor party in savage,
uncompromising hostility to the Cabinet; with
the dismal cause of the Foreign-trade party prac-
tically abandoned by Reid.
* * * *
All through the session the Labor party had
been kept in the shafts pulling the Government
coach along—sometimes through unpleasant mud
—for the promised reward of a Federal Industrial
Arbitration Bill. To secure this, Labor members
had made many concessions—sometimes much
against their grain. As the time came for the
political P.N. to be met, the Government of Com-
promise became uneasy. Kingston, in whose
charge the Bill was, drafted a measure which on
every side bore the mint-mark of his fearlessness
and his thoroughness. The Tories stirred uneasily.
John Forrest, their champion in the Cabinet, got
the ear of Barton ; and the Argus and the Kya-
bramistsand Irvine put their fears before him. The
first sign of trouble was the resignation of King-
ston. Then the Government brought down a
poor, emasculated, harmless Arbitration Bill, war-
ranted not to arbitrate in most cases. The Labor
party and Kingston announced their intention of
putting the Bill into shape. The Barton
Government resolved then to wreck it, imagining
that the Labor party could be coaxed back into
good humor easily enough. It was in that idea
the mistake lay. The wrecking was done ob-
viously, quite intentionally, and most clumsily.
Lyne and O’Connor were both away at the time,
and Turner was taking no interest in the matter
at all, sitting in the Chamber and blinking ab-
stractedly as the moves of the game progressed.
The clause expressly excluding State employees
from the benefits of the Arbitration Act was
reached; the Labor party.moved an amendment to
do away with that exclusion. Deakin explained
that the clause had been put in “to test the feeling
of the House,” and was careful to correct a mis-
taken argument that the Constitution gave no
power to the Commonwealth to apply industrial
arbitration to State employees. He practically
invited the House to carry the amendment. The
Labor members were full of joy, and began to
muse over the coyness of the Government which
now gave them what had been at first refused.
The amendment was carried, the Government
making no special effort to object. Then the blow
fell. The House was adjourned, and the next day
the Bill was dropped. By this trick, so clumsily
obvious, the loyalty of the Labor party to the
Government was shivered to atoms.
* * * *
Events then rushed on breathlessly. Reid
arrived in Melbourne and probably came to a
definite understanding with Kingston. It was
generally surmised that Kingston was willing to
co-operate with Reid, provided the latter dropped
Freetrade. The Labor party had a short, angry
caucus, and it also soon came to an understanding
with Reid. It was only necessary now for Reid
to give the sign and the Government must go. A
motion for adjournment moved by him seemed at
first to be the tolling of the execution bell. Then
it was made clear that the time to strike had not
come, and the Government enjoyed a brief
respite—if it could be so called.
* * * *
Reid had another thing in view. The Electoral
Bill, empowering next elections to be held on the
old divisions, was not yet through the Senate.
The Opposition leader had great hopes that it
would be killed there. He had been personally
whipping up his., party in the Senate to
reject or seriously amend the Bill. In that case
the elections for the House of Reps, would have to
be conducted with each State as a single electorate.
N.S.W. would, under such circumstances, prob-
ably return “ the Freetrade bunch” to a man, and
the Protectionist and Labor constituencies would
be disfranchised. Such a bit of gerrymander-
ing commended itself at once to the Opposition
leader. The Labor Senators were actively can-
vassed by Reid personally to trap them into join-
ing in the plot. But they were too clever for that.
“ Do I look the sort of chap that a man would try
to sell a gold brick to ? ” one Labor Senator was
asking in the lobbies. “Well, no, but why?”
“ George Reid just came to me and told me that
he had formed such a high opinion of my
capacity and my intelligence that he would
like to discuss the affairs of the nation with me at
regular intervals. He was in need of an honest,
sound, highly intellectual man on whom he could
lean in moments of doubt and difficulty, and I
just filled the bill. Then he started to speak about
the Electoral Bill. Now, if you had a gold brick
to sell, would you pick on me as a likely cus-
tomer ? ” And he walked away, mournfully com-
plaining that his friends, who had persuaded him
that he looked as though he were at least threatened
with intelligence, had deceived him. The Labor
senators did not support any Reid gerrymander,
and the Electoral Bill went safely through.
* * * *
At this stage, Reid and the Labor party were as
two men in a dark passage, moving to a common
goal—each with a knife, and each resolved not to
let the other fellow get behind him. The Senate
had given a kick to the Government in another
matter. The Eastern Extension Cable Co. agree-
ment, by which the Commonwealth surrenders its
right to give preferential treatment to its own
cable as against the E.E.C. Co. private line, came
up for final ratification. The Senate had a majority
against the agreement and when the Government
attempted to force a resolution of approval
through, the House carred a hostile motion for
adjournment. Thus on one day the Government
was defeated in both Houses simultaneously on
vital points of policy.
* * * *
The personalities of the Australian Parliament
have changed notably this session.. Kingston
is now an ill man, and probably feels that he
cannot take up the responsibilities of leader-
ship, even though the democratic demand in the
House is unanimously for him. He seems to be
willing, however, to lend aid to Reid —provided
Reid “drops the Freetrade tommyrot.” Barton
is better, physically and mentally, than he has
been for some years. His convivial tumbler now
contains milk and hot water, and he never tires of
enlarging to members on the merits of this bever-
age. Thinner than of old, haggard and pale,
Barton yet is fresher in mind and body, and
every day he seems to improve in health. In
verbal encounters with Reid he shows a wit,
a vigor, and a courage that he long lacked. It
is a pity that the better Barton of to-day
should have gone over so absolutely to the
Tories, the State-frigh cers, and other enemies of
Federalism. George Reid, too, is physically in
good form : he has been indulging in physical
culture and Turkish baths to get his bulk down.
Deakin was profoundly disappointing during
crisis week. Whilst the other men, moved with
indignation, were showing the best and strongest
sides of their characters, his weak amiability was
exposed in cruel contrast. Deakin’s speeches
suggest a man whose memory has mastered his
intellect. Mostly they are made up of potted
phrases. When the occasion is big and he has had
time to prepare, the potted goods are of the best
quality with a flavor of eloquence—but, still,
merely potted, not fresh, and with no blood in
them When Deakin speaks in debate unprepared
he dribbles out all the old hack expressions and
quotations of the weary penny-a-liner ; the stuff is
like the carrot mash of a cheap jam factory.
Dea kin’s speeches never arouse, though they occa-
sionally entertain, the House. George Turner,
the patient financier, seems to take no interest at all
in most political issues. Attending the House
regularly, he si„ts on the Ministerial bench resting
his chin on one clenched hand and blinking behind
his spectacles. He is adding things up and
calculating the effect of sinking funds at compound
interest. In the case of a change of Ministry, the
Labor party should urge his inclusion in any new
combination, and he should come in and blink
arithmetically by the side of Kingston or
Reid ; Turner deserves to be perpetual
Treasurer of Australia. J. C. Watson’s grip on
the House of Reps, is astonishing. He is evi-
dently fitted for power; with increasing autho-
rity he has shown increasing vigor, increasing
firmness, and no progressive head-swelling.
Hughes, the next man in the Labor party, is
getting back to his old State form, after a
long illness. Some FederaF members have not’
yet been able to understand the high repu-
tation he won in N.S.W. ; they soon will.
A most popular Oppositionist is Conroy, in spite
of his bitter bark. Conroy always talks as Sir
Oracle, with a warning against any rival yap ;
and, as Watson pointed out the other day, no
sensible dog would try to compete with him. But
the House credits Conroy with sincerity, and
views with half-tolerance, half-admiration, his pose
as a master of political economy.
* * * *
In the Senate, the most conspicuous figures are
the big South Australians. In Kingston, Downer,
M ‘Gregor, Symon, and Baker— four of whom
are in the Senate—S. A. sent a most notable quin-
tette to the Parliament. The mutual hatreds of
four of these give the initiated some amusement.
The Senate’s average of ability, in spite of some
weaklings, is very high, yet the House shows
sometimes a jealousy as to its position which
would argue small-mindedness. It is haunted
ever by the fear that it will be classed as a mere
Upper House. O’Connor was away the week of
the crisis, and Keating, of Tasmania, was doing
his best to maintain the O’Connor tradition in
manipulating the House ; he is the Government
whip and shows great acuteness, which is aided by
his personal popularity. One of the jokes of the
House is Senator Alexander— haw— Perceval
Mathkson, whose clothes are cut in England and
whose bleat comes from the best Oxford maker.
Mr. Matheson is fond of talking lineage, and
pointing out that he is a—haw— Perceval. The
growth of a national feeling in the Australian Par-
liament is remarkable. Scores of members entered
the Legislature anti-Federalists. Now, excepting
a very small section who have no minds to change,
every member is at heart an Australian, and
not a Victorian or N.S. Wales man or Tasmanian.
A few still disguise their real feelings to pander
to the State frights people, but the real feeling of
the Parliament is genuinely Australian. The
Legislature has “ found itself ” already.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Pall of Philp.
Just at present it is difficult tip say anything
definite about the political situation in Queens-
land, save that Philp has handed in the resigna-
tion of the longest and worst Continuous Ministry
ever known in Australia ; and that Browne,
leader of the Labor Opposition, has been “ sent
for” without much hope of forming a Ministry.
Philp didn’t fall as a matter of immediate
necessity ; his majority had certainly got down to
two, but on the face of things Philp, with a
majority of two, should be better placed than
Browne, with a minority of two. If Philp’s
party could be permanently got rid of, and the
Continuous Government brought to an end,
that would be a good thing on any terms. The
Continuous Ministry has a record of extravagance,
corruption, Toryism, and shameful incapacity
that it would be hard to equal- Yet it
is a pity that it should have gone down, not by
reason of its big loans, its deficits, its Kanaka
policy, its poll-tax, its servility to the rich," its
oppression of the poor, its faking of the vote,'its
frantic anti-Federalism, or any of its other sins;
but because it tried at long last to levy a little
much-needed taxation on the bounceable importers
of Brisbane, and because it and the Courier
haven’t been on speaking terms since it refused
that paper a large advertisement. It is a pity,
too, that the vote which brought the Queensland
Democratic party almost in sight of office should
have been a vote against the proposed stamp
duties—-so much needed for revenue purposes.
It wasn’t a vote that these duties were inade-
quate ; or that they should be increased and
supplemented by a land tax, but a straight-out
vote on the question whether the Brisbane
Fatman should be taxed or not. The Labor
members could hardly help voting against
the proposed duties because of their inadequacy,
and they couldn’t help the Cowley crowd, which
voted as it did because it regarded any taxing of
the Brisbane Fatman as an outrage, from lending
its undesirable support; yet the situation was a
complicated misfortune none the less. And it is
difficult to see that much good can be done with a
party which is partly honest democratic senti-
ment, partly soured and embittered Courier, and
partly outraged Cowley. It may be, of course,
that in the general shaking up of things something
better will eventuate than seems at present
probable. But, on present appearances, it doesn t
seem at all certain tha,t Queensland is done with
its Continuous Government.
The “Herald” and “Telegraph’s” Boycott.
With the prospect of a democratic rival in view
the S.M. Herald and Sydney Telegraph have
demanded that all their agents shall sign a docu-
ment pledging themselves to sell no Sydney
morning papers except those two—any breach of
this and a large number of other conditions to be
followed by instantaneous stoppage of supplies.
How they reconcile this truculent demand with
their beautiful gospel of freedom of trade and free-
dom of contract doesn’t appear—probably they
don’t reconcile it. If a trades union attempted
any such gross act of tyranny, the two papers
would call its conduct boycotting, conspiracy, a
shameful interference with the liberty of the
subject, and so on. The S.M. Herald, which
seems to have some sense of shame if its con-
temporary hasn’t, defended its position last Satur-
day by publishing this :
The proprietors of the S.M. Herald have requested t e
news agents selling that paper to agree to the conait
that they shall not sell any other daily morning pap
than those they now handle. An attempt is being ma


by interested parties to represent this action as boy-
cotting a certain proposed new daily paper. This is an
unfair and misleading view of the situation. The pro-
moters of the proposed new venture have issued pam-
phlets and circulars abusing the Herald, and now wish
to circulate their journal, if it should be published, by
using the machinery for distribution which the Herald
has built up in the city and suburbs, and throughout the
length and breadth of the land. It is open to them to
appoint their own agents, and distribute their papers by
other means; consequently there can be no boycott.
Our agents will recognise that we have always treated
them fairly and courteously, and as liberally as the
burdens imposed upon us by the Commonwealth and
State Governments would allow. Having done business
with them for many years on the pleasantest terms, and
to our mutual satisfaction, we claim that it would be
unfair to use against the Herald the position they have
gained by co-operation with us. On the commonest
grounds of business equity we have a right to ask new-
comers in the field of journalism to find their own means
of distribution, as we have done in the past, and not to
pose as martyrs and talk of tyranny and boycott because
we decline to allow the friendly services of our agents to
be used against us.
This attitude of paternal bunkum would be
justifiable enough if the S.M. Herald had
created the news-agencies which circulate its
papers, but it did nothing of the kind. The news-
agent has to find his own shop and his own capital,
and to show that he is a reliable person ; then he
sells the Herald and pays the proprietors for
it, to the enrichment of John Fairfax and
Sons. John Fairfax and Sons have no more
right to dictate to him what he shall sell
besides the Herald than he has to dictate to
them what enterprises they shall go into apart
from their leaden paper. It isn’t a question of a
new paper finding its own means of distribution
just as the Herald had to do. Certain means of
distribution exist. The Herald didn’t make
them. They grew up by private enterprise and
they, to a great extent, made the Herald. Now
it and the Telegraph demand that these means of
distribution, which they are allowed to use freely,
shall be used by no rival. Probably, if they dared,
they would also demand that the railways should
carry no Sydney morning paper except the
Herald and Telegraph but that newcomers in
the field of journalism should find their own
means of distribution in that direction also. The
Herald and Telegraph had as much to
do with creating the railways which carry
their papers as with founding the shops which
sell them. There would be no more boycott
in ordering the railways, if they thought they
could control the railways, not to carry a rival
paper, than in ordering the shops which they
think they can control not to sell it. The
rival paper could obviously be distributed by
balloon. What has Parliament to say to this
gross interference with the liberty of the subject ?
The New Propsting.
Premier Propsting, of Tasmania, begins to im-
prove upon acquaintance. The first Propsting
that we knew—the one who was so cordially
backed by the anti-income-tax push—looked un-
desirable. The Propsting who muddled the
smallpox outbreak so badly, looked foolish. The
Propsting with the bogus surplus of about £7OO
(manufactured by using the great bulk of the loan
funds as revenue and by not providing for £lO,OOO
or £20,000 of special expenditure) looked nefarious.
But a fourth and far more promising Propsting is
n °, w OR view—the one who has come forward with
this programme, and thereby given Tasmanian
Toryism the largest shock that it remembers:
Abolition of the Legislative Council as a separate
Chamber (the two Chambers are to sit as one).
Reduction of the Governor’s salary to £l5OO a year.
A Truck Act to clip the talons of the sawmill-store-
keeper and sweating contractor.
Liberalisation of Mining Act.
Early closing of shops.
Efficient factory inspection.
Compensation to injured workmen.
Increased Land Value Tax.
Double.land-tax on absentees.
Self-assessment of land values.
Amendment of Employers’ Liability Act.
Simplification of Supreme Court practice and adminis-
tration.
Adult Suffrage.
Arbitration Act for the settlement of labor disputes.
Compulsory' acquisition of estates for closer settlement.
One of these measures—the proposal to abolish
the Upper House as a separate body, but to let it,
with its property franchise and its Tory tra-
ditions, sit as part of the Assembly—is only a
very moderate kind of reform. It was Neil
XiEWis’s idea, and it wasn’t his best idea by a long
way. The other measures are all good, if
vigorously carried out, and the only question is
how far Propsting’s vigor extends. Certainly,
he has started well—more than well, in fact. His
land-tax proposals run from |d. in the £ on small
estates to 2d. in the £ on very large ones,
absentees to pay double in every case. That pro-
posal is the best news that Tasmania has heard
for many years. It is a direct and vigorous
attack on the great V.D.L. Co. absentee incubus,
and is such a large and comprehensive reform
that it will in itself lift Tasmania out of the rut
of very old Toryism, and give it a long start along
the road of Progress. If the Propsting scheme
is carried, it will put Tasmania far ahead in this
respect of Victoria, N.S.W., Queensland or
Westralia, and, as far as concerns the
absentee-tax, ahead of Maoriland and S.A. as
well. The only question is whether Propsting
is strong enough to fight the Propertied Chamber
•—the House of Dodery and Dobson —to a finish.
That House is now a raving lunatic, and is
hurrriedly putting its least gouty foot foremost in
the march to battle, and breathing forth threaten-
ings and cannon and ammunition. Still if Prop-
sting is willing to fight, even to the extent of
stopping supplies and letting the machine of State
go on strike till the House of Dodery caves in,
he will win. The House that is cumbered with a
big waistcoat and a burnished hat can’t “scrap”
much if it is tackled by an enemy who is utterly
regardless of Marquis of Queensberry rules.
Anyhow, The Bulletin wishes Propsting well.
If he is as good as his programme, he is a very
different Propsting from what this paper sus-
pected.

Willoughby Election.
The return of Gregory Wade at Willoughby
(N.S.W.) election is presumably a triumph for
somebody, but who that somebody may be the
Lord alone knows. The new member represents
the same set of vague moral precepts as did the
old member. Willoughby is a suburb of little
red-tiled villas, straw hats, cheap gentility, and
tennis, and the fact that the Labor candidate (at
least the daily papers so described him) scored
1079 against 317 recorded for the Labor candidate
at the general election is a surprise in the cheap
straw-hat suburb. Therefore the Labor party,
with a gain of 762 votes, need not despair. The
new member, who stands on the same platitudes
as the old member, gathered in 207 more votes
than the old member did at last election,
so there seems no reason why he should
despair either. Tha't is how the matter stands
so far as persons are concerned. As regards
principles, it seems that some principle has
been vindicated, but what principle that may
be, no human being can tell. Wade stood
for the Carruthers policy, and what the Car-
ruthers policy may be even Oijy Joseph him-
self has never been able to discover. He says
it consists of the Restoration of the Sound Con-
stitutional Principle of True Responsible Govern-
ment, which is exactly the same noise that one
hears when a tin dish is hung up and whacked
hard with an egg-whisk. It is large and poly-
syllabic, and is some relation to the rhododendron
and the hypotheneuse and the epigastrium. Fur-
ther, Wade is in favor of judicious economy,
like everybody else, but whether his idea of
economy lies in not borrowing at all or in
judiciously borrowing £4,000,006 a year, there
is nothing to show. Carruthers was once
before in a desperately economical Ministry
which had a regular annual deficit, and which
ran up the loan expenditure to nearly £5,000,000
in a single year, and which borrowed £11,500,000
in three years ; and the opinions Wade has got are
understood to be the same which Carruthers
doesn’t know whether he has got them or not.
(If this sentence seems confused, that must be set
down to the tangled circumstances of the case.)
Otherwise the party to which Wade belongs is in
favor of all the sound, moral remarks to be found
in a copy-book, and its principles are large, vague
and indefinite, like a good elephant in a dream.
No one has ever been able to extract a clear state-
ment from Joe Carruthers as to exactly what he
will do if he gets into office ; he always retreats
backwards hurriedly into his hole saying some-
thing about the Constitution. And it is under-
stood in a general way that Wade will retreat
hurriedly backwards into the same hole as Carru-
thers, and make the same remark as he dis-
appears.
The Reid Referendum Idea.
George Reid’s proposal for a referendum on the
fiscal issue has been a good deal misunderstood.
What Reid told the Australian Parliament was
that, “owing to the shocking gerrymandering of
the Ministry,” he found it impossible to make the
fiscal issue the test at the next elections. So he
proposed to drop it, and asked the Government
to refer fiscalisin to a decision by mass referendum.
The Opposition leader spoke with deep feeling.
He had just failed, after vigorous effort, in an
attempt to gerrymander the Senate into rejecting
the Electoral Bill and practically disfranchising
all the N.S.W. Protectionist and Labor con-
stituencies so far as concerned the House
of Representatives. If Reid had succeeded
in so loading the dice as to foroe on the elec-
tors a system which would have enabled the
Sydney Morning Herald - Daily Telegraph
ticket to sweep N.S. W, for the House of Repre-
sentatives, he was willing to play fiscalism.
Failing m that, he wished to drop a hopeless
issue which will keep him out of office so long as
he holds to it. That was the vital part of George
Reid’s announcement; he had killed the Dry Dog
and hauled down the Freetrade flag finally. It
was always a tattered and stained affair,
willing to float over high Protection for Free-
trade newspapers, sugar, beer, tobacco, jam,
biscuits, confectionery, and many other items. But
still, ReiO used to call it a Freetrade flag, and the
voters used to take his word —voters being given
to foolishness. Now, however, The flag with its
stains, and the cause with its lies, its hypocrisy
and its cruelty of striving to smash the small,
struggling Australian industry, whilst leaving
unattacked big, influential industries with money
to spend in defence of their rights, is definitely
done with. -George Reid pursues office without
any Freetrade impedimenta. The referendum pro-
posal takes all fiscal responsibility off his si iouh lers.
Said proposal, put forward to cover up his
retreat from an untenable position, was obviously
insincere and unmeaning. What possible hope is
there of the' Barton Government deciding to take
a referendum on the fiscal subject on the plea
that, owing to its own mancEuvring, a fair vote is
otherwise impossible ? But George Reid can do
nothing honestly. Wishing to drop Freetrade, he
hadn’t the courage to tell the lady that he was
done with her and was after a new love. He
must needs invent a high moral pretext for his
desertion. The invention of high moral pretexts
is one of Reid’s specialities.

Bargaining in Politics.
The Australian Labor party has been taught a
sharp lesson concerning the folly of bargaining
in politics. At the opening of this session that
party had three very definite duties before it:
to protest promptly and sharply against? the
Naval Tribute scheme, to register a public ex-
pression of opposition to the attacks made' by
Victorian Premier Irvine on the Australian Con-
stitution, and to repudiate the pseudo-election of
alleged Senator Reid to the Legislature. It failed
in all of them, and on many subsequent issues
made serious concessions to the Barton
Government on matters of principle, to save
time, and to save the Government from
trouble. The price for all this service was
to be an Industrial Arbitration Act. Dangling
that before them, the Barton Ministry led-the
Labor members along paths which often deviated
from the way of strict democratic duty. Now the
Labor party finds all its complaisance gone for
nought, and the Arbitration Bill deliberately and
contemptuously destroyed. Having gained from
the Labor party all that he needed for the present,
and having a lively faith in his power to continue
to befool that party, Premier Barton had no
compunction in swindling it out of the price of its
services. It would not have been natural if it had
been otherwise. A party which is willing to
make a sacrifice of one principle for the sake of
another is never credited by the other side
to the bargain with good motives. It is
considered to be guided by expediency and not
by principle, and when the time comes it is
expected to sacrifice the second principle as
willingly as the first. There can be no sounder
rule in democratic politics than this: never bar-
gain one principle against another. A thing
believed in should be held to firmly, utterly, and
without regard to consequences. The idea that
by sacrificing one right cause another right
cause can be served is inherently fallacious. Had
the Australian Labor party taken up an uncom-
promising attitude of hostility to what it believed
to be evil, from the beginning of this session, much
that stains the record of the Parliament would
have been saved; and the Arbitration Bill, if it
had not had a better fate, certainly could not have
had a worse one.

The “ Telegraph’s ” Lie.
Sydney Telegraph, in its leading article of
September 9, resurrected an old lie that it hadn’t
been using for some time. For many years the
Telegraph used to periodically draw comparisons
in the Imported Calico interest between N.S.W.
and Victoria. It always started with the lie that
N.S.W. was a Freetrade State, which it never
was ; the Telegraph itself used to enjoy 50 per
cent. Protection. Then it pointed out by inference
that unless Victoria, with only a quarter of
N.S.W.’s area, had more people, more live stock,
more agriculture, 'and more manufactures than
N.S.W., Protection was utterly damned. Then
it lied promiscuously. It was quite refreshing
and suggestive of the Telegraph’s best Ananias
days when these figures turned up in its leader of
September 9: —
1890-1. 1900.
Victoria—Employees .. 56,369 .. 64,207
N.S.W. 47,958 .. 66,135
As a matter of fact, little Victoria was nearly
4000 employees ahead of N.S.W. in 1900—the
figures are faked by comparing Victoria in 1900
with N.S.W. in 1901, when, under Kingston’s
Protective tariff, it had made an advance of 6000
employees in one year. This good old Telegraph
lie has had such a long rest that its reappearance
seems like the return of an old friend. The Tele-
graph, by the way, has the unspeakable assur-
ance to quote Coghlan as its authority, which is
something extra, even for an utterly unscrupul-
ous rag.

“ Favorable ” Wage Conditions.
Local manufacturers, in order to secure the benefits of
supplying public works with cement, have been com-
pelled to pay a minimum wage of 7s. per day of eight
hours . .It is estimated that the cost, of producing
local cement is just double that manufactured in Europe,
where, taking f.o.b. price at 4s. Bd., less Is. per cask,
only 3s. Bd. remains for freight to shipping ports and cost
of manufacture. This at least shows what favorable
conditions must prevail in Europe as compared with this
country. —Sydney D.T., Sept. 4.
The workers are occasionally given a peep into a
real Freetrade position by the Freetrade news-
papers. This is one of the occasions. The Tele-
graph contrasts the unfavorable wage conditions
in Australia (where the laborer must be paid 7s.
a day for an arduous, unwholesome, dusty task,
with the “favorable conditions ” of Europe, where
the laborer gets £1 a week or less. The price
of cement mostly represents labor. The raw
material is plentiful, and very easily obtainable ;
it is labor makes it of value. So, if German
or British labor is obtainable at less than
half the cost of Australian, naturally the
German or British cement is produced at
about half the cost of the Australian article.
If, then, Australia wishes to produce cement, it
must either come down to the “ favorable ” wage
conditions of Europe, and reduce the laborers to
3s. a day, or impose Protective duties. That is
the alternative in so many other industries that
The Bulletin can never begin to understand why
an Australian Labor party can be anything but a
high tariff party.

The Bewilderment of an Allegedly Intelli-
gent Citizen.
One of the most depressing articles The Bulletin
ever came across was contained in Jos. Syme’s
Liberator —an interesting publication issued by a
bigoted Melbourne atheist —of September 5.
Syme’s paper struggled in nearly a page of
anguished literature to find out why The Bulletin
so strongly objected to G. H. Reid. The Bulletin
had an idea that, at various times and seasons, it
had made the thing clear enough, but it seems
Fiat, as regards Jos. Syme, it has failed. It
objects to Reid : (1) because he preached Free-
trade and practised a considerable amount of Pro-
tection, and did a mean double shuffle by
pointing to the progress of his protected
industries as a proof of the blessings of
Freetrade ; (2) because of his Gash Basis swindle
whereby he made a sham surplus through not
charging himself with a half-year’s interest on a
great part of the Public Debt; (3) because he had
a series of huge deficits (in one case his shortage
was <£600,000 for a single year), and had the in-
credible effrontery to deny their existence; (4)
because when two independent committees of
inquiry refused most emphatically to certify to
the correctness of his accounts, he had the un-
speakable cheek to refer to their denunciation of
his methods as a triumphant vindication ; (5)
because he increased borrowing, used more loan
money for revenue purposes than any of his pre-
decessors, “gerrymandered” the electoral rolls
and denounced “gerrymandering” as an unutter-
able sin ; because he opposed the graduated land-
tax, old age pensions, and local government;
because he broke his promise to reform the
Upper House, after being twice returned to
power on that issue; and because he lived
on the Labor vote and has the coarse
effrontery to denounce the Barton Government
for doing likewise, and at the same time is
bargaining for a chance to live on the Labor
vote again; (6) because he exists politically to
crush Australian industry for the benefit of the
importers; (7) because he was, while Premier of
N.S.W., a mean slanderer who heaped up deficits
of his own and called them the “ Dibbs deficit ”;
(8) because he “yes-noed” on the Federation
Bill; because he changed sides three times on the
±edera! Judiciary question ; because he has been,
at one time or another, on both sides on every
question of importance—in short, because he is a.
political disease, a silly clown, and a public mis-
fortune. And Jos. Syme’s paper, after vainly
wondering why The Bulletin objects to Reid
can only assume that its objection must have
something to do with priests and R.C.-ism, and
holy water. Jos. Syme is an intelligent citizen
ot a kind, yet he can’t realise that all Reid’s
stupendous faking of the public finances, and the
corruption he brought into politics by his habit of
having huge deficits and denying their existence,
and covering them up by strange juggles, should
constitute any reason why a paper that tries to be
honest should object to him. The idea of public
honesty being a matter of any importance to any-
body seems beyond Syme’s comprehension; and
so Syme falls back on the holy-water explanation,
firmly convinced that the small squabbles of sects
overshadow all national questions. And Syme, to
some extent, represents the popular sentiment.
Australia thinks so little of honesty that Geokgk
Reid, denounced in the bitterest language as a
political swindler by a financial committee, two-
thirds of .which was nominated by himself, is still
a power in politics. In a country where honesty
counted for more than it does here, Retd wouldn’t
find a single constituency that wouldn’t think it a
disgrace to be represented by him ; but Australia
is too like Jos. Syme.
