The Upper House and Private
Enterprise.
As a legislating body, the N.S.W. Legislative
Council exerts a malign influence, which is now
fairly well understood ; but it exerts other malign
influences which, not being so well understood,
may be more insidious. Take its influence on the
Public Works Committee as an example. This
committee, which advises Parliament upon all
proposed public undertakings that are estimated
to cost over .£20,000, consists of four members of
the Assembly and three of the Council. Parlia-
ment is certainly not bound to adopt its recom-
mendations; but in 19 cases out of 20 it does.
Ordinarily, in fact, a work gets no consideration
apart from what the committee gives it. Occa-
sionally, no doubt, when a matter of unusual
magnitude is proposed, and when the opinions
with regard to it have been strikingly in conflict,
one House or the other may discuss the matter ;
but rarely does that affect the result. Even when
the Railway Department has reported against the
project (as in the case of the Cobar to Wilcannia
railway) the Parliament has nearly always
followed the committee. The plea usually
put forward is that the committee is a
semi-judicial body, that it takes evidence and
decides according to the weight of evidence, and
that that is just how every proposal ought
to be dealt with. Certainly, most of the com-
mittee’s time is occupied in hearing more or less
unreliable statements as to the number of
pumpkins which Plug Vale could export if only
it had a railway ; and then, having ascertained
the cost of the railway, all that is left is a small
problem in mathematics as to whether the
promised pumpkin trade is large enough, which
can be worked out just as well, perhaps, by a
Legislative Councillor as by anyone else. But
the committee’s functions are not always
of this character. Sometimes the question
is one entirely of opinion—a question, for
instance, between private and public enterprise;
and then the Legislative Councillors may be
depended upon to go solid for alleged private
enterprise every time. The Broken Hill water
supply proposal is a case in point. A Victorian
company, whose business is to earn dividends,
now controls the supply. It might have made the
supply practically permanent, but as that meant
curtailing dividends it did nothing, the supply
failed, and an important industry was thrown into
confusion. Moreover, the company’s existing
supply has been several times officially"pronounced
unfit for human consumption. Almost the whole
of the witnesses whom the committee examined
asked either that the company should be bought
out or that, disregarding the company, whose
supply had proved inadequate, the State should
undertake a separate service. By four votes
to three, however, the committee decided that the
business should be handed over still further to the
dividend-hunting company, and that both the
co.’s lease and its rights should be extended. In
other words, alleged Private Enterprise having
made a disastrous mess of things so far, it should
be rewarded with the right to go on making a
mess of things for more years than previously
agreed upon. And this remarkable recommenda-
tion, seeking to protect Private Enterprise even
after it had failed conspicuously, goes to Parlia-
ment, notwithstanding that, of the people’s four
representatives on the committee, only one
approves of it. The three nominees of the Council
voted solidly for it, though, and so it was adopted.
But it 13 hardly possible that the Assembly will
adopt a recommendation of which its own repre-
sentatives almost unanimously disapprove. That
three elderly gentlemen who don’t represent the
people think otherwise is hardly sufficient reason
to reverse the country’s settled policy that water
supply, at any rate, should be under public
control.
- ■+
The usual row over the Bill has started in con-
nection with Bigjohn’s goldfields water-supply
in Westralia. Finding that the concern was
being run at a loss of £135,000 a year,
and that for every £1 worth of water they
were using, the goldfields were paying about
45., while the other 16s. was coming out of
either taxes or loans, the Government decided to
collect an eighteen-penny water-rate. Even then
the Government won’t get a third of what the
scheme should yield ; still, a third will be a con-
considerable improvement on a fifth. The row is
about that eighteen-penny rate ; the people who
are busy digging up the gold and carting it out of
the country at the rate of two or three millions a
year denounce it as “a cunningly-devised
scheme for their further exploitation.” What
they reckon a fair thing is that they
should pay about 3s. 6d. a thousand gallons
for the water they use; and that when there is
plenty of water in the local dams and tanks they
shouldn’t pay anything. The fact that interest
and sinking-fund on about £3,000,000 will have to
be provided, whether they use any water or not,
doesn’t trouble them; the scheme is a “national
affair,” they fay, and the amount ought to come
out of taxes or loans, they don’t care which. The
Government says it really can’t see its way to pay
more than two-thirds of the rich goldfields’ water-
bill just now; and so the howl continues. Aus-
tralia can any time see a good many pitiable spec-
tacles; but it is doubtful if there is a more pitiable
one on hand just now than that of probably the
richest district of the continent denouncing its
Government because the latter won’t pay more
than two-thirds of its water-bill.
"Cohen at the Gate.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
David Evans, ex-Mayor of London, expresses regret at
our methods of keeping the British investors out of the
country. —Newspaper item.
Bow down your heads and supplicate,
And meek and lowly be,
Swing incense that will mitigate
The wrath of Lombardy.
Throw out the cursed Labor crew,
Who try to legislate
For lowly masses, while the Jew
Is waiting at the gate.
Don’t bar the greasy nigger out;
Don’t cuff the guileless Ohow;
And greet Mahomet with a shout
Of welcome, and a bow.
Be civil to the Eastern trash,
No matter what their hue,
And Cohen with his bag of cash
Will come and pull us through.
Don’t mention hours of work or wage,
We’ll labor night and day;
It might put Cohen in a rage
And keep his loan away.
Throw out the democratic scum,
And offer flesh and blood,
And ask of Cohen will he come
And pull us from the mud.
So kneel and swing the incense high,
And pray for him to come.
Our blood and sinews he will buy,
And make the country hum.
Let arbitration matters rip,
Let agitators wait,
For Cohen with his sweater’s whip
Is waiting at the gate.
N.S.W.
J.F.D.

♦
“ Twelfth Night ” provides the race-week
bill at Sydney Royal, and nothing of Shakspeare’s
could serve the purpose better. The holiday
audience that is out after almost riotous fun gets
it when Sir Toby and Aguecheek’s midnight
carousals bring in, first, the Clown, and then
Maria, and finally Malvolio to make as merry a
scene as ever was acted. But, though there is so
much flue fooling, it isn’t all of that sort; the
burning, rapturous passion of the Duke and the
concealed love of Viola especially give the oppor-
tunity for some of the Bard’s finest lines, and the
Clown’s part introduces some of his best-known
Songs. “ Twelfth Night ” will last a week longer,
giving place on the 26th to “As You Like It.”
** * *
Westminster Abbey Glee Singers have
caught on in Sydney like a new religion. “ Turn
Amaryllis,” sung by the full strength of the glee
at last Monday’s concert, was one of the most
gleesome things heard in the Town Hall. Leonard
Hubbard and Dudley Causton are such favorites
that the audience would like to adopt them—inex-
pensively, of course, and provided the entertainers
would entertain their hosts with song and musical
sketches all the time. There will be a matinee
to-day (Thursday) and concerts to-morrow (Friday)
and Saturday evenings, and after that a striking
of the musical tent.
** * *
The three-year-old “The Power and the
Glory,” which began another run at Sydney
Lyceum on Saturday night, makes a serious effort
here and there to be a little bit unlike other
yellowdramas. In the ordinary course, Miss
Duggan, who comes back to undergo the old
persecution, ought to be happily married to Inman
at the end of the fourth act ; but, instead, she is
the central figure of a transformation scene which
is alleged to represent the Birth of Spring—a
gorgeous combination of girl in tights and sun-
flowers. The girl in tights strike this paper’s
weary eye favorably, but the sunflowers trouble
its heart; lor sunflowers don’t bloom in spring.
Still, they may be dandelions with 10ft. stalks;
in which case the foregoing derogatory remarks
are withdrawn. Prior to the appearance of
the vegetable, whatever it is, Miss Duggan
is persecuted with much success by Lawrence
Dunbar and Miss Guildford, helped some of the
time by Willard, her lawful husband. At the
start Willard promises to go straight; but the old
habit of being a villain proves too much for him,
and he denounces his innocent wife, and thus
causes most of the trouble. Finally, he has a
dagger stuck through his heart in several places;
whereupon Dunbar remarks in great surprise that
he has killed him. Evidently Dunbar had an
idea that Willard is now so used to having daggers
pushed into his heart that they no longer affect his
health. Ada Guildford is the lady of bad charac-
ter and good clothes, whose ma keeps an open-air
restaurant—just the sort of part that suits Miss
Guildford, who does unusually well. Inman,
the sailor hero; Pattie Hughes, the small child
who loses her sight, and regains it and loses it
again to help out the plot—these and some others
help to make up what, from several points of
view, is the best performance of the Anderson
season.
** * #
At Sydney Criterion, Charles Holloway’s
co. has been playing the Russian story of “A
Life’s Revenge” to good houses; and on Saturday,
being joined by Alfred Dampier, Holloway will
revive “ Robbery Under Arms,” with Dampier,
of course, as Starlight, the picturesque boss
robber.
** * *
By the time this Bulletin is in print
“The Players” will have staged “Sowing the
Wind ” at Sydney Palace. This very capable
amateur organisation opened a week’s season on
Saturday with “Dandy Dick” and played it, on
the whole, very well. Compared with the
womenfolk, however, the men were poor. Every
woman is naturally something of an actress,
and has 'only to learn to extend the range
of her power from a circle of friends to a
filled theatre. Every man, unless he is a Yes-No
politician or some other unworthy type, is
naturally unfitted for acting, and has to learn
everything. Amateur companies should begin
training their men a year or two before their
women. Mrs. M‘Donald showed as perhaps the
best of the co., though Miss Marshall’s perfor-
mance in a much more difficult and exacting part
was praiseworthy.
** * *
The Willoughby-Geach Co. opens next
Saturday, in “Mistakes Will Happen,” at Sydney
Palace. Yes, they will—especially during the
racing carnival when most persons back the wrong
horse. Then they are no laughing matter. The
Willoughby-Geach show is represented on the best
authority to be a laughing matter, pure and
simple.
** * *
“ The Toreador ” succeeds “ Lady Molly ”
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday. It is its
first appearance in Sydney. Two men helped to
compound the libretto of “Toreador,” and two
built the music, and if too many cooks haven’t
spoilt things Sydney will see an interesting show
on the 19th.
Although there are no snakes in Wirths’
Circus the wisdom of the serpent was visible in
the inorease of the horsey exhibitions while the
A.J.C. Spring Meeting was in full blast. The
particular horse item which appealed to this
writer was Mr. Phil Wirth’s act on the charming
pony Major. August, the dummy, who used to
be carried out of the ring wrapped in the folds of
the elephant’s trunk, now rides the dromedary in
a startling manner. The effect is peculiar, and
reminds one of the progress of a cork on a raging
sea. When the humped gentleman is going at
full blast August leaps on board, and lands
with his legs round the dromedary’s hump
and both hands taking a firm grip of the tail.
It has a most stimulating effect on the dromedary.
Pagel is still fooling recklessly with his lions
When he gets a firm grip the lion has no chance,
but the danger is that some night when Page,
has not banged him down sufficiently hard thl
lion will get his grip first. Leo doesn’t seem te
mind having his jaws opened for the expensivo
seats; what irritates him is when Pagel scruffe
him round and stretches them for the edification
of the beastly shilling crowd. He objects loudly,
and when he is ruffled by being swung up
and dropped suddenly that lion is the angriest
beast ever seen in Sydney. The mastiff which
shares the cage with the lion acts the part of a
peacemaker. Whenever the lion becomes un-
usually threatening, the great dog bays and
bounds between him and Pagel. Pagel has lately
taken to taming tigers in private. He went in
among three, seized the most obstreperous one by
the muzzle and front paws, and hit the other two
with him.
*# * *
In this issue appears the portrait of W. A.
Robyns, the young Australian basso, who is about
to take his voice to Europe and whose farewell
concert happens at Sydney Town Hall on the 23rd.
W. A. Robyns is a Sydney product, and com-
menced singing as a chorister at St. John’s,
Darlinghurst. He made his debut with Marie
Narelle at Sydney Town Hall, and since then has
appeared with Mdlle Dolores, Dani, Phil
Newbury, and F. Barron Morley, the Sydney
Leidertafel and the Amateur Orchestral Society.
His last big success was in the “ Persian Garden ”
show. Also, he has sung twice by command before
Governor-General Tennyson—not that Tennyson
wears the wild, long-haired expression of a man
who knows much about music.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli has made a welcome change
in the “first part,” and a charmingly painted and
brightly dressed stage picture makes “Jolly Japs ”
a good feature of the show. Otherwise the pro-
gramme is much the same as before.
The adaptation of Tolstoy’s “Resur-
rection,” which came to Australian light at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.) last Saturday, is a heavy, un-
convincing “problem play,” or melodrama with a
purpose, marked by some strong bold character
drawing, many Zolaesqne depictions of unpleasant
truth, and a few educational touches of wholesome
pain and pathos. As an entertainment it strikes
one as an unpromising venture. The few who will
take it seriously are already well informed about
the evils that trouble Tolstoy the preacher, whilst
the large majority can’t be persuaded to think
about them at all. “ Resurrection ” is too gloomy,
too brutally ‘improper,” too sermonic for the
multitude. Moreover it is practically divided
into six acts, and three out of the five intervals
are rather long, and the first act is preceded by
an unnecessary Hymn of Resurrection, and
the finish is the weakest part of a pro-
longed sadness. The best scene is laid in
the jury room of the court where Katusha is being
tried for murder, and here Eardley Turner, one of
the imported members of the co., gives a convinc-
ing representation of a dull, sensible, kindly bore.
The contrast of jury types is good comedy, too.
A prison scene shows some lurid side-lights of
truth, as well as the striking figure of a degraded
heroine ; also a cerulean incident in the prison
dispensary is a forcible foil to the story of Poti-
phar’s wife. But, for the most of it, “Resurrec-
tion” is an improbable yarn depending upon the
efforts of two principal artists. Miss Maud
Jeffries, as Katusha, gives a realistic performance
of the yielding girl, the depraved woman, and the
sad penitent—though the penitent might be a more
interesting miracle than she appears. Julius
Knight, in the fictitious character of the conscience-
stricken resurrected Prince, is never really impres-
sive. His rulingidea on Saturday, seemingly, was to
maintain a Julius Knight pose, and nearly all the
time he might have been having his photo taken
in the attitude of a Julius who had turned his eye
sideways towards an admiring gal in the amphi-
theatre, and was trying to hear her breathe. The
watchful-listener pose grew very tiring as the
night wore on. There are some well-managed
stage pictures in “Resurrection,” and the
numerous minor considerations have been care-
fully attended to.
** * *
At Melbourne Princess’s the reminiscent
frivolity of “Are You a Mason? ” is still received
with thanks by pretty large audiences. Amos
Bloodgood’s imposture becomes too hot for him to
hold, owing to his wife’s active inquisitive interest
in Freemasonry; but she has been the same awful
woman for the previous 20 years, and during all that
time Amos held on to his fraud without burning
his fingers. So it would appear that the Bloodgood
family circle has never been visited by a real
Mason until the curtain rises, otherwise the sin
of the old man must have found him out. In
which case the three-act farcical comedy could not
have been born with its present motive. This is
a comedy wherein the things which do happen are
only moderately probable in comparison with the
difficulties which didn’t arise aforetime.
** * #
Bland Holt and co., at Melbourne Royal,
are scaling “The Golden Ladder” at the rate of
six rungs a week, and all is well with that respect-
able piece of literary carpentry. It has been
suggested by somebody that an occasional Thurs-
day matinee at the Royal would supply a want.
Melodrama before dinner probably seems lighter
than at night, and, anyway, there is a population
that rarely goes to a theatre in the evening.
Theatres seem wickeder after dark.
** * *
At Melbourne Opera House a new “turn”
is coming to replace Dan Crawley this week, and
Robert Needham, the tuneful baritone of the
Castles’ co. has already arrived. Madam Marzella
with her enthusiastic performing birds and her
silent, passive eagle remains at the “ last nights ”
stage, but the long farewell must terminate very
soon. Saturday concerts at the Exhibition, and
other concerts of more or less moment are happen-
ing as usual.
** * *
The first of present series of Hambourg
concerts happened on Saturday (September 12th),
when the forceful pianist, in conjunction with
Marshall-Hall’s orchestra, proved a draw strong
enough to fill the Town Hall in every part. Last
time the star shone hereabouts he had to thaw
local antipathy to cold-weather concerts. In
Saturday’s swelter (judging by the frenzied
applause) he seemed to fulfil the mission
of a bracing salt wind, fanning the fevered
brow of musical Melbourne. One factor in
Hambourg’s success is the popularity of his
performance with musical teachers and students,
for to them there is something tangible and
definable in their idol’s work—viz., his sensational
technique. Technique, they argue, must come at
last to the hard grafter, unlike “soul” and
“temperament.” So the human go-carts gloat'
over the methods of the motor-car, and, resolving
to do likewise, practise hand-gymnastics till
they’re threatened with “pianist’s paralysis,”
whilst Hambourg himself never practises more
than three or four hours per day. Saturday’s
concert dealt with four compositions only. The
orchestra, on itsown, gave Gluck’s “Iphigenie,”and
the “Siegfried Idyl’’(Wagner). Hambourg and the
orchestra co-operated in two concertos by Schu-
mann and Tschaikowsky, respectively. The
Tschaikowsky thing was a dramatic, interesting
work, not too solid, but softened by a wistful
andantino, serving as a plaintive interlude
between the alarums ft id excursions of Acts 1 and
3. There are queer jarring crashes in the Russian’s
mhsic, sometimes suggestive of Chinese melodic
methods, and opportunities for brilliant execution,
which Hambourg tackles by crooking his fingers
talonwise and pouncing on handfuls of notes like a
hawk swooping on a chicken. At the close of each
concerted piece, the short, smooth-faced Genius
—practical showman that he is—held up his hand
to be shaken by his brother Genius, the big,
bearded conductor. First time, Marshall Hall,
who was quietly mopping his beaded brow when
the enthusiastic paw beckoned pleadingly from
the foot of his conductor’s cliff, had the air of
misinterpreting Markie’s plea. Present Ham-
bourg season includes fixtures for Tuesday
evening (15th), Wednesday afternoon (16th),
Thursday evening (17th), and the evening of
Saturday (19th).
~ The Adelaide Royal has, for some mys-
terious reason, been afflicted with two of the most
lamentable “shockers” on record, in quick
succession. The Anderson collection of criminality
even eclipsed that of D. Barry It was called
“The Face at the Window,” but might equally
well have been styled “The Murderous Assault
and the Galvanic Battery,” or “The Crib from
Edgar Allan Poe.” It will be remembered as the
only play on record in which a character had to
act a heavy scene after he was dead ; and Diver
contrived to look very dead indeed On alternate
evenings he impersonated the aged and gentle and
altogether improbable Nelson of Buchanan’s
historical imagining. The co. is now at Broken
Hill, a town which is understood to like its drama
gory Cuyler Hastings is giving his version of
“ Sherlock Holmes ” to a public which hasalready
seen the part well played by Plimmer, the support
being practically identical in each case. The
stationary bicycle race on the Tivoli stage proved
to be only a moderate attraction, but Rudinoff,
the all-round performer, shows a big drawing
power. Victor Kelly has gone, after a long stay ;
he is a humorous comedian and seemingly
boneless athlete who deserves more prominence
in the bills than he is apt to get. Lemare, at the
Town Hall, had audiences which, though enthusi-
astic, were sadly too small. Adelaide has almost
forgotten that it once claimed to be a musical
city.
RESURRECTION.
In a rural Russian district, where the snow is shininsr
white,
Two maiden aunts give welcome to their nephew, Julius
Knight.
Their protegA Katusha, who is innocent and sweet
(And ripe for indiscretion, like the famous Marguerite),
Falls victim to the tempter, falls as easily as lead.
The careless aunts have told her to bring clothing for his
bed,
And when upon her errand she obediently goes
The ladies leave their nephew to his bachelor repose.
So, when Katusha enters with the blankets (no, the quilt),
He swears he loves, and love is gold, good solid gold—not
guilt.
The damsel cries, “ O, let me go ! ” —then nestles on his
chest;
The curtain drops (at times like these the curtain knows
. what’s best).
Two hearts with but a single thought, two sins that count
as one—
The curtain tells the public that the Trouble has begun,
And all because those maiden aunts departed from the
room
Ere yet the girl had spread the quilt which brought about
her doom.
Ten years elapse. Katusha now has got an awful name.
The maiden aunties turned her loose before the baby
came;
They turned her loose—good pious things!—upon the
wicked town.
She’s fond of drink, she likewise smokes, she’s going
down, down, down,
And for another woman’s crime she’s standing in the dock
When Julius Faustus Knight receives a great dramatic
shock
On learning that the artless wench he’d loved, and left
next day,
Is now the most be-ruined girl that ever went astray.
He doesn’t criticise the plot, he hurls no bitter taunts
At them who wrought the greater wrong. He doesn’t
: curse his aunts ;
He simply gives himself the job to win Katusha back
To where she was before his aunts had sooled her off the
track.
He sacrifices bride-elect, he spends a dreary time
In dodging round the jade who’s “ jugged ” for someone
else’s crime.
At first she doesn’t seem to care ; she wonders what’s his
game;
Her penitence moves slowly like a tortoise that is lame
But presently she drops the drink, and turns from
cigarettes,
And leads a decent prison life,much chastened by regrets,
Whilst Julius schemes to set her free, with nuptial ends
. in view.
The outlook has a rosy tint, although her past is blue.
At last the looked-for pardon comes ; Katusha gets the
show
To answer *• Yes ” and take the man who mashed her lona-
ago,
And have the lawful right to place a quilt upon his bed ;
Instead of which she answers “ No,” and shakes her solemn
head.
And when the lips that bid him go, her faithful Knight had
kiss’t
She pairs off with another bloke—a bilious Nihilist.
Thus ends the play, a play of woe, a sultry, doleful tale.
Its livest characters are “ lags,” it smells of Russian gaol.
Its moral point is not enforced ; in vain we wait to see
Black vengeance overtake the pair who caused the
miseree;
They simply die of honored age and Julius Faustus
plants
Some daisies on the little grave of those d d maiden
aunts.

The bank borrows at the at-present low rate of 3£ per
cent, and lends on agricultural lands at the also at-
present low rate of 4J per cent. Profits are increasing
handsomely. This State Bank is, of course, different
from ordinary banks in that it has no deposits at current
account to work upon—it has to make all its profits out
of its capital and its as yet small reserves, and even then
it is limited to a charge of 4 j per cent. Nevertheless,
mainly through the growth of the reserves, the profit is
now equal to 10s. Id. per cent, on the funds (capital and
reserves') as at the commencement of the year, as against
7s. lOd. for the previous year. An unsatisfactory feature,
however, is that interest in arrear, though still very
small, has increased considerably. In March, 1901, the
total was £339 ; in March, 1902, £711; in March, 1903,
£1735. No very great amount of new business has been

Premier See, in a wild moment, has promised'
that N.S.W. will have a large surplus this year. It may
be so, but what the public knows so far is that last year
closed with a very large deficit ; that the present deficit,
with two months of the new year gone, is nearly eleven
time* what the shortage was at the end of August, 1902.;
that there will probably be less money refunded by the
Commonwealth than theffe “Was last yearthat there is
much more interest to pay than there was last year; that
there is £70,000 to pay on account, of the Uivil Service
Superannuation Fund that was not payable last year;
and that-the same old See and Ow r e’Sullivan are running'
the show.

Golden Links Eclipse (W.A.) mill continues
to beat all records. During August it treated 2580 tons
of ore for a yield of 2287oz. fine gold, thus establishing a
world’s record of 10 '6 tons per stamp daily. This per-
formance is attributed to mill being run by electricity,
which provides a much more uniform and regular motive
power than steam. The value of the return is £9700,
which leaves a profit of fully £7000.
* * * *
Flagstaff, (W.A.), which lias been
reconstructed six times, is to be woundup, and its assets,
consisting of an ancient 20-head mill, originally erected
on the defunct Big Blow, are to be handed over to the
Greenmount Co., Southern Cross, for £35,000 in fully
paid shares.

In the case recently brought by the Wilfley Ore
Concentrator Syndicate against the Central Cookman
Gold Mining Company, the Victorian Chief Justice has
granted an Injunction restraining the defendants from
making or using any concentrating table constructed in
accordance with the Wilfley specification or in colorable
imitation thereof. The Sole Manufacturers in Australasia
of the Wilfley Ore Concentrators are the Austral Otis
Engineering Company Limited, South Melbourne.*
* * » *
Thousands of pounds are yearly thrown away
because insufficient care is taken to save the extreme
fines and slimes in concentrating mills. There is only
one ore dressing machine which is unquestionably entitled
to first place as the best and surest means of saving this
material. We refer to the Luhrig Vanners, which have
proved their superiority on all leading mining fields and
may be seen daily in use at the gold, silver, lead, zinc,
tin, copper and other mines where up-to-date ore dressing
is in vogue. Messrs W. and J. Lempriere, of Melbourne
and Sydn63', are the Sole Agents, and will be pleased to
give full particulars on application.*

m • 0 *
British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

* m * *
Members of the South Australian Parliamen-
tary Railway Commission, wandering through the Stores
Depot at Pt, Adelaide, bruised their shins against a large
quantity of permanent and reproductive works which had
been lying there for years. There is, among other things,
all the material for a lighthouse. Twelve or 15 years ago
it was bought in a hurry for about £1500, the Owe’Sulli-
van of the moment intending to putitup on some distant
point in the Northern Territory. But South Australian
Ministries didn’t last more than a few months at a time
in those days ; and when the new man came in he proba-
bly mistook it for a shooting gallery, and decided to let
it stay where it was. South Australia borrowed £1500 to
buy it, and Bull Cohen has been paid about £700 in
interest on the purchase since then. Other trifles on
hand include two large guns which have never been fired,
and which have been taking up a quantity of valuable
space for over 14 years.

The Commercial of Tasmania does not show separately
what amount it has derived from the recovery of
debts previously written off; and, as Tasmania has
been having an Unusually good time lately, it is
probable that a considerable amount of the half-year’s
profit has come from this source. If that is so, then the
dividend will have to be watched ; once before this bank
ran up its dividend (to 26 per cent, that time), and after-
wards wished it hadn’t. The bank’s liabilities to the
public amount to £1,571,054, against £1,572,881 12 months
ago, a decrease of £1827 ; but its advances, etc., total
£1,259,697, against £1,135,171, an increase of £124,526.
Cash and liquid securities are £550,434, equal to 35 per
cent, of the liabilities to the public, which is a very solid
reserve.

The Tasmanian Produce and Cool Storage
Co. (Launceston, Tas.) completes its second year :—
Added to
June, Profits. Dividends. Reserves. Deficit-
£1068
Reserves
1902 .. £1,151 — £1,161 £83
1903 ..811 — ' 811 894
In the first year the amount spent in preliminary ex-
penses, £568, was written off. In the foregoing, however,
neither preliminary expenses nor goodwill are treated as
an asset. The goodwill item, originally £500, is being
written off, and has already been reduced to £400. The
co. is to be commended for having thus promptly got to
work on these two paper assets. An unsatisfactory
feature, on the other hand, is the very rapid increase of
overdraft. In June, 1802, it was £71; in June, 1903,
£4331. The Produce and Cool Storage is much too risky
and irregular a business to make it wise to borrow largely
in order to carry it on. Nevertheless, the prospects of a
Tasmanian produce co. ought to be rather greener than
usual, Tasmania being a small island where it frequently
rams and the cow flourishes luxuriantly.

I.O.F. alleged insurance concern—another
balance-sheet comes to hand
Added to Total
Dec., Income Claims Expenses Funds Funds
1901.. £524,621 £325,335 £62,496 £136,790 £1,020,711
1902.. 581,727 344,358 61,347 176,022 1,196,733
The figures for 1901 were dealt with in B., Sept. 10, ’03,
Those for 1902 leave matters just about where they were.
The interest earnings are still rather poor, being equal to
£4 2s. 9d. per cent, on the mean of the funds, as against
£3 19s. 8d. in the previous year. (The A.M.P.’s rate for
1902 was £4 9s. 2d. per cent.) The expense rate
was £10 10s lid. as against £10 18s. 3d., and this is irre-
spective of an unknown extra, amount required as “court
dues.” In 1901 the forfeited and surrendered policies
numbered 12,929, assuring £2,438,608; last year they
reached the vast total of 15,476, assuring £2,618,705.
Twelve policies lapsed in this way for every one on which
the society paid out any benefits ; this represents a
larger loss of the savings, mostly of the poor, than ever.
Notwithstanding these wholesale surrenders (the Red
Indian calls them “ surrenders ” now, though no sur-
render value is attached to the policies), there are still
policies in existence purporting to insure £46,612,918.
The funds are thus equal to a minute fraction over 6d. in
the £ of the amount insured. (The A.M.P., on the same
basis, can pay 7s. 3d.) And, finally, the assets stated to
be in Victoria (where the head office for Australia is) total
£7121, while the amount of insurance in force there
was £124,200, the latter representing an increase of
£15,500 for the year.

The share capital is £177,164 ; and on this amount the net
profit was equal to £2 4s. lid. per cent., as against £2
13s. 4d. in the previous year. If, however, instead of
paying 2£ per cent, on the £193,727 of debentures, the
society paid the current bank rate of 4 per cent., it would
have left enough to pay only 12s. Id. percent, on the
share capital. On this showing the assets require a great
deal of writing down ; and the directors are just now
cngaged upon this work. The liquidation of the society’s
liabilities on debentures continues to make good progress
notwithstanding the small earnings; amount outstanding
at the end of each of the past 10 years has been as
follows:—
1894 .. .. £417,874 1899 .. .. £262,707
1895 .. .. 368,985 1900 .. .. 241,720
1896 .. .. 322,195 1901 .. .. 222,489
1897 .. .. 304,367 1902 .. .. 208,287
1898 .. .. 283,420 1903 .. .. 193,737
In 10 years this debt has been reduced by £241,799, to
accomplish which the nominal reserves have been reduced
by £20,350 (the reserves stood at £40,356 at the end of
1S93). If the remaining assets are as good as those which
have been disposed of, the position is, therefore, by no
means hopeless; the recovery of the full amount of the
capital is simply a matter of patience. The alternative
is to write down assets and capital, put the affairs on a
sound basis, and go into regular business again promptly.
On the mean net profits of the past three years the society
could have paid 4 per cent, per annum on the average
of outstanding debentures, and 2J per cent, on £47,960 of
shareholders’ capital; or it could have paid 3 per cent, on
debentures-and the same on £109,000 of capital.

* * * *
Premier Irvine of Victoria is a very angry
Kyabrammer because London Financial News casts
doubts on the genuineness of his great sham surplus. Yet
when a surplus is made by taking £100,000 out of railway
funds set apart in previous years for certain fixed pur-
poses, postponing payment of £110,000 of liabilities, and
reckoning certain receipts of July and August in with the
revenue of the year which ended the previous June, it
seems to be up to somebody to call the thing a sham.
* * * *
After taking a referendum of ratepayers, Fre-
mantle Municipal Council has decided to establish its
own tram and electric lighting system. Council had
a proposition from one Rogers before it, which
Roarers indignantly withdrew when a referendum was
decided on. Then the Perth press railed about “ capital
being withdrawn from the country,” as though Rogers
were staggering along the wharf with his carpet bag full
of wealth. But Rogers had never put any capital into
the Fremantle scheme, and the Council will invest just
as much capital as Rogers proposed to spend. And, for
all the present evidence there is to the contrary, the
capital in this case might have been like that of Snow,
who doesn’t make much progress at finding capital for
the Adelaide trams.

The figures in the last line are to some extent an esti-
mate, as the exact amount required to pay the extra £
per cent, interest to depositors and debenture holders on
November 1st is not yet known. This extra £ per cent, is
charged in the foregoing to trading expenses ; if only the
old 2J- per cent, were charged, the trading profit for the
past year would be £1643, which is, with one exception,
the greatest since the boom burst, notwithstanding that
the year’s accounts are charged with £2000, depreciation
of Victorian Government debentures. Sales of property
have, however, resulted in a heavy loss ; this is due to
clearing off a great quantity of vacant handkerchief
allotments at an alarming sacrifice. More progress than
usual has been made with the reduction of liabilities on
debenture and mortgage, mainly by paying off a first
instalment of 2s. 6d. in the £ of the principal all round.

bilities (hitherto made by repurchase) has been :—

The result of the large operations of the year has, how-

of debt, carrying in the past 2£ per cent, and recently 3
per cent., has been exchanged for debt carrying about 4£
or 5 per cent., which doesn’t seem any too good busi-
ness. But, taking it altogether, the society’s progress is
really better than the figures indicate ; because, although
in order to reduce the liabilities on debenture and mort-
gage by £134,932 in the past six years it has been
necessary to absorb £60,907 of the society’s capital, yet
the remaining assets are better than they were. For-
merly they consisted only of properties at book values ;
now they also include £35,356 worth of Government
stock.

As to the amounts mentioned above as having faded
out of the accounts, it is pretty certain that, for the
greater part, they represent money paid either as
bonus or dividends in addition to the amounts
stated as having been paid. There is, however,
involved in the earlier disappearance, an amOutit of
£150, which was alleged to have been put to “special
reserve for branch accounts”; but nothing more
happened—it just faded out of sight. Most likely there
wasn’t anything wrong ; all the same, if there were it
would be practically impossible to check it. The last
two balance-sheets are, however, satisfactorily drawn.
The business recently has been unprofitable. In the last
six months the gross earnings were £6277, as against
£10,206 in the six months ending June, 1901, while the
general expenses, wages, salaries and fees were £7317, as
against £7447. In other words, earnings fell £1040 short
of paying general expenses, without taking into account
interest and rent (£849), bad debts (£678), depreciation,
&c, Of course the season has been unusually severe.;
nevertheless it is obvious now that the dividend and
bonus distributions a little while back were most excessive.
* * * *
Broken Hyll British is looking better than
usual ; more milling ore is exposed than at any time
hitherto. The mill is now working two shifts daily, and
the out-turn is about 2000 tons a week. When the mine
resumed work it was thought that lead would not go
below £1110s. again, but the August average was slightly
under £11 3s. Silver is, however, firmer ; and there is a
good market for Broken Hill concentrates. The British
can therefore escape a loss, though it is believed that
nothing below £11 for lead is profit.
* * * *
Myall’s United (N.S.W.) has cut the lode at
the 700ft level, and advices from London state that the
cable messages from Australia indicate very encouraging
prospects; the lode is described as being payable Co.
has recently purchased the large air-compressor which
the defunct Hongkong Co erected at Mount Macdonald.
This addition to the plant at Myall’s in a measure confirms
the supposition that there are more hopeful prospects in
view Myall’s people are again nibbling at the Peak Hill
mine in the same district. A year or so ago they were
negotiating for the property, and a mere trifle blocked
the completion of the transaction. Peak Hill ought to
be a good property if properly handled.
* * * *
The Great Cobar (N. S. W.) deal is officially de-
clared off and the mine is now the property of the Reid-
Longworth syndicate. The reason alleged for the failure.of
the Joseph option syndicate is the strong antipathy which
Londoners show towards any Australian mining proposi-
tion, owing to the “ trend of socialistic legislation ” This
is arrant nonsense. The plain, unvarnished reason
is that J.B could get no satisfactory explanation
of why he should be asked to give the option syndicate
practically £700,000 for the transference of its rights
in a £1,000,000 purchase. It is said that £5000 is all that
was deposited here and in expert’s fees and other ex’s.
The option people may have spent £20,000 more. Had
they been content with a £1,500,000 flotation which would
have given them the handsome profit of over £200,000 the
probability is that the co. would have been formed. It
is understood that the owners will now proceed to equip
the mine on a scale somewhat commensurate with its
productive power
• * * *
Occidental, Cobar (N.S.W.), last half-year,
crushed 23,031 tons of ore for 2746oz. gold, or 2dwt. 9gr.
per ton ; 17,200 tons of tailings were cvanided for £6587
worth of gold, or 7s. 7fd. per ton. The extraction by
cyanide was about 81 per cent, and the cost 3s. 8£d. per
ton. Dividends amounting to £3150 were paid. Directors
contemplate the erection of 10 additional stampers.
When they absorb the Western, as they hope to do some
day, they will want 60 stampers more Petty jealousies
block the desirable amalgamation.
* * * •
The Vulcan, at Irvinebank, N.Q., probably the
deepest lode-tin mine working this side of the Equator,
made a profit in the past six months (to August 14) of
£7953, of which £6930 went in dividends and dividend
duty. The co. has no mill, and the custom works which
it patronises lately put up the prices, affecting profits to
some extent. The co. is now going down to 900ft., with
good prospects. It. has a cash reserve fund of £3030, in
addition to a cash balance of £1872. The small mining
co. which steadily establishes a reserve fund is a pleasant
surprise.
* * * 0
Though most Charters Towers’ stocks show a
severe shrinkage, the field’s returns continue satisfactory.
August dividends were £33,850, making the year’s total
so far £278,102, against £241,802 for the first eight
months of 1902. Queen Cross Reefs, one of the few
stocks that have moved up during the past three months,
supplies, however, £20,000 (2s. per share) of the total of
£33,850, and only four others, each for small amounts,
are on the list. Calls, on the other hand, are mounting.'
Queen Cross’s total for the eight months is £110,000.
Brilliant and St. George, which paid £3600 as usual this
month, will probably shortly increase the amount. This
mine has paid £23,400 in divs. during the past six
months (equal to 65 per cent, on its capital), and is in a
good financial position ; after debiting the August div.,
a cash balance of £23,000 is left—an increase of £9902 in
the six months. Mining costs have been reduced to
£1 Is. 6d. per ton. Since 1898 it has won £1,244,768 of
gold and paid £634,800 to shareholders.
* * * *
The visit of an Adelaide director to Smith’s
Creek tin-mine (N.Q.) has led to a wholesale clearing-out
of officials. The mill has worked long enough to get into
fair running condition, but has disposed of most of the
available ore, and will shortly be closed down, pending
the chance of prospecting operations revealing further
supplies. This problematical event will mean a liberal
supply of cash ; and who is to provide it ? It seems
likely that Smith’s Creek will soon be a sad memory.
Someone has blundered seriously in connection with the
show. Money has not been spared to give it every chance,
and the Adelaide men who have been hit should insist
on a thorough investigation.
* * * *
The Victorian Government, being “of opinion
that the existing law relating to mining does not hold out
to bona-fide investors sufficient security for the investment
of large amounts of capital,” announces that it will ask
legal sanction for “a system whereby, instead of labor
covenants, mining leases will contain covenants for the
expenditure of fixed sums year by year during the cur-
rency of the lease.” This Bill, if it is ever introduced,
will have to be watched very carefully. There is little in
the cry that present mining leases are insecure ; there is
far more, in fact, in the complaint that it is too difficult
to secure the forfeiture of leases which are being
merely shepherded by speculators, waiting for their
neighbours to prove or disprove something. But, apart
from this, the proposed new system will not give increased
security worth twopence. What, however, it will do will
be to help make the miner’s occupation more uncertain
and irregular; and its irregularity is already its curse.
Two or three co.’s, which will have to spend among
them, say, £2500 a year, may decide to spend the amount
in a few months. They will announce that they can find
places for 30 or 40 men ; then, the £2500 spent, they will
be masters of the situation perhaps for 18 months. And
miners know what that means—the co.’s will tell them
that, though they don’t really want them, and
needn’t etnploy them, they can go on tributing.
if they like, or they can have a contract at a
starvation rate, or they can “ fill in time” at 5s. a day.
And then other leaseholders who are spending the money
that they have to spend under the terms of the lease will
point out that other co’8. are getting work done for 5s.,
and they will want their work—even the work that they
are bound to do—done for 5s. ; and, with the 5s.-a-day
men available, they will get it done. As it is, the miner,
and the people who live on the miner, are heavily handi-
capped through the industry being so capricious, and
employment so irregular ; and Irvine’s plan will make the
handicap heavier. What is really needed is something to
make the employment more regular ; and if the capitalist
also needs more encouraging, then give him a longer lease,
for short leases generally mean inadequate development,
and the stripping of a property of only its richer patches.
* * * *
There have been great ructions at Bendigo of
late. The New Chum and Victoria fiasco, which is one of
the most unfortunate events in the history of the field,
has led to the retirement of the mine manager and the
rejection of two directors who sought re-election at last
meeting. No fewer than seven managers have been
called upon to resign within the past month. It is a
healthy sign to find shareholders taking this intense
interest in the practical management of the mines. For
many years Bendigo has enjoyed a clean reputation ; its
mines have been worked on honest lines, and, anomalous
as it may seem, this is the reason why its stocks have
commanded so little attention in other
the spec.-sellers and other market manipulators
have not been afforded a chance to exercise
their baneful influence. Latterly there have been
occurrences which indicated that the purely market
men were making a desperate effort to get control of
several stocks, but, if the genuine mining men persevete
in their present determination to keep things on the
straight track, these nefarious gentry will soon be
crushed.
• * * •
Bendigo divs. for the expired portion of present
year total £202,187, as against £126,918 for same period
last year. The year’s distributions ought to reach close
on £300,000, which will be a record for nearly 20 years.
* * *
Associated (W.A.)isnow in a strong financial
position. The old opera-bouffe plant has long reposed on
the scrap heap, yet its initial cost and the cost of renova-
tions have up to now figured as assets in the accounts.
By appropriating £161,713 premium on shares (43,400
were sold in Sept., 1899, at £12 apiece), the plant and
buildings have been written down to current value as
estimated by manager Gleisberg. Another £212,702, also
from premium on shares, has been written off develop-
ment, wiping out cost of all developments prior to
April, 1902 During last financial year the co. cleared
£81,959, from which £23,223 was appropriated for
depreciation and one-sixth development for year ended
March 31. The whole of the old Ropp furnaces have now
been dismantled, and the full dozen Merton roasters will
soon be in full swing, and materially improve extraction,
raising it from 70 to 90 per cent., or fully 5dwt. Pros-
pects have very much improved of late, developments in
all parts of the mine being satisfactory. It is now
generally conceded that the rich Oroya chute will sooner
or later be located in the Associated, as its trend south-
wards appears to be 70 yards east of the Kalgurli’s south-
eastern boundary peg.

Some two years ago a charitably-disposed
person named Waite bequeathed some hundreds
of pounds to the Parramatta Benevolent Asvlum.
There is no such institution, but it was agreed
that the testator meant the Benevolent Society.
Before the court could part with the money a Bill
had to be passed by Parliament authorising it
to thus interpret the will. Up to date Parliament
has done nothing, spending its valuable time on
Billjim’s dirt money. Meanwhile, the Society is
so hard up, many of its subscribers ceasing to sub-
scribe when they heard of the windfall, that it
has suspended operations.

• tij- lunatic has turned up
in Melbourne, and created a scare at the
commonwealth Government House and in the
Eastern Extension telegraph office. This rem-
nant of the Jingo lunacy was a Britisher
traveHmg for the benefit of the world at large,
and he landed in Melbourne with a burning desire
to save the Empire from the Russians. Conse-
quently he made straight for the office of the cable
company with a sheaf of cables to the Admiralty
and to the admirals on all the naval stations
ordering them to rise up and save the Empire at
once. He also had a loaded pistol. The messages
were signed “ Tennyson,” and when the officer in
charge said it would be necessary to get the
brovernor-General’s seal, he went off to get the
seal with such promptitude that before the cable
othcials could telephone to Government House he
had turned up there and was shaking his gun at
the world in general. After a struggle he was
disarmed and taken before Panton, P.M., and
worthy, “Do you believe in
the Bible ? was promptly sent off to an asylum,
it is curious how promptly the Law sends a man
to an asylum when he even mentions the Bible
to it.

The first Anglican Church services in
Victoria were held under a gum tree, a little south
of the site of the present St, Paul’s Cathedral, in
March or April, 1836. There is only one survivor
° f , tl ’?' se , who attended—a lady living near Kew.
Ihe Wesleyans about tied with the Anglicans;
the Kev. Orton, of the former sect, came over
trom iasmama when Batman brought his family
across. The Methodies pitched their tent under
a she-oak on Batman’s hill, the south side of the
city. At that time, or a bit later, there were
sheep-yards where St. James’s now stands.

* * * *
The Governors of the Adelaide Public
Library, Art Gallery, and Museum threaten to
close one of these institutions (probably the last-
named) as a protest against the way in which the
Jenkins Government has starved them lately.
Eleven years ago, when the buildings to be kept
up were much smaller, the grant in aid was
£2200 a year more than it is now. It is claimed
that the S.A. subsidy for these purposes is,
“relative to population, the smallest in any part
of Empire aspiring to cater for the mental
entertainment of the poorer classes,” while all
institutions, both buildings and contents, are
really such as a much larger city might be proud
of.
London Law Notes prints the all-iinporr
tant announcement that the meaning of the word]
“humph” was recently decided in the Irish
Appeal Court. Justice Madden and Justice
Boyd held that the expression was one of dissent,
while the Chief Justice and Mr. Burton thought
that it merely indicated a dissatisfied state of
mind. Finally, the Court of Appeal decided,
once for all, that the word “ humph ” is an expres-
sion of doubt or dissatisfaction. A deal should
depend upon the way in which the word was
spoken. It may belike the Chinese word “chow,”
which has 23 different meanings, all depending on
the tone in which it is uttered. It is not the word
that does the harm —it’s the nasty way you say it.
* * * *
Melbourne Board of Health has decided to
recommend the Government to permanently close
Melbourne General Cemetery against further
interments after the end of June next. This
action has been taken for sanitary reasons; the
place has, since its Opening, received no fewer
than a quarter of a million candidates for the
regulation 6x2 feet of permanent resting place,
and things are beginning to get crowded and
uncomfortable. But it is so like a Board to begin
reforming ten months hence.
• • * *
Deposed by a petitioning husband in a
recent Sydney divorce cgse that he was married
in a down-town matrimonial agency, both the
contracting parties being under age. The bride’s
uncle posed as her father for the purpose of giving
parental consent, but to the disgust of the wedding
party, the'minister pointed out that the Marriage
of Minors Act also required that the parents of
the bridegroom should consent. Luckily someone
remembered the cabman outside, and he Came in
and “consented.” Apparently almost anything
serves for a consenting parent.

That man at Coogee (N.S.W.) who tinrew
himself over a cliff last week deserves. t©> be
remembered as the Polite Suicide. Being arrested
on a charge of passing valueless cheques* be said
courteously to the detective : “Please excuse me
a moment,’ and then took a short out over the
precipice. Victoria, a few years ago, produced a
similar individual. He was a commercial man
living in a house, the back garden of which
abutted on the Yarra. A ’tec at the front door
told him he was wanted for embezzlement, where-
upon he also asked most courteously.to be excused
while he got his hat. It wasn’t till a fortnight
later that the muddy river gave up its Polite
Suicide.

“ A. Pofcliecary ” remarks :
Chemist’s par. (3/9/’O3) touching the eccentricities of
the poison bottle recalls a creepy tale told to me by the
leading chemist in a large and flourishing and tiiuch-
medicine - consuming suburb shortly before he died
through not taking his own medicine regularly. “ I felt
particularly dull and heavy, the other morning,” sa d he,
“ and no wonder, for I had been rung up four times the
night before, and I had to leave my breakfast to make up
a prescription for a Judge. Part of it was a pretty stiff
dose of strychnine. The case was evidently an urgent
one, for the servant who called with the prescription'had
ridden his horse so fast that it was flecked with foam.
After I had finished my breakfast I started to clean up
the counter—and found a Joz. weight stuck underneat.l
the scale I had us d in making up the Judge’s prescrip-
tion. Of course there was no cab on the stand opposite,
so I started to do a sprint for the Judge's house in the
fastest time that I could travel. My luck was ‘in’ that
day, however, for just as I was bursting out of the shop
door I met the Judge’s messenger limping back fora
fresh dose. He had been thrown through his horse slip-
ping at a sharp turn, and the medicine bottle had been
smashed. The weight was fastened to the scales by some
ointment stuff, but how it got there is one of the many
mysteries that make the poor pill-roller’s life a warfare—
and a Bulgarian one at that.”
A PRAYER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bring me out of the Ruck, 0 God !
From where the toilers slave and plod,
To 1 ? earth SWeats with h «"?ering crowds ;
To coot green fields and sun-kissed clouds.
With Th y help 7 fools call it luck !
y strong right hand, bring me out of the Ruck !
Out of the Ruck, O Lord, I pray,
Bring me, from where the brawlers bray
Drunk with their greedy golden lust,
Away from the clatter and hurry and dust,
the bo °£ ed B °uls lie in the muck,
i Thy long right arm, take me out of the Ruck !
HIPHEN.
A SOLILOQUY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sitting here in my old armchair,
The long years pass before my eyes
In slow review. And this I swear:
Of all the might-have-beens that rise,
The worst one is—supposing there
Had been no serpent in Paradise?
H.E.
A QUESTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You have heard of the story of Adam and Eve—
Of Eve and Adam, I beg your pardon—
That frivolous couple who lived by God’s leave
In a biblical age in a beautiful garden.
Now, I’m wondering why (the cause who can trace f)
The Maker towards them His heart should so harden
As to banish this pair without a grace
In that biblical age from that beautiful garden.
It seems to be certain, excepting this pair,
No other like soul on this earth did tarry ;
There never was priest or parson there
To preach or to pray, to baptise or to marry.
Now, this is the question that I have to leave
To gossiping woman, either maiden or madam :
Was Adam really married to Eve,
Or (save us !) was Eve but the mistress of Adam ?
H.S.W.
JOHN PHILIP COOR.
ALAS!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is the hue I love—
The color of the primrose in the woods,
Of burnished buttercups ’mid Austral grass
Of dancing daffodils at dewy dawn—
The color that is neither grave nor gay,
Nor sweet nor sad nor sorry—
But smiling, cool insouciance. I could weep
To see this calm tint, desecrate and vile,
Upon the tail of that unhallowed cur —
The Yellow Pup

That fat oyster, the estate of Jones, of
Footscray (Vic.), deceased, being opened, is now
in process of being assimilated by the devil’s
brigade. Jones foolishly died intestate, leaving
±.‘30,-000 odd, and already the recognised claimants
to the estate include a sister, six cousins, and two
nephews. There are also some 30 or more
claimants not yet sorted out, so to speak, includ-
ing 17 Welsh, five British, three from Maoriland,
and several sundries. These have a “possible,”
but the English commission now taking evidence
has also been attacked by over 300 people who
want to share the estate because they are named
Jones, and the vast and complicated suit threatens
to extend out to the day after Judgment. The
only man who can afford to die intestate is one
with a name like Micah Spottlejar. When a
Jones or a Smith does it, all the estate goes in
investigating the claims of Joneses and Smiths.
* * * *
During the August sittings of the Divorce
court at Melbourne altogether 48 petitions were
heard, and three struck out. Thirty-nine divorces
were granted. In 29 cases the wife was the
petitioner. The grounds on which these succeeded
were plain desertion (23), desertion with other
offences (2), misconduct (3), drunkenness and
crueity (1). Husbands gained decrees on the
grounds of misconduct (6) and desertion (4).

Reported from Tasmania :
A circular has been issued by the Government to all
civil servants, asking if they, or their wives, have any
private sources of income.
That seems to indicate a new development of
Jvyabratnism-—to treat the civil servant’s salary
like a Victorian old age pension and reduce it if
he has other means of livelihood.

“ Kati’s ” philosophy :
Love, courtship, and marriage are most interesting to
watch from a safe distance. I love to see the play of
forked lightning some way off, but I don’t wish to handle
it. I might hurt it. These light goods are soon damaged.
Or it might hurt me. Anyhow, I’m satisfied to look at
marriage or forked lightning. Any person who wants to
stroke the mane of either of them can have my share of
its mane.
AT THE RAILWAY STATION.
HE (who is not so sober): “I say, Mister; is
all them clocks right? ”
HE (who is quite sober): “Of course they are.”
HE (not so sober): “ Well, I must be bad.”
It is solemnly alleged that when the
lymph ran out in the early stages of the Launces-
ton panic, sundry doctors used glycerine instead
and took the money just the same. The people
who were glycerined felt safe anyhow, and their
hair, which had been standing up, lay down again.

“ F.C.” : Re the nice new bed from the
time-payment warehouse, and the great rag-and-
bottle industry. A lady friend of mine lately
turned out, with a view to airing, a flock bed
she had just bought from a big city shop. In it
she discovered a considerable portion of a woman’s
dress, including the pocket which had a shilling
inside.

Australia’s expected visitor and prophet,
John Alexander Dowie, of the wings, has patented
a machine, with clockwork attach-
ment, for the purpose of registering
the intercessions on behalf of those
of his faithful followers who are
sick. When the ailing one has dis-
gorged to a specified amount, he
or she receives from the machine
aforesaid a printed slip, reading
“Prayed for, Ist April, 12 p.m.,”
as a guarantee of good faith. To
relieve the dire stress of those
Zionites who experience a sudden
pain far from headquarters, Dowie
now—at a fixed rate per prayer, of
course—prays over the telephone,
so that the intercession is heard by
the patient. And for those who
Eine to have the pious prophet’s
eauteous sentiments .always on
tap, the prophet aforesaid has
raised his gentle voice at the
mouth of the phonograph, and
undertakes, for a more or less
number of dollars, to forward
cylinders bearing the x'esult to his rabbit-faced
adherents in any part of the globe. Dowie, as a
money-making humbug, is sublime ; not even the
hooked-nosed Booth conceived so brilliant an idea
as turning out prayers to the faithful, at a fixed
Charge, by machinery !

“ D.” : The colored quack is a magnificent
pile of conceit and ignorance. One anchored at
my gate a few evenings back, and addressed a
small crowd on his professional ability. Brown,
who lives three doors up and suffers from rheuma-
tism, hobbled up slowly. “I could cure him in
twenty-four hours,” said the colored gent. Mrs.
Jones walked past. He diagnosed her coinplaint
in half a second ; it was a fatal disease and ho.
assured the audience he could cure her in three
minutes. Then my better half came up the street,
and the “ nig.” recognised her when she was still
yards away as a sufferer from “sluggish liver.”
Here I climbed over the back fence into
M'Dougall’s yard, and borrowed M'Dougall’s
dog. The diseases it suffers from are large teeth,
bad temper, and great celerity. It cured the
nigger in two minutes of his worst disease—the
disease of being outside our gate.
A Melbourne man on Tait’s railways :
Either Vic. Railway Commissioner Tait is carrying out
the set instructions of the Cabinet to bring the service
to such utter ruin that the people will presently be glad
to lease or sell the lines, or else he is grafting here the
railway policy of Canada—the country of long distances,
land grants and small cities. In either case, the disastrous
results are showing themselves. The system, as a whole,
js admittedly hard to deal with, but Tait is rapidly bring-
ing even the suburban lines—hitherto very profitable—to
a non-paying state. Such a master of “ traffic ”as Tait
was alleged to be must know the folly of frequent time-
table changes, yet these have been the order of the
month, since he took charge. A glance at the present
tables makes one wonder whether the designer thereof
was a passenger-traffic expert or a road-roller fireman.
Such populous suburbs as Box Hill, Surrey Hills and
Canterbury get three trains to town between 6.37 and 7.7
p.m., then there is a vast gap till 8.57. Oakleigh has to
cool its heels on the platform for an hour and a half at a
time ; populous places like Mordialloc and Mentone see
vast blanks representing two hours’ time on their tables ;
even Bent’s Brighton only gets one train an hour in the
evenings. Brighton is naturally furious, which would be
a pleasing episode if that were all; but, meantime, the
railway revenue is falling worse than ever. When the
infrequent trains do run, they mostly consist of nine or
ten huge carriages more than half empty, save for the
busy hour morning and evening. What the suburban
traffic needs is a frequent speedy service of short trains,
with engines kept up to the mark and permanent way in
thorough order; but, either Tait hasn’t sense enough to
see this, or it isn’t intended he should.
* * * *
Some years ago a Tasmanian Government
decided, and persuaded Parliament, that it would
be a noble idea to construct a road to the signal
station at Mount Nelson, where the finest view,
within easy access of Hobart, is to be obtained.
The road was started, and for a long period con-
struction went on. This national work has grown
very gradually, and is not yet completed; but,
thanks to the method of its growth, it is so far
not of the slightest use to anybody. Fact is, that
the Works Department commenced the road
away at the back of everywhere, and worked on
to the top. From this back of everywhere to the
top there is now a very passable road, indeed, but
as there’s no connecting link between the back of
everywhere and Hobart town, the road would not
be a bit less valuable if it were a boy with bunyips '
on him.
Mark Lome writes :
Re “ Z.’s” remarks (Bulletin, 9,7/’OS). If, as he says,
it is the best class of man who is addicted to the practice
of fathering 1 big families, the glorious future of mankind
is assured. But is such the case ? Observation shows
that the broad-shouldered, straight-backed man, with the
bright eye and clear complexion—the man who meets
adversity and conquers every time—is usually content
with a family of three or four sturdy editions of himself.
He will see that they get, physically and mentally, the
best possible development, and in the majority of cases
they do him credit. If you want to get the best
progenitive results (taking numbers as a standard), the
white-faced, weak-kneed draper’s assistant, or the con-
sumptive clerk who braces himself up with a course of
whisky revivers, are sometimes a howling success. Again,
observe cases where statisticians have sought to prove
that the criminal instinct is hereditary. These show
that certain notorious examples have, in a couple of
generations, been responsible for a prolific army of black-
guards. Now, where are the direct descendants of the
great men of history—poets, statesmen, generals, etc.?
No ; I think the champion of the big family is wrong.
The above isn’t conclusive. Very often the
people who figure out how one notorious criminal
had 146 criminal descendants are not statisticians,
but only Methodist preachers. In any case, they
generally include in the list the descendants of
the criminal’s brothers and sisters, and on that
basis many pious men who died unmarried left
thousands of descendants. As to the where-
abouts of the descendants of great men, it is
difficult, in many cases, to trace them, because
they have dropped into obscurity; still, when a
title was hung on to them to keep their names to
the front in books of reference, they seem fairly
traceable. The descendant of Columbus is a live-
meat duke at present. The descendants of Welling-
ton, Marlborough, Alfred the Great, Robert Bruce,
Frederick the Great, Rudolph of Hapsburg,
Henry of Navarre, Henry Parkes, Abraham (of
the Old Testament) and Othman, can still be
found ; and whatever doubts there may be about
Parkes and Abraham, the others were great men.
The descendant of Milos Obrenovitch would prob-
ably be alive to-day if he wasn’t dead. Nobody
can prove that the descendants of hundreds of
other great men, including Nimrod and Hannibal,
are extinct. “Mark Lome ” should bear up.

“J.F.D.”: Note the trampled, crushed
expression of the poor terrace houses. The doors
when open give one the impression that the
structure is gasping for breath. I could never live
in a terrace house ; the pained look on the face of
the middle house protesting against the continual
squeezing, and the heavy breathing of its chimney,
are too much for my nerves. The middle house
seems to live in constant dread of being crushed
back out of the line, and put somewhere in the
back vard. And they are getting worse, they are
growing taller and thirnor. The backbone of
staircase is the only thing that keeps.the walls
apart. Some day the middle house will die of
suffocation in dead earnest and the struggle to
extract the corpse for burial will be like trying to
get a smashed snake from between two iron safes.

That “grand old flag” boom in the
Yicto ian State schools seems to have-been buried
with Sargood. When the movement was started
it was understood that the office of hoister or
keeper of the rag was to be conferred on the
good prig of the school as a special recognition of
his goodness. But there can’t be any good prig
left, for the G.O.F. is seldom hoisted now. In
one school, certainly, it is flying day and night, but
that banner was nailed to the post to save string,
and is a permanency.
Beside the awful flood of “loyal” slush
from the literary caterpillars about the “glories,”
“magnificence,” “awe-inspiring ceremonies,”
"evidences of our empire’s might,” &c., &c., at the
late lamentable Durbar, the plain, true words of
Mrs. Craigie (“John Oliver Hobbes”) gleam like
pearls set in gold. Speaking of the procession of
native princes, shesays: “What histories, what
traditions, what crimes they represented. This
portion of the procession seemed a horrible medley
of the infernal and grotesque, the ancient barbaric
and the modern vulgar, the superb and the
squalid ; a manifestation of power without glory,
of rank without grace, of riches without beauty,
of pomp without philosophy, and pride without
strength.” Then she tells of one prince who was
half insensible from opium, having a “loyal,
beneficent smirk painted on his inexpressive
countenance, so that it could neither fade nor
falter.” This part of the vain, squalid circus was
realistically suggestive. A painted smile on
India’s face, to hide the hate below.

“D.H.R.”: In reply to “David” (8.,
6/8/’O3) on a lip question. None of the three
reasons assigned by ‘ ‘ David ” are correct. The
long upper lip in female school teachers is the
result of trying to look severe and impressive by
compressing the lips, thereby elongating the top
lip, which is not so firm as the bottom one, and
elongates easily. The long upper lip predominates
also in male teachers and parsons, and is notice-
able more amongst the elder personages than
amongst new recruits. Know, however, one
exception to this rule. A young lady teacher of
my acquaintance, who is a splendid teacher, has a
short upper lip, and kisses admirably. She
generally kisses me, so I know.

The Australian who is streaking to cold
Canada will strike trouble when he starts to look
for a divorce. Except as to New Brunswick and
Nova Scotia, that business is done there exclu-
sively by the Dominion Senate ; and the Senate
doesn’t do it until after about three tons of for-
malities have been complied with, and even then
misconduct is the only ground that is recognised.
Only about seven applications are dealt with per
session ; the rest of the people who might make
applications mostly put up with it, or go over the
border to Dakota or some other place for a while.

A striking example of the humanity of the
undesirable immigrant is related by a misguided
philanthropist who was raising funds to save the
babies in China. She waited on several “Lillie
Buk-stleet” merchants, but got nothing. “ Why
won’t you subscribe ? ” she asked a prominent tea
importer at last, in desperation. “No want save
Chinee baby. Too many Chinaman alleady. Let
some die ! ” was the laconic reply.
OUR JOURNEY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Time is a vista of endless slopes,
Astrewn with human smiles and tears ;
Life is a river where all our hopes
Are stepping-stones across the years.
Youth is a pathway, sunlit and fair,
Rosy-red in a land of dream ;
Age is a mountain hoary and liare,
Ghostly grey where the grave-lights gleam 1
TAS.
J. E. PHILP.

“ K.” : Patience is a very desirable virtue;
especially in Russia. Just heard of two Russian
prisoners who escaped after spending four years
arranging their plan of campaign. The man who
devised the plan could only communicate it to
the other at the rate of one word a day, except on
rare occasions when he spoke two. The pair saw
each other just once in the 24 hours when they
Eassed in the exercise yard, and Ivan Ivanovitch
roke the news by instalments to Paul Pauloff
as they crossed each other. Penal sentences are
fairly long in Russia, else the game wouldn’t be
Worth the candle.

lobe-drops. A diamond for the stud part, then a

springs. Somebody told me she had seen at

A star cellist’s engagement is off, joyous news
or the girl’s mother; she would not welcome
the star cellist as a son-in-law. Stars are not
good as matrimonial mates. This cellist’s valet
used to bnlliantine his hair and manicure
his priceless paws. Such pets should never be
taken out of the water-lily lake of elegant
bachelordom to be thrown into the suds of
domestic life.
Peterkin, the basso, and his wife, Marjory
airne, who came out with Lloyd, have been
lmng at Manly all these days waiting the
Crossley arrival. He has been practising against
great rivals, those league-long rollers thundering
on the beach, so his voice is no reedier than of
yore.
Another artist who has adopted Manly is Gran,
the Lord Jeffreys of the “ Sweet Nell ” co. It has
always worried me to know why anyone who has
to come to town every day lives at Manly. Fancy
taking two long sea-voyages between your morning
coffee and your vesper mutton. Yet people do,
and grow fat on it. They play bowls and press
sea-weed and pass the basket at divine service.
Was at Manly the other evening, and our enter-
tainer, whocarriesa humorous watch, turned us out
just m time to miss a boat, so we wandered about
u hour and I got a glimmering of the reason
why Manly is inhabited. No whirr of trams, not
a rumble of a cart, no crowing barn-yard bird,
no barking dog ; no sound but the splendid bass
or the ocean. People live there because they can
s | ee P soundly. Not a human, save we two, was
abroad, yet the night was quite young. The police
force of the district had foregathered under a
ijghted window, and was listening to Peterkin
and his Marjorie singing. On the long beach
(where the undertow takes you out and drowns
you) all the sea-poetry from Homer to Tennyson
that you have read and forgotten breaks the dusty
seal of memory, springs to the lips and recites
itself.

One who knew the late Phil May well in
London says he was that unique personality, a
famous man without an enemy. He was - recog-
nised wherever he appeared in the streets, and
the cabbies liked him so well that they would
entreat him to drive free. When he was intro-
duced to a person his formula was, “Glad
to ™ oefc you. Tell me the story of your life 1”
At the Savage Club banquet in honor of Nansen,
the explorer, the Duke of Cambridge, on seeing
Mill enter the hall, said “Here is the first
gentleman in England ! ” It was understood that
May s regard for the feelings of others was so keen
that he never rasped their susceptibilities, and the
compliment was paid on that score.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The surprise in connection with the late
Duncan Gillies was the discovery that he was a
married man of six years standing. How the
supposed bachelor must have chuckled when he
read paragraphs crediting him with matri-
monial intentions towards this tuneful maid or the
other moneyed widow ! Maybe, though, there
was nothing to chuckle about. A bridegroom of
over threescore, whose bride is parted from him
.by the breadth of the Indian Ocean, might be
conscious of little to laugh at.

There is a daily lesson for the dyspeptic
moralist in the patrons at the IVtuto. or Beauty
scope establishments. There the amorous fat man,
the raw youth, and the giggling girl take their
rapturous pennyworth of peep. I looked on at
the fat man taking a view of Susannah, or some-
thing of the kind, the other day. He approached
the machine with a smile of gusto decorating his
countenance. Then he seized the handle, glued
his face in the aperture, and excitedly started to
see Susannah. His bulging cheeks grew gradually
red —he was laughing. In his delight, he turned
the handle rapidly just as he was near the climax
of his gloat—there was a click, and his eyes peered
into emptiness. He drew himself up, disap-
pointed, peered around, saw no one looking,
dropped in another copper, and turned the handle
slowly this time, to make Susannah last longer.
All the fat men gloat in a like way.
“ A fair globe-trotter, who recently encountered
Joseph Chamberlain, brings a novel light to bear
on the screw-maker. He has a manner of meeting
one that is so furtive, shrewd and sly that a feeling
of uneasiness arises. He is polite to punctilious-,
ness, but a newcomer experiences that uneasiness
through all the veneer and the soft assurance of
the exotic in the button-hole. His speeches strike
the ear strangely. He hesitates, dashes at a sen-
tence, delivers it with point, and hangs fire at the
end. He always hesitates, and always gives
weight to the prepared words. He is a strange
man, automatic, cold and calculating.

“ Miss Beatty Kingston, the large, timid blonde
of ‘ Are You a Mason ? ’ co., confesses that, at one
time, she had thoughts of following her father’s
footsteps along the Inky Way, but, having inter-
viewed the terrible head of a large paper concern
in London, she changed her mind instanter.
She gravitated then to the footlights, and moved
on to legitimate drama, playing once with Henry
Irving, when that potentate exhorted the novice
to play her little love passage ‘ with more sympathy
and affection.’ Therein spoke the actor-manager.
Miss Beatty Kingston still needs to be exhorted
to invest her art with emotion. She is merely
carried through her present part by her physique
and her gorgeous frills.

ventures in this State. In town and country,

long, long ago. Ada may expect a bigger hustle
among the pump-handlers than Melba endured*
The Melba magnificence, and the Melba bluntness,
and the Melba mastery of mind kept off many of
the timorous. But panegyrists (mostly feminine)
praise the Crossley for amiability, and innocently
prepare a wild burst of welcomers from Colling-
wood to Croajingalong. By the way, Melba had
a father. Crossley has, as a pathetic background,

“ Mr. J. Hamilton Reid, just dead at the age of
52, had climbed from the position of junior book-
ing-clerk in the Victorian railways (starting work
at 14) to the important post of Auditc r-General of
the State. He climbed* too, without losing, a
shred of his natural geniality, frankness, and
loyalty to the old chums of his youth. The
troubles of the railway men, consequent upon
their unsuccessful strike, helped to drive the first
nail into his coffin. His position debarred him
from expressing what he felt, but he looked 10
years older at the end of the battle than he did
when he first knew that his old mates had declared
war. In his day he had been a good cricketer,
was a great reader, and particularly susceptible to
the charms of poetry.

Cyntliias adoring him in this capital, but on Satur-
