“George Coppin grows into old age happily,
even gracefully, for one of such bulk. Although
these are days when he can’t amble any distance
without a stout stick and a daughter to spread
out the rug for a rest, the fine old man is cheerful,
good-tempered, and looks when seated something
like the monumental Dr. Johnson with his massive
head sinking into his bent frame. He had a
- prominent place at the Saturday Hambourg
concert, where the Bland Holts and their party
showered'all kinds of attention on the veteran.
He showed his appreciation of the Hambourg
strains by thumping the floor with his stick and
waving his colossal handkerchief. He’s a virile
old soul for 90.
“The Huttons were in the Tennyson box first
night of ‘ Resurrection.’ Lady Tennyson had a
tightly-compressed wreath of blue flowers that
fitted on her small crown knob in a lid fashion.
Her dress was happier. White in tone, it had
some artistic finishings in a black applique.
“‘Resurrection’ is sobful. .It is years since
women sniffed, blew their noSes, and' shed the
brine of sympathy as they did at the first presen-
tation of the Tolstoy tragedy-oil Saturday night.
Maud Jeffries’ under-lip has a curious little twist
at moments of strong anguish that moves the
heart of an audience to an instant pity. Her
pathos is genuine; her groan real. It’s a far cry
from the quaint and arch Katusha of the demure
twin-plaits, the scarlet knots, and the tremulous
tongue in the country house scene, to the
Katusha of drunken habits and evil records,
in the prison yard. She plays the derelict in the
proper key, and screeches her drink curses with
horrible realism, while the tearful audience mops
up the brine of deep feeling. In the Siberian
days of her reformation Katusha is still pursued
harrowingly by the moist eye of the females, and
her final stroke of pathos before the crucifix of her
dead past and her present purification brings this
aquarelle in art to a most watery conclusion.
J.C. W. should stretch a few clothes lines over the
stalls for drying of cambrics between the acts.
No woman’s * wipe ’ can stand the strain of ‘ Re-
surrection.’
“In these nights of strenuous Tolstoy drama at
Her Majesty’s, it is high time that.the bill of the
playhouse (which has Flossie Fewclothes executing
a dizzy back-kick on the cover) should be more in
keeping with the sort of show that is going. A
Scarlet Woman of Sorrow on an Ethiopian back-
ground, or a wrathful Fate overshadowing a
People of Sin, or the Tragic Muse herself in
dishabille, would be more meet and proper
designs for the programme cover. The present
giddy picture should not stay. It’s like a can-can
interlude at a sensational funeral.

“ The motor grows more common. At s o’clock
the other day, in Collius-st. (Melb.), I counted
seven of the fiendish things pulsing along with a
great whizz and aloud smell. Along Toorak-rOad
one sometimes catches a glimpse of a motoring
she, but not often. Grace Palotta, long ago,
dusted and begritted herself and her chiffons on
the highway in a motor, but no other smart
feminine reckoned that the game was worth the
spoil. A woman can go much faster in a spotless
crepe-de-chine than in a motor-car.
“The» crack she-golfer has tried all ways of
getting away from feminine traits and feminine
vanities. She would be all that may become a man.
The best example up to date is Miss Bateman, of
Warrnambool. She cut an extraordinary figure
in the eyes of the 1 random onlookers at the
Tournament last week. Her square, manly
figure was covered with a scutty skirt, clearing
the ankles, a sac coat, a dicky, a tie, and a straw
decker. Her hair was polished up and brushed
away under the crown, so as to appear short-cut
from the distance. It was not short-cut, though ;
she has not yet burned her boats. The champion
adds to the gentlemanly cut of her outfit by a
strong sun-tanned face, with a pair of keen mas-
culine eyes. Her golf play is strong, clear, crisp.
She scores particularly at ‘ putting ’ shots, which
are as neat as flawless billiard strokes.

“The suicide of chemist Davenport closes a
sombre history pf two closely linked partners.
Messrs. Simpson and Davenport entered the
laboratory together 40 years ago, or perhaps a
little less. They were apprenticed and plodded
along together. Both were with the venerable
Ford more than 20 years ago. Then the two
joined forces and started an establishment of
their own ip Swanston-street. The firm flourished.
They went along to Collins-street, and thrived
further. Then Simpson developed mental
trouble. He fought against it, but finally drifted
into the care of the proper authorities.
Next, the only daughter of Mr. Davenport died
under pathetic circumstances. Brain trouble
threatened in his case. Last act, death by
poison.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.


HALE AND HEARTY.
“Three and a half years ago I was fit for nothing,” says Mrs. Louisa Player, Georgina Terrace, 49
Wolfe-street, Newcastle. “My blood was too weak and watery to keep out the nagging pains of Neuralgia
and Rheumatism. The sharp, darting stabs in the back of my head nearly made me mad. Often I
clutched my head in a spasm of agony to get a moment’s ease. I couldn’t turn round without twisting my
whole body. The tingling aches in my arm had me so that I didn’t know which way to hold it. I couldn t
as much as hold a needle or thread. The shortest walk tired me out. Often I fell flop in the street with
dizziness. I thought my heart would break loose after the least exertion. X was too weak to eat, and I
looked like a corpse. I hardly had a drop of blood in my body. I was too tired and weary to keep my
eyes open or take any interest in life. Every medicine you could mention I swallowed, but none of them

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Rabbi Davis, patriarch of Sydney Syna-
gogue, retires with a pension of £3OO a year.
Phil May worked until three weeks before
his death, when his sight became paralysed ; but
he had been “dying on his feet ” for many months.
The Australian is already getting into
S’African politics. Ex - insurance drummer
McLartyi who tried for the Towers (Q.) in 1893,
has been elected to the Natal Assembly.
A newsagent —a J.P. of several years’
standing—was fined half-a-crown for yelling out
too loudly newspapers for sale in Sydney lately.
The unpaid Bench was always a nuisance.
Said of a wealthy man in one of the
Australasian States that he couldn’t get a fashion-
able club to accept him as a member until he
bought up its mortgages. Then he sim ply cantered
in.
“Nosey” Bob, N.S.W. executioner, is
said to be jealous of a particular member of Par-
liament. The member has small chance of his
seat at next elections, and from inquiries he has
been making, there is a suspicion that he is after
Mr. Howard’s billet.
Philp has been 17 years in Queensland
Parliament. The Labor party drove him out of
office when Treasurer in the Dickson Cabinet, and
has now again kicked him out when Premier.
Philp’s first office was Mines Minister in
MTlwraith’s bunch in 1893.
There is still living at Adelaide, and still
in collar, Dean Marryat, who was chaplain at
Cockatoo Island and Darlinghurst Gaols, Sydney,
over 50 years ago. In fact, he settled in South
Australia, in 1853. He has just finished 34 years
at the fashionable North Adelaide Anglican
tabernacle.
John Howard Angas, reputedly the richest
man in S.A., and probably one of the wealthiest
dwellers in the Commonwealth, will complete his
80th year on October 5 next. He has recently
been seriously ill, but is now stronger. He has
but one son and one daughter between whom to
divide his money.
The people who write most violently to
the papers about the way Labor legislation is cal-
culated to ruin the country seem to be mostly the
representatives of institutions which managed to
ruin themselves most effectually, and a good part
of the country along with them, before there was
any Labor legislation.
THE LATE DUNCAN GILLIES,
Ex-Premier of Victoria.
The Argus stoops very low in its vilifi-
cation of Tom Mann. The other day in a leading
article it referred to him as the “enthusiastic
socialist, enthusiastic —fora consideration.” The
Argus proprietary sells its high ideals and its
noble, but bloated, aspirations for a great deal
more per annum than Mann receives.
The late British Premier, Salisbury, was a
mighty jobber. He put his son, who was a pup,
and his son-in-law, who was a nonentity, and his
two nephews, one a dismal failure and the other a
tired man of some slight ability who plays golf,
all into his Ministry. And Britain is so used to
that kind of jobbing that it stood it all without a
murmur.
Globe-trotter Earl of Lonsdale, now visit-
ing Australia, says he spent two years in a log-
hut in the Arctic regions, sailed the German Em-
peror’s yacht to victory in English waters, was
one of the founders of Klondike, and has oecu-
Eied a high position in the War Office. And now
e can say that he has taken his life in both hands
and travelled by Bent’s train.
The post of Victorian Auditor-General
appears to be a strangely unlucky one. The posi-
tion was created in 1902, and the first auditor-
general was James Bagge. He held the post for
a few months only and died suddenly. In Feb-
ruary last the vacancy was filled by the appoint-
ment of J. H. Reid, ex-railway accountant. He
has died as suddenly as his predecessor.
Arthur Morgan, Queensland State Speaker
and possible Premier, started life in Warwick
(Q.) 48 years ago. He inherited politics from
Morgan senior, who represented Warwick for a
number of years, and was Chairman of Committees
during the Maealister, Thorne and Douglas'
Governments. Morgan owns the Warwick Argus,
and in his spare moments dashes off a leader or
two. He also owns the constituency, is a man of
weight (about 18 stone) and well set up, but a
pigmy compared to his little brother Sydney, who
is 6ft. 3in. and weighs, in condition, 26 stone.
DR. RAMSAY SMITH,
S.A.’s suspended Coroner, &c., &c.
E. T. Hooley, whose death was reported
by cable a week or two ago, and whom some
papers confounded with the boomster who gave
St. Paul’s its Hooley communion plate, was in
the early days a Western Vic. cow-thumper.
Afterwards (somewhere in the middle sixties) he
went to Westralia, tried to stock the wild nor’-
west, and came a cropper through poison-weed
and other things. Afterwards he tried the
Murchison, but without much success, and eventu
ally became Dalgety’s manager at Perth, Turf
Club president, a Legislative councillor, a brewery
director, and a pillar of “holy church.” Then,
having also written a bush yarn, he went to
Switzerland and died.

Agent-General Copeland was to distribute
his portrait in “court uniform” among
the newspapers. Copeland is remem-
bered in N.S ,W. as aheated, vigorous

shirt-sleeves and with much blanky.
The blanky was forgiven for the
shirt-sleeves. But now—Copeland
is not the sort of ham to look
well in frills ; and he ought to have
the sense to know it.
Archer, M.L.A.’s “damn”
in N.S.W. Assembly the other day,
was a mere bubble up of his old
Palmer gold-digging life. Every-
one said “damn” on the Palmer
when Archer was there. He said

when not lucky, And the fights
with the Chinese were nearly all
conducted with that weapon. On
entering a publichouse the cheer-
ful salutation was “ Damn you ! ” ;
on leaving the chorus was “Oh,
damn you ! ” It was the language
of love and hate, a slight inflection
of the voice making all the dif-
ference. The venerable Archer
is the reverse of profane. He beat
the pious M'Millan in the contest
for Burwood chiefly on the ground
that he was a pious man who didn't
swear so often as M‘Millan, and
M‘Millan is said to swear only
once in 10 vears

Malcolm M‘Eacharn, to the secretary of
the Vic. Employers’ Federation : “Until your
executive council has the decency to withdraw
the charges recklessly made against me, and which
I have proved to be glaringly false, what it
may * deem ’ of me or my opinions -is to me a
matter of supreme indifference.” One always
remarks that the ordinary public person, engaged
in a quarrel with another public person, uses
adjectives of agitation to express his “indiffer-
ence,” which is invariably “supreme.” Supreme
in all their bearings, the M‘Eacharns of this world
are supremest in their alleged indifference to
whatever makes them shriek.
The S.A. Treasurer came in for another
windfall on Tuesday, September 8, when Mr. 0.
C. Searfe, a Norwood butcher, died. He had just
inherited a large amount from his brother George
Searfe—whose estate paid the Government £75,000
in succession duty—and it is estimated that he
was worth quite £150,000. He was a quiet,
retired person, who contented himself with selling
chops and steaks, and he sold them to some pur-
pose.
Richard Williams, Bendigo’s best-known
mining manager, has gone out, aged 72. For 37
years he had managed Johnson’s Reef, a mine
which, though it hasn’t done any great things
lately, has several times led the field ; before that
he was general manager of George Lansell’s
mines. The miners liked “ Dick ”; he got plenty
of work out of them, but his mines were always
well ventilated. Unlike most old Cornish miners,
he thought a deal of “book lamin’,” and bar-
racked hard for the local School of Mines.
Dr. Rentoul, the plucky Melbourne cleric,
is generally on the right side. He battled alone for
a White Australia at the recent Convention of
Churches. Bevan twaddled for the Black, to try
the influence of Christianity and civilisation on
him. Bleeds Fitchett went strongly in the Black
Brother’s favor. The meeting passed a resolution
to petition the Australian Parliament to allow
Kanakas who wished to remain to stay in the
Commonwealth. Then it discussed why people
do not go to church.
Sam Winter, of Melbourne Herald, and
brother Joe, of the R.C. Advocate, have returned
from a short trip to Japan. The inevitable smoke
night was given by the employees of the Herald
and Weekly Times, Kunnel Reay in the chair.
Same old sycophancy, same old stiff, sit-still-and-
booze-while-bosses-scratch-backs sort of concern.
Carrington, the pious editor of the Weekly
Times, fairly surpassed himself. “A time of—er
—stwenuous stwife, of stwess and stwain—er —, ”
was one of his happiest efforts. Sam Winter
spoke for about an hour on his travels in Japan,
but, as his remarks were addressed principally to
Massina (the chairman of directors), who was
sitting near him, they were, for the most part,
inaudible. Some idea of the church-like effect of
this “welcome home” is to be gained from the
fact that not one man had the heart to get drunk.
During the big maritime strike, W. M.
Hughes (now M.H.R.) was sent by
the Labor Executive to speak at a
public meeting of sympathisers
called in a backblocks township.
He was given his return-ticket,
and had to “find” himself. Ar-
riving just before the meeting,
with an empty stomach and
three 2d. stamps, he rushed to
the post-office, which declined
to do a deal, but succeeded
in swopping his stamps with a
fruiterer for a bag of peaches.
He hurried to the meeting,
hoping he would have time to
eat a few while the chairman
was introducing him, but the
chairman only said, “Mr.
Hughes will now tell us all
about it.” He spoke for an
hour, and then sat down and
reached for his peaches to find
that the chairman had “ wolfed ”
them ! It was about that time
that he established a retail refresh-
ment business out Glebe Island
way (where the Sydney abattoirs
are). Hearing someone in the shop,
one morning, Hughes answered the
call, to find a wild-eyed prime bul-
lock wedged between the counter and
the side wall. The only weapons to
hand were the lemonade bottles on the shelf.
With a concentrated fire of these, the sanctity of
the back parlor was preserved. The bullock
couldn’t walk out backwards, but, in desperation,
it sat on the counter, pivotted round, and escaped
gladly to the slaughter-house.
Zebina Lane, the first manager of the
Great Boulder, now grand panjandrum of the
Collie Coalfields—the man who has probably made
as much coin out of Westralian goldfields as any
Australian—has got into the Legislative Council.
Mr. Lane, who is Victorian born (his father is still
mine-managing at St. Arnaud) is presumably
going into politics for the good of his health.
Barney Byrnes, Sydney’s coal merchant,
whose business has been floated into a limited co.,
now occupies the late Parkes’s house at Faulcon
bridge, on the Blue Mountains. Parkes financially
wrecked himself making that prodigious attempt
at founding a family estate. Marble mantel-
pieces, etchings and engravings tell old tales. But
the grounds are in disorder. Busts and pedestals
are broken, and the classic figures the old man set
up are mostly gone. The Government tidied up the
grounds a bit when the Duke of York visited the
place as that personage, when a princelet, planted
a tree thereabouts, but wind and rain and curiosity-
hunters have had their way. Henry Parkes and
Clarinda, his first wife, are buried at one corner of
the scene of desolation.
Four Australian beer-makers were created
by the Pope Knights of St- Brewery—or
St. Gregory. They were the late Toohey, of
Sydney, and Coghlan, of Ballarat; . also Nick
Fitzgerald and W. H. Archer, of Melbourne
(Archer is largely interested in Gastlemaine ale).
The late Dan Brophy, of Ballarat, who sold beer
if he didn’t make it, was likewise a Papal Knight.
Yet St. Gregory isn’t recorded as having been a
hard-drinking person.
Concerning one of the “ old families ” :
The Kings, descendants of Philip Gidley, who became
third Governor of N.S.W., after filling in a few years as
Commandant of Norfolk Island, cover a large part of
Australia. The original King had a considerable family,
and the best known of them, Philip Parker, who entered
the navy and became rear-admiral, but finally settled in
N.S.W., had seven sons. Most of them took either to the
water or the land. Several of them took to both. That
was the experience of Philip Gidley, of the third genera-
tion, for nearly a quarter of a century past dozing in
N.S W. Legislative Council. As a youngster he joined
the navy, and served in the famous Beagle ; later on he
became N.S.W. representative of the gorgeous
absentee Peel River Land Co 'Another brother,
just dead, Charles Macarthur, '■ had much the
same sort of experience, only, instead of sticking
to the moderately fertile coast lands, he went
further back and struck the usual disaster; and in his
old age, largely in consideration of the two names he
bore, the Government provided him with a succession of
none too comfortable billets. The Macarthur of his name
he derived from the Camden Park Macarthurs —the people
who introduced the merino to Australia—with whom the
Kings made two or three family alliances. Another
modern branch of the family is represented on the Sydney
police-court bench by Lethbridge King ; the Lethbridges
were one of the early Yandemonian families, into which
the Rear-Admiral married. Another common prenomen
of the modern King is Parker —some of this branch have
drifted out as far as Westralia. The original Parker came
to Australia as Gipps’ private secretary in 1838, and later
on became Premier. But all the Kings haven’t been
admirals and such like. One of them was lately keeping
a small bushman’s pub. in far-west N.S.W.
MUSIC AT THE MINES.
GEORDIE (as the silvery tenor responds to another
enthusiastic encore): “Shut thy face, thou
brayin’ cuddy, and let Ongcoor come out!”
Chas. McDonald, M.H.R., and watch-
maker, who moved the amendment on the Arbi-
tration Bill on which the Barton Government
was defeated, is a fighter all through. Even in
Queensland labor circles in the old days when
‘ ‘ things were made willing ” he was reckoned
about the most determined “sticker” on that
side; and, thinking that his red-hot views would
suit torrid Flinders better than any other place,
they sent him up there in 1893 to win the seat
from Gold ring who had held it for years. After
a hard fight he won by about 30 votes. In the
stormy times of ’B9-’92 he was sent down south
during the strikes to get coin and sympathy. Up
to then the south hadn’t been stirred by Laborites
to any great extent; and the way that McDonald
talked in those days sent shivers through the older
unionists. He held Flinders easily from 1893 till
Kennedy, covering the same district and some
more, wanted a Federal member ; and McDonald
won that easily. He has done more electioneering
travel by bicycle than any other man in Australia ;
most likely more than any other man in the
world.

Aboriginalities.
Maryborough (Q.) has just captured its
historic crocodile. He had been a legend and an
institution on the Mary River for something liko
20 years, and Maryborough was long divided into
two camps—the people who believed in the ’dile
and those who declared there never was any ’dile.
Other things being equal, a Parliamentary candi-
date who believed in the reptile could always beat
one who didn’t. But even the most frequently
shot-at crocodile gets killed at last, and recently a
young man in a boat drew his gun at a venture
and plugged the old institution in the eye. Its
body was washed ashore, about a week later, 20
miles from the place where it breathed its last.
The weather was hot, and nothing like the odor
of that crocodile was ever before known in those
parts; 13J feet of crocodile that has been dead a
week is a terrible event.
* * * *
A week’s out-back weather on the Barrier,
where the water famine has been : Sunday, warm
and cloudy; S-unday night, thunder, lightning,
and rain ; Monday, heavy rain ; Tuesday, fine ;
Wednesday, cloudy morning, fine afternoon ;
Thursday, fine; Friday, dust all day; Friday
night, violent dust, thunder and lightning, heavy
rain; Saturday, fine; Sunday, clouds, thick dust
all the afternoon, heavy dust storm in evening,
clear starlight night. The unsolvable problem:
Where does the dust come from ? On Sunday,
while the dust was raging, the larger creeks were
still running and pools of water lay everywhere.

Nemo : He “ Alba,” on Hindus dying
to order (B. 23/7/03). The Kanaka is tarred with
the same brush. Was stopping a few days, once,
at Tooth and Cran’s sugar-plantation, on the
Mary River (Queensland), and was assured by the
overseer that it was no uncommon thing for
Tommy Tanna to take to his bed, foretell his early
decease, and die “on time”; this, too, after
being examined by a doctor, who failed to discover
any reason for death save pure cussedness. The
Australian aborigine has the same faculty, and,
among some tribes, it is only necessary to tell
Bmghi that he has been “ pointed at” (with a dead
man’s bone) to see him wither up and die, in the
full flower of his youth and beauty.

“ Caber ” : Can any of your aboriginally
crowd tell me for sure to what length the genus
carpet-snake stretches out ? When meandering
through the Brisbane Museum I saw a sixteen-foot
reptile labelled “ carpet-snake.” Did the Queens-
land naturalists blunder, and should the specimen
have been labelled “rock-snake”?
Australians should see that the Immigrants
Restriction Act is rigidly administered to exclude
the black and tan element of whatever nationality.
In every Victorian town of note, colored gen-
tlemen control the majority of fruit shops and
restaurants, and are rapidly taking up other
lines of business. Their standard of living is
very low, and the hours of labor almost the full
round of the clock. They give big rents for their
premises and make white competition impossible,
and, of course, the landlord, in the interests of
property, gives three loud cheers for this class of
tenant.

* * * *
The N.S.W. Assembly got talking the
other night about residence conditions on way-
out leases. Dr. Ross said he knew of a case in
which husband and wife, holding a lease each
requiring separate residence, built their home on
the boundary, so that the line bisected both the
bedroom and the principal article of furniture in
it. The House guffawed and told the doctor that
somebody had been hanging on to his leg again.
But it is quite likely this scheme was adopted
for all that ; the selector knows the land law is a
fearful and wonderful thing, and he does all sorts
of fearful and wonderful things in the belief that
he is nominally carrying out its provisions.
Dozens of “residences” certainly are built
on boundaries, even if the line doesn’t
run through the bed. Scores of selections are
held by young women, the daughters of selectors,
and in these cases the scheme is quite common.
If, as often happens, a brother and sister have
taken up adjoining selections, each requiring six
months’ residence in the year, either two huts are
built, one on each side of the line, or the two huts
are joined on the boundary, and they profess to
live on one side of the boundary one six months
and on the other side the other six.months. This
works all right where only six months’ residence
is required, and even sometimes where continuous
residence is in the bond, but a cantankerous
inspector is liable to cause trouble. Husbands
and wives can’t manage it as successfully as un-
married couples. There is an awkward legal pre-
sumption that the husband lives where the wife
is; and even if the boundary line bisects the
bedroom, the law says there is no evidence which
half of the bed is Billjim’s half.

A SPORTING OFFER.
KINDLY SOUL : “So you find times hard, do
you? ”
THE BEAT: " Hard? Hard? Here ” (desper-
ately) “gimme threepence, and see me drop
dead! ”
It was given in evidence in an out-back
N.S.W. court lately that a woman, tbe wife of a
selector, had herself sunk a well 40ft. deep on a
block held by her under miner’s right. She
reckoned well-sinking was easier work than
fencing, of which she had “ done miles.”
“ Revilo ” : Some M'aoriland
rivers teem with fish which the
Maoris are very ingenious in catch-
ing. I once saw a party of natives
fishing on the bar of a river. Two
rows of stout manuka poles were
stuck up about 25ft. apart in the
middle of the channel and when the
tide began to ebb, a long funnel-
shaped net was fastened in the open-
ing, the lower edge pinned firmly to
the bottom, and the top edge
fastened to a bar crossing from one
set of poles to the other. The net
was emptied every quarter-hour by
lifting up the long tapering end and
turning the contents into a canoe.
When the flood-tide commenced to
run strongly the net was simply
turned inside-out and set the other
way. Result of three-quarters of an
hour’s fishing: 58 eels, large numbers
of flounders and sea mullet, 701 b.
weight of pilchard, a few schnapper;
also katawai and hundreds of young
red cod. The river was about 60yds.
wide at the place, but the strip of
water wherein the fish were caught
was only 5 yds. wide.
* * * *
“Inver.”: Consider the cocky’s
dog. An ugly, blue or yellow no-
breed animal that massacres all
crawling and creeping things that
may have designs on fowl-roost or
potato-patch, -relentlessly chews in-
vading “townies” and agents, and
follows the plough all day, with oc-
casional spell-ohs to cool off in an
adjacent dam. Divides his attention
between the horses and the rear
plough-wheel, and generally sheds a
tooth or so in injudicious tussles with
the latter. He revels in water, where-
as collies mostly have a hydrophobic
horror of a wetting, a more than
womanly fear of “electrical disturbances,” and a
cat’s distaste for mud puddles. The collie, in
fact, is a failure on a farm. Its morbid sensi-
tiveness is against it, and it is too severe on stock.
The ideal cocky’s dog does a lot of barking and
running, but never does anything or gets any-
where.
“ Neer-do-Weel ” : I have noticed a
curious custom amongst Queensland bush school-
children —confined to the female children, I
think; at all events, haven’t noticed it among the
boys. My office in a western township was close
to the school, and when the
children were leaving in
lie afternoon I’d often
hear something like the
following : “ Teacher sez
to me, ‘You tyke thet
’ome agyne, Tulip Vera,
an’ do it k’rec’, an’ bring
it agyne ter-morrer; ’ an’
I sez to ’er, I sez, ‘I
shan’t do nothink er the
kind, Miss Hoolan. Yer
a fair caow, an’ yer
alwiz a puttin’on me.’ ”
“Yer didn’t sye nothnik
er the kin, Tulip Vera;
yer not gyme.” “I did,
Constance Meelyer, I
tell yer.” “ Well, strike
yer breast an’ spit, if
yer did, before I’ll be-
lieve yer.” Whereupon
Tulip Vera would strike
herself hard on the chest
and expectorate vigor-
ously, after which Con-
stance Meelyer’s doubts
would vanish, and Tulip
Vera would proceed with
her fiction amidst achorus
of admiring “Ohs!”
There seems to be much
virtue in the spit, for
once when a five-year-old
damsel was called upon
to make oath in manner
aforesaid, her action was
met with a volley of in-
dignant “But yer didn’t
spit.” “I did.” “Yer
never.” “I did, I tell
yer ” ; and so it went on
until it was decided the
lady must perform the cere-
mony again, which she did to
everyone’s satisfaction. I have
seen kiddies running along,
chattering like magpies, and
every now and again one or other
would bob her head, strike her
chest, and spit, to give point to an
argument or to clinch an assertion.
“ Chasselas ” writes :
“ Scallywag ” (Bulletin 27/8/’O3) is very far off the
track in stating that birds rarely meet with an accidental
death. Writer has picked up dozens of quail killed by
striking wire fences ; and wild turkeys and other birds
often strike telegraph wires and are killed. Have seen
quail almost cut in two by the force with which they
struck the wires. Have also seen larks impaled on the
barb used for top wires in country fences.

“ Mallee ” : Hungry sharks draw no rigid
“color-line.’’ Writer was once becalmed in a
windjammer off the coast of Sumatra, and we
were shooting at bottles with a rifle, when a shark
appeared, and we started on him ; but the bullets
MR. W. A. ROBYNS, A PROMISING YOUNG
AUSTRALIAN BASSO.
only bucked off his dorsal fin. We hooked him,
and, on investigation, found stowed in It is pantry
most of the black glass from the bottles we’d
smashed. Also, in same waters, harpooned a
boomer that had straightened out our hook, and
the brute sprang half out of the water to meet the
iron.
THE STIRRUP CUP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I am half afraid of the West I ” she said, looking out
where the sun went down
Like a ruddy fire on a mountain spire and the shadows
creeping brown;
And I bent my head as she raised the cup, this girl that I
loved the best,
And just for a moment of time I too was afraid of the
great red West.
It has parted so many lovers, I thought; oh ! the men
that, hopeful and brave,
Have pledged their girls in a stirrup-cup and gone out to
a Western grave !
I had laughed at the danger of drought and flood, but for
the sake of a blue-eyed maid
I turned my eyes from the beckoning range, and I looked
in her face—afraid !
N.S.W.
WILL H. OGILVIE.
THE HAM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where Tassy’s pathways Eastward trend,
Through miles of God-forgotten scrub,
Somewhere about Creation’s end,
I chanced to strike a wayback pub.
’Twas twenty miles from Might-Have-Been,
’Twas fifteen miles from God-Knows-Where,
And all the country in between
Was hungry as a rabbit snare.
I hunted up the man in charge ;
Perhaps I waked him from a snooze—
His moral vision wasn’t large,
His horoscope was rimmed with booze.
He’d limejuice cordial in the bar,
And terebene, and schnapps, and rum,
And all the trackers near and c ar
For his chain-lightning used 3 come.
It was the apple of his eye,
A fearsome home-brewed “ Knock-me-down,"
He used to make it on the sly
To Jew the customs folk in town.
But I assured that son of sin,
That keeper of a wayback pub,
’Twas not for rum, or beer, or gin,
My empty being yearned—but grub.
He turned away and scratched his head,
And clicked his tongue within his cheek,
“ Can’t give yer any duff,” he said ;
“ Blokes eat me out of duff last week !"
“ But there’s some pig I cured myself,
You’ll find it’s not too bloomin’ bad 1
And there’s a bottle on the shelf,
I’ll warrant it won’t leave you sad.”
I gazed upon the trusty ham,
1 viewed the late lamented hog.
“ Those marks y®u see upon the scram,”
He said, “ were made by Thompson’s dog.”

Last week I took to my bed
With a cough and a cold in my head,
My friends were all sighing, as they thought I
was dying,
But I banished all fears when I said :
“ Bad cold I may have for sure,
But to die—that I will not endure ;
J ust send for the stuff that will cure any cough,
Some Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.”*

* * * *
Croydon (Queensland) is a good deal
perplexed over the finding of a large live alligator
down one of its wet shafts. The theory is that
the animal walked 150 miles from the Norman
River and threw itself down the mine as a protest
against Socialistic legislation, but everything is
uncertain, save that the ’dile was killed by having
a rock dropped on it.
THE OTHER MEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I know a lot of fellows who
Seem never to be done ;
But talk away, by night and day,
Of battles they have won.
“ The brute weighed fully half-a-ton
And knew a flaming lot
About the game, and just at first
He made things very hot.
But when I’d finished up with him,
All there was left to show
Was just a heap of rags and bones
His mother wouldn’t know.”
I’ve heard that yarn a score of time 3;
I’ll hear it scores again ;
But what I want to know is this,
Where are the other men ?
What does become of all the chaps
Who get knocked out in fight ?
I’m looking out, but none of them
Has ever crossed my sight.
And then there is another crowd—
They mostly run to curls—
Who pitch a heap of stories steep
About their luck with girls.
And not with girls alone, for if
The half they say is true,
Their luck holds good with wives as well,
And even widows, too.
They never met a woman yet
Who could resist their charms ;
But maid and matron, young and old,
Just rushed into their arms.
Well, here’s a question I would ask —
Assuming that is so—
The folk who owned these women first,
Wherever did they go ?
The husbands, of those charming wives—
The chaps who were cut out—
I’ve never seen them, thouerh they must
Be somewhere round about.
There’s still a crowd to be described—•
You’ve met this sort, of course ;
When two or three, or more, maybe,
Got started talking horse.
They’d tackle anything at all
That ever wore a hide,
And don’t know what a fall is like,
For, bli’me they can ride.
How they poke fun at anyone
Who speaks of outlaws then.
“ Itats! Talk like that’s the sort for kids,
But we are blooming men.”
Now here again my voice I raise,
And ask if you have known
A rider who’d admit that he
Had even once been thrown ?
I often meet the chaps who stick,
But what I want to know
Is where on earth, or t’other place,
Do all the duffers go ?

LONG YEARS, GREY YEARS AGO.
—From a painting by A. J. Fisher, at Royal Art Society Exhibition.]
[For The Bulletin.]
YEA . . . and thou speakest, too,
“ Making my pulses leap, my spirits glow,
As when we two were youth and maid,
Long years, grey years ago.
0, Vision Beautiful!
Thou earnest, and thy lips are redj
Thou sayest that thou lov’st me still,
And I am comforted.
What false old tale was this ?
They said, that Death was foul to see ;
But Death hath brought me back my Love,
And sweet is Death to me.
RODERIC QUINN.
N.S.W. Art Society’s Show.
Most persons, especially those who have covered
their Household walls with gunny-sacks, de-
corated with dragons, storks and arabesques,
know that a love of Art is the true essence of the
Higher Life, It is love of Art that sends Sydney-
siders to view the last agony of the Art Society.
It is love of Art and other various emotions or
reasons that drive the artists to paint or perpetrate
pictures.
Some 365 pictures—oils, water-colors and black-
and-white—are hung in this, the 24th annual spasm
of the N.S.W. Art Society. Only the hanging
committee knows how many are sentenced to
banishment, and only the banished know how
unjust are the judges. There ought to be. a fresh
deal of the pack in the manner of judging the
Society’s September garnering. For the Tree
Picture (there are hundreds of specimens in
the exhibition) we ought to have the Con-
servator of Forests as judge. For the Harvest
Scene with its stacked wheat and standing
lucerne a produce merchant. For the Farm Scene,
with sheep and kine in the clover, or dying out
back in the dust-pan districts, a meat-salesman.
For the many flower pieces a florist, who skewers
table decorations and buttonholes. For the good
old barn of our youth a jerry-builder. For views
of Old Sydney, the Owe’Sullivan. For the nude,
a Turkish bath attendant. For the allegorical or
imaginative picture, as for instance Sid. Long’s in-
scrutable Fleur-de-Lys—the Omniscient Himself.
The General Public is easily pleased in its Art
menu. Give it a blue sky, a sea dipped out of the
same blend, a patch of sunlight with cool shade
under the spreading tree, and a line of misty
purple mountains in the distance, and no matter
how crude, the. G.P. will be very pleased, and
possibly say that the picture “shows great
artistic restraint.”
Those who forge and utter such sentiments pos-
sibly think that the painter’s self-restraint is
shown by his not seating a pair of lovers under
the tree. The bright colors stimulate the eye,
their harmonization into a landscape is quite a
secondary matter.
This Spring’s Exhibition is very youthful
in coloring. The intending exhibitor mostly
begins to work in August when Nature’s
sap is up, so there’s a glory on the leaf
and a rapture in the flower. The exhibitor, espe-
cially the female, is often a poetical creature
given to painting up to tag 3 of alliterative verse or
phrases, so we have “Where the Quiet Colored
End of Evening Smiles,” “ The Grey Robe of the
Rain,” “Gre3 T Grief” (in opposition to Swinburne’s
“Green Pleasure”), “Long Years —Grey Years
Ago.” The tenderly-sad and the softly-sombre
appeal to the pure white souls who paint pictures
for the Art Society’s show.
President Lister-Lister’s huge picture, “ Linger-
ing Summer,” occupies more than it deserves of
wall space. It is a riot of color—orange, gold,
rose and green. The scene, which is from a bit of
the Coolangatta estate, looks in its juiciness and
mellowness and greenness quite English, you
know. All the Listerian bag of colors are having
their annual outing. Piguenit’s “Thy Clouds
Drop Fatness” is a picture that demands that you
halt before it in admiration. A great grassy plain
covered with cattle—gazing, meditating, dozing,
and all shadowed by the dusk of racing clouds,
full to bursting with their weight of rain. In his
“ Sunset on Lane Cove River ” Piguenit proves
his possession of romantic feeling—a fine thing
to be able to use on the end of your hog
and camel hair brushes. Sid Long, after he
had finished his allegorical picture, “Fleur-
de-Lys,” must have felt that he had failed to
bring “before men’s minds the image of the
thing his heart was filled with.” “Fleur-de-Lys ”
shows a commonplace, half - undressed young
woman with apricot-colored hair, who is seated on
the ground against a background of iris lilies.
Her figure won’t bear publication, and the o iginal,
if there was such, of one of the starved children
who keep her company, must have been sketched
in the surgical ward of the Children’s Hospital.
The arms of the other infant resemble the fore
legs of a foal. But Long has several portraits that
do him great credit. That of Mr. John Perry,
Minister for Public Instruction, is a live portrait!
Sitter’s weary, humorous, and I-wouldn’t-believe-
your-affidavit expression is capitally brought out.
Julian Ashton, who also shows some attractive
views of old Sydney, has a portrait of Henry
Gullett, a gentleman with shining massaged
cheeks, good-natured black eyes and very sunburnt
hands. Fred Leist’s principal canvas, “Saplings,”
shows a girl’s figure standing, one hand clasped
behind her head, and through the transparent
drapery of tender baby-gum-leaf red, the fine
limbs are seen, but the damsel has not the appeal-
ing slenderness of sapling youth. On either side
of her is a growth of gum saplings, harmonising
with her drapery.
The delicate treatment of shallow water, that
soft tint of green sometimes seen in evening skies
and water-flowers, must have “fetched” the
National Gallery Trustees when they bought
Mann’s modest canvas, “Terrigal Haven,” for 30
guineas. The unruffled crescent looks fit to
harbor fairy craft. A. J. Fischer’s “ Long Years,
Grey Years Ago’’big picture (it carries a verse
by Roderic Quinn) is an old man to whom is
appearing the spectre of the sweetheart of his
youth. The joy, fear and amazement of the old,
hairy lover as he half raises himself from his bunk
to greet the vision is very pathetic, and there’s a
palette full of pathos painted into the hands
which seem at once to grope and to caress. As
for the spook, she is a green spook with blue. aura
and red palms. She must have been slaying
someone. “A Sou’-Easter” looks as if Henri
Tebbitt had painted it from the wreck of the
Dunbar place. Green and white seas batter a
rocky cliff, and wind-driven waters make yeasty
spray, and spume and froth and climb ; it is the
best sea-scape in the Ex. and makes a bad sailor
seasick. A lot of tame sea-pieces at sunset
hour are in the Exhibition. Placid bays, rippled
harbor inlets, pretty coves—but there is no
magic in the seas, no languor in the sunsets
that. color them. “Sensibility” and “per-
ceptivity” are good words, although they
have a Jane-Austen-novel sound, but they are
hereby tendered to Howard Ashton, to one H. J.
Recknell and C. H. Hunt as properties to be dis-
covered in their landscapes. Howard Ashton,
whose water-scape is a delightful picture, deserves
something more than Jane Austen phrases, and
Recknell’s “Haunts of Memory” stops in be-
holder’s memory until the day after to-morrow.
Among Dattilo Rubbo’s efforts are a specially
good deed and an unforgivable mortal sin. First
is the “Connoisseur” and shows an old anti-
quarian, a Dickens-like creation, with long coat
and knee breeches, looking through a magnifying-
glass at what might be a crucifix or a statuette. By
his expression he has a real “find.” The second,
“ The Odalisque,” is a bold-faced hussy with dis-
torted figure and limbs,, lying on a couch. No
Sultan would give twopence for her in the flesh —
much less 30 guineas for her portrait. Mr. Rubbo
had better give her away with a packet of nerve-
food and a pennorth of speckled apples. Garlick
supplies a strong and faithful study of a group
of blue kangaroos lying down. So that’s how a
’roo’s hind legs look when telescoped out! If the
’roo studied appearances he would never be seen
in repose, never be caught napping.
Young ladies who paint daffodils should never
mass them. The flower is such a pure Greek that
one or two of her is sufficient, and she should stand
upright on her own stem, not lie down, flattening
her adorable corolla. Amongst the men who can
paint a smiling woman—the real, spontaneous,
mirthful smile—is Moore-Jones. Two “smiling”
pictures bear witness. Fred. Leist’s “White
Boa ” (pastel) is worn over a pale-green blouse,
and with a big black hat. The narrow-shouldered
girl has an uncommon, pretty little wedge of face
obtrusively framed in black hair. Mr. Leist,
whose portraiture improves by leaps and bounds,
has treated her well; but number 227, the portrait
of a girl with fine, brown eyes and hair, is his best
effort. Norman Lindsay dominates the Black and
White section with his “ Scoffers ” and “ Curate’s
Little Wife” —both clever and wickod.
A. CONOR-O'BRIEN.
“WHY DON’T MEN GO TO CHURCH?”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sabbath bells again are calling,
Why is it we do not respond V
The shades of night about us falling,
We stand aloof in sheer despond ;
We hold not less the good, the holy,
We seek the truth in liberty ;
The manlier love that lovest wholly
The broader, kindlier, charity.
Like lonely birds that cry forsaken,
The bells are ringing faint and few ;
What mingled memories they awaken,
Of old loves still reproaching new,
And hates that bigots blindly cherish—
There may be gain in seeming loss,
But flowers there are that trampled, perish,
And lie around a Christless cross.
Shall ancient wrongs our free hearts sever,
And close to us the open way ?
Life hath its goal, and onwards ever
It moves, and must, or pass away ;
The hallowed altars of our fathers,
May waste, and crumble in the dust,
From out the heap of dross Time gathers
The gold, and holdeth it in trust.
Our virgin pastures tell no story
Of war that steeped its feet in blood,
The commonweal is all our glory—
For Christ’s sake have we much withstood ?
Faith falters ; still the vain strife rages,
And rival bells inspire our song ;
The end liss hidden in the ages
We only see, and feel the wrong.
We shrink and dread the first cold starkness
The solitary nevermore,
Dim lights there are that break the darkness,
And yet shall pierce it to the core.
Though faint, the Sabbath bells are ringing
And God’s will surely shall be done,
In strange ways ever nearer bringing,
The many whom He counts as one.
ANDREW SARD.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will betaken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on M 9. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned un less stamps (of any
State) accompany.
Joram : Singularly unequal. Two of the stanzas
stand out so brightly from the other 12 as to give sus-
picion of a crib E.L. : Just a glimmer of merit....
J.D. (Tas.) : Learn to spell. It doesn’t absolutely follow
that a man who can’t spell simple words can’t write, but
the odds are against him Chas. B.: Wales is not repre-
sented on the Royal Standard. The three lions (origin-
ally leopards) of England appear in both the Ist and 4th
quartess. As for the badges on the florin, the leek does
not appear with the rose, thistle, and shamrock, because
that vegetable has never been taken seriously as the
emblem of Wales Coo E. : Verse not very bad, but
“ Sunset ” a very hackneyed theme Vet: Will print...
J.A.R. : “Telephone” rather a used-up subject....
Sydney Pauli : Crude and irregular F.C.B. :No vigor
or spirit in the telling Frank O’R. : The central idea
is hoary Epsilon : Obscure —not profound... .Durdles:
Spoilt by the clumsy and involved style W.A.O. :
Clever, but belated Arbaces: Too explosive and
shrieky. With less vehemence you should make a mark.
E.J.D. : “ Prelude and Coda” too trivial Swingle-
bar : Rather dull G.L.: “Overheard” declined
Rei Panes: “Scent of the Wool” a too-hackneyed
subject. The number of poets whose thoughts are
carried back to the old station is terrible. Will
print “Sylvia." Byrnong : Reads realistically, but
hasn’t point or weight enough M.E.C. : With
brighter, terser handling might have been readable.
....Argo: It is a very common blunder, due to
confusion between the terms “ contradictory ” and
“paradoxical.” We rightly say “contradictory as it
seems ” ; but, since “ paradoxical ” means only appar-
ently contradictory, we should say “ paradoxical as it is”
—not “seems.” V. de M. : More briefly treated it
might have had achance Thurla : Will print “ Chang”
as an aboriginally J.A. : Body-snatching yarn an old
Irish tale B.E.N. : Puzzled to know which was worst.
Finally decided that they were all the worst! Ruis :
They have some merit, but not enough Jumbo:
Appropriately elephantine... O.: Reads like a
cross between Shelley and Kipling... .W. B.A. : Very
correct in form, but empty of all life and movement....
Dorian : Your touch isn’t deft enough for such a ticklish
subject Borax: “Why is Herbert Spencer so neg-
lected ? ” Probably on account of his stodgy manner
S.P.A.R. : Declined Isabelle: Ideas and figures too
familiar R.F.G.: You were a week late with your
poem, Irvine’s charger having been treated of already.
It’s a very hard thing to be up-to-time in a State where
the locomotive is still suffering from Bent Alma S.:
Pretty album verse Disgne : That man who tries to
get The Bulletin on to the Hobart Library table is no
friend of ours The Hack : Thanks; the brick is about the
same weight, anyhow L.M. : There is no clumsier way
of libelling an individual than to start “ a certain indivi-
dual,” and then to make the identity quite clear and utter
some slanderous statement. A jury interprets that as
cowardly malice Bandar: “ Star Worlds ” declined....
M. Culp'in : On the whole prefer the scheme we have long
advocated—of requiring the banks to hold 40 or 50 per
cent, of their cash reserves in State notes, as practised in
Canada Rosa D. : “Greeting” won’tscan.. ..D.H.R. :
“ Sold ”is very thin'and weak H. Gibson : Deciphered
it with difficulty and found it hardly worth the trouble.
Ruff Rydor: Hasn’t style or vigor enough L.W. :
Agree that the Slimes is “ a cur of a paper,” but don’t
see the necessity of amplifying that obvious fact in 320

conventional lines. You’d probably do better work if you
would strike out for yourself D.G. : Too spasmodic
and patchy Dusty M. : You have concealed your
meaning (if you have any) with great skill F.0’0.:
Some descriptive power, but spelling and syntax badly in

but thin and unconvincing in story. Try a simpler and

A GUARANTEED CURE FOR PILES.
PAZO OINTMENT is guaranteed to cure any case of
Itching, Blind, Bleeding, or Protruding Piles, no matter
how long standing, or money refunded. Cures ordinary
cases in six days. This is a new discovery, and it is the
only Pile Remedy sold on a "ositive guarantee. 2s. 3d.
Of Chemists, or from PARIS MEDICINE CO., 28, Shoe-
lane, London, England.*
A. J. Knowles, one of the founders of the
Canada bicycle trade, and the man who brought the Red
Bird bike to Australia and made a big name in the Aus-
tralian cycling business, turned all the horses out of
Tattersall’s bazaar, Sydney, the other day. Then he flung
out the nose-bags, and rugs, and chaff-cutters and the rest,
and plunked in £12,000 worth of motor-cycles, pleasure
automobiles, motor-touring carriages, and motor-goods-
trucks. Knowles, who taught Australia that the way to
run fast is to do it on a bicycle, has now undertaken to
teach Australia that the way to go driving is to do it in a
motor-car, a vehicle which never wants horses, and which
neither shies, bolts, nor runs away when left standing
and not hitched. Knowles has opened stores in
Sydney and Melbourne, and he will shortly open in
Adelaide, Brisbane, and Westralia. Also Maoriland will
have the Knowles Motbr Co. Ltd. Already there is a
motor’bus plying between Napier and Taradale (M.L.).
Pat, the driver, reports that it goes “ like a streak.” The
fact about the ’bus motor is that it costs from 40 to 75
per cent, less than the horse ’bus for working. The motor
has been adopted by Europe and America for all kinds of
service where horses were formerly employed—delivery
carts, furniture waggons, heavy waggons, &c., &c. ; and
the extent of the trade can be gauged from the fact that
France sent 3,000,000 francs’ worth of pleasure auto-
mobiles to England last season, while in the past three
years one American manufactory has sold 22,000 motor
pleasure carriages.*
In response to the appeal to return Boer
Bibles, one of those articles was given up last week
to the Queensland authorities. Said Bible was
modestly wrapped in a brown bag, and the person
returning it did not give his name. The honesty
of the farm-burner ! He returns the one thing he
can’t pawn. No Boer whiskers or teeth have yet
been handed back.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.

Debility Ended.
Bile Beans Again Pbominent.
“Your Bile Beans have proved a valuable
remedy in iny household,” says Mrs. E. J. Ramus
of 1 Arundle-street, Forest Lodge, Sydney. “I
am sometimes troubled with debility, especially
during the hot weather, and when I feel done up
with a tired, listless feeling, I take a Bean or two,
with beneficial results. As a gentle purgative
they are all that can be desired, and leave no evil
after-effects whatever. My son and daughter also
take Bile Beans, with like beneficial results, and
from an all-round experience I can safely say that
Bile Beans are unequalled for poor appetite
sleeplessness, and that “out of-sorts ” feeling so
prevalent during the present season.” If you are
feeling “run-down” or “out-of-sorts,” take a
lesson from this lady’s experience and tone up the
system to meet the severe tax imposed by the
changeable Spring weather. Bile Beans will be
found a speedy and permanent cure for biliousness,
headache, indigestion, constipation, piles, debility’
female weaknesses, nervousness, bad blood’
pimples and all skin eruptions, bad breath’,
anaemia, loss of appetite, weakness after influenza’
Spring ailments, and,_ in fact, all ailments that
owe their origin to a disordered liver. Bile Beans
are obtainable from all medicine vendors, price
Is. lid. or 2s. 9d. large box, (contains three times
the Is. size.)*
The Canadian Government finds it necessary to
increase its issue of paper money from rather over £4,000,000
to over £6,000,000 in order to meet the public demand.
In Canada the banks issue their own notes as they do
here--if there were no private note issues the Govern-
ment note would probably take the place of the banks’
paper, and, in that case, the Government note issue
would probably run to about £12,000,000. The Dominion
note issue is based almost entirely on that law which
requires the banks to hold 40 per cent, of their cash
reserves in Government paper money. The Government
will hold £1,500,000 in gold in order to meet any notes that
may be presented—the other £4,500,000 will be a perpetual
loan to the State, without interest, and without reducing
the available currency. This is the system, the adoption of
which in Australia would furnish the means to acquire a
Federal territory and build a capital; after which the
revenue from that territory would be available for the
steady reduction of the public debt without levying a
penny of taxation upon the people. The present system
of keeping £20,000,000 of coin (representing a loss of
) nearly £BOO,OOO a year in interest) lying in the Australian
banks is a ruinous follv.

An angry shareholder forwards a copy of the
balance sheet of the Bray-brook Implement Go. (Vic ) for
the year ending J une 30th, !9°3. All things considered,
b ? qrobab y do !t we !L t 0 be an f?ry. The earnings were
~ 8> on th ® ° ther side, directors’ fees and auditors’ fees
alone amounted to £290. Other expenses (interest, can-
vassing, salaries, &c.,) reached £2226. Including what
appears to be a. quite inadequate allowance for deprecia-
tion the year s losses were £2763. The co.’s debts to the
Pl'Mm »ow amount to £lO 635 ; and a general deficiency
of £9511 is shown. Probably the real deficiency is much
larger, because, as said just now, it is almost certain that
the writings-ofif for depreciation have not been nearly
large enough. The banks and principal creditors have
secured themselves more or less by taking deben-
tures covering the whole of the assets ; if they
forced a realisation it is pretty certain that little
or nothing would be left for the shareholders. The co
is a good type of what Australia would be if the forei-m-
-trader had his way. It depends upon the “ great natural
industries of the country—it has tried to live by relying
upon the farmer and the grazier. And already it has lost
lialf its capital (the capital was some time ago written
down from 1 39,640 to £19,820 by order of the court), is
now in the hands of its money-lender, and looks very
much as though it might easily lose the rest of its capitai.

* * * *
“Wanderer”: Unhappy developments have
occurred in Lloyd s Burraga copper mine. Over thirty
men have lately either left or been discharged. Things
nave been made very uncomfortable for the leadin'"-
members of the miners’ union. The secretary was askeS
to either resign from the A.M.A. or leave his billet,
lie, like many others, went. Earnings are being cut,
and in some cases grown youths are working for 4s. per
shift. It is likely that the trouble will be taken to the
Arbitration Court. It cannot be said that the union
men were actually discharged (excepting the secretary) ;
but men were taken from work at which they earned
9s. a shift and asked to go shovelling for 6s. Yet lam
told that more ore is being- raised at present than ever
before, and also that there is abundance of good ore in
the workings. It is also stated by those who should
know that the ore is giving 15 per cent., although
shares are falling. There is no sound reason why wages
should be reduced. Also, it looks something more than
coincidence that the miners forced out of work are
leading members of the A.M.A.

“ Schelite ”: Bulletin (27/8/’O3) stated that
“ the late Lewis Lloyd was heart-broken after a syndicate
bought the Burraga mine.” The Western miners would
not have mind"' l had his heart broken years before, for
as a sweater he w as first in hard-rock mining in Australia.
He paid 6s. per day to miners, the wage in some other
mines being 7s. 6d., but the usual wage Bs. 4d. Men who
owned up to working in mines from one end of Australia
to the other would not confess to Burraga, which would
have brought down on them all the odium attaching to
blacklegs.

In the last five years the Broken Hill Pro-
prietary Co. has paid in freight alone, to Australian
States and companies, £1,124,531—£704,748 to the S.A.
Government, £250,956 to the Silverton Tram Co., and
£168,827 to coastal shipping cos. On top of that is the
amount paid to deep-sea vessels for carriage of bullion,
timber, &c.

Of the 960,000 shares in the Broken Hill Prop.,
all but 350,713 are now held on the London register ; so
that, of the £48,000 dividend just paid, £30,465 goes
Bull-wards. Of the £17,535 distributed in Australia
Adelaide takes £8531, Melbourne £5417, and Svdnev
£2368.
* * * »
The mistake that was made years ago by N.S. W.
Mines Department in issuing mining leases covering gem-
bearing country at White Cliffs, is causing trouble again ;
at lease, that is causing part of the trouble—the other
part is caused by the original owners passing their leases
on to an English co. with an absurdly high capitalisation.
This co. tried to make ends meet first by working on
day-wages and then by tributing, but couldn’t ; and
later on, with the approval of the Mines Department, it
threw its leases open to anybody who liked to pay a
license fee of £6 10s. a year. With the poorer gem in
small demand this tax is felt severely, and the miners are
making another attempt just now to get rid of it. It is
an awkward situation. The Government, having granted
the leases, cannot do anything ; while even at £6 10s. per
miner per year the co. can’t make 1 per cent, on its capi-
tal. The only people, in fact, who have scored are those
who got the co. to take over the leases at the price.
* * * *
Sydney Stock Exchange members are growling
because several mining cos. do not publish returns. Why
don’t the Exchange men act instead of growl? They
have a simple remedy at command. It is a condition of
a stock being “listed” that the secretary of the co. shall
furnish reports regularly. If, after warning, this is not
done the Exchange should not hesitate to remove the
stocks from the list. Shareholders would then want to
know the reason why, and the game of secrecy, which
may suit some directors and secretaries, would be
played out. The greatest sinners in this respect
are Oobar companies. Take a continuous group
—-Ohesne.v, Young Australian, Western, and Occidental 1
Shareholders are reminded occasionally that the first
three still exist by intimations of calls or reconstructions ;
there is never any report as to what work, if any, is being
done. Occidental pays a dividend now and then, but
whether it is earned out of the mine, out of old tailings,
or whether the directors are magnanimous enough to lend
the money, no one outside the charmed circle ever knows :
even the half-yearly reports are silent on the subject.
The Mines Department is the worst sinner of all in toler-
ating this state of things ; it ought to insist, as the
Departments in other States do, on mine-owners fur-
nishing monthly returns, to be available to the public.
The works which the Broken Hill Proprietary,
Block 14, and other Broken Hill cos. are erecting will
not establish the zinc industry in Australia ; all they are
designed to do is to concentrate the zinc-bearing
material, which will call for little labor, the product
being shipped to Europe to be worked up into metal by
more enterprising communities.
* * * *
“Shaker ”: It would be advisable to abolish the
cash rewards offered by Queensland Government for the
discovery of new goldfields, retaining the reward claim as
at present. The existing system encourages a certain class
of alleged prospectors to report the finding of a new
field on totally inadequate grounds, in the hope that the
crowd, when it conies, will discover something which will
enable the said alleged prospectors to claim the reward.
It is a dangerous game, no doubt, but it has been played,
with various degrees of success, several times of ‘late
years. There probably remain very few goldfields, of
sufficient importance to warrant the pajunent of a cash
reward, to be discovered in Queensland, and the poll-
taxed Queenslander can’t afford to go to “ duffer” rushes
these times. It would do no harm, too, to hang, say
half-a-dozen newspaper correspondents, as a hint not to
use quite so powerful a microscope on alleged new finds.

“Tambaroora”: When writer left Charters
Towers, three years ago, he sold 300 Queen Crosses at
7s. 6d. —and smiled. At present Queen Cross, owing to a
lucky crosscut into a foot wall, is paying a monthly 2s. a
share, and scrip (doubled issue) is selling at about 70s.
This constitutes the heaviest cross writer has so far had
to bear.
* * * *
W.A. official figures for 1902 show that gold to
the value of £7,947,662 was won, and £1,424,272 was paid
in divs., or about 18 per cent, of the product. The mar-
ket value of shares in div.-paying co’s. is about £11,500,000,
so that the proportion of divs. to value is about 12 per
cent. There may be individual mines, such as the
Boulder and Perseverance, which may be worth ten
years’purchase, hut in the aggregate'it is certain that
stocks, comparatively low as they now are, are grossly
over-valued. Since 18S6 £37,670,312 worth of gold has
been produced, and £7,483,112 has been paid in divs. •
investigation would probably disclose that the latter does
not wholly represent mine profits, but includes share-
dealing profits. There were 20,476 persons employed in
mines in 1902, and “ the value of gold produced per man
was £3BB”—a calculation which points to nothing in par-
ticular in the absence of information as to the amount of
capital expended in addition. Altogether, the figures
make one thing clear—that mining operations are expen-
sive in W.A.

* * * *
The erection of the Associated Northern (W.A.)
treatment plant is making good headway. The main
shaft has nearly reached the requisite depth for opening
out on the rich ore alleged to have been located
by the diamond drill, but there are still sceptics It
should not be forgotten that Capt. Parkes, after locating
the rich chute in the Oroya by boring, crosscut 50ft. west
from the shaft, then drove north, and finally found the
lode by crosscutting. 150 ft. east, so that the drill
utterly misled him. In sinking the shaft on the Northern
the boreholes have not been found, so the drill probably
went off at a tangent. It turns out that a letter from
Capt. Rodda, dated March, in which the true state of the
ore reserves was given, was suppressed ; hence some of
the trouble in London. Mr. Watt, at one time metal-
lurgist of the Kalgurli, has been appointed assistant
manager, a not very enviable position in the present
precarious state of affairs.
* * * *
Lake Way Goldfield (W.A), a northern wild-
cat, has been successfully reconstructed with a 2s. assess-
ment, which will provide another £15,000 to play with.
Great stress is laid on the fact that manager Leslie Hall
has accepted fully paid shares in lieu of £2OOO cash due to
him by the old co. The co. paid, in October, 1900, one 6d.
dividend, which was not earned ; since then it has been
in a chronic state of collapse. The mill has treated some
8000 tons of joz. stone, and shareholders were assured
that tailings assayed 10 to 13dwt. Holders were
also assured that the property contained a lode 14ft. wide
of similar stuff to that treated, and that this could be got
out so cheaply that mining cost only 4s. and milling ss.
per ton. This was in February 1902'; yet in August 1902
the mill was hung up, and ail hands fired out. Since then
the mine has been closed. Either the interests of share-
holders have been sadly neglected, or the estimates were
all wrong.
* * * *
Peake station, close in to the heart of Aus-
tralia, is making a big struggle to set the mohair industry
on its legs ; the station’s flock of angoras, most of them
either pure or but one or two removes from pure, now
numbers 1200. They are shorn every eight months ; each
clip gives about 41b. per full-grown animal, and the hair,
which must be pure white, is worth from lOd.tols. Bd.
per lb. The present main sources of supply are Turkey
(chiefly Asia Minor) and S’Africa. In S’Airica there is an
export duty of £lOO a head on angora bucks. The drier
the climate the finer the lustre of the fleece ; and if
Australia can get hold of something besides its thirst
that is improved by a drought it will be a godsend.
Years ago the late Price Maurice tried to set
the industry going in the far north of South
Australia ; and the Peake people say they are
convinced that he failed to make a commercial success of
it simply because he tried it in a place with too good a
rainfall. On the average, Great Britain buys £1,250,000
worth of mohair annually ; for the first half of this year
the figure was below the n0rma1—9,170,1731b., valued at
£565,994, or a trifle under 15d. per lb. S’Africa and
Turkey supplied all of this save 150,04 lib.

Several Chow miners who have been for a long
time workingan alluvial claim at Beechwoi th (Vic.), are
about to float a company with a capital of £lO,OOO, and
have ordered a 320-horse power steam emrine to drive
gravel pumps and giant nozzles, etc. The board of
directors will be composed of Chinese, but they intend to
employ ‘‘ competent European managers and engine-
drivers.” Some “good union miners” will also be
engaged at ordinary union rates.
* * * *
“Kea”: Re Kotuku oil springs (M.L.). They
are now boring at a depth of 550 ft. The flow of salt
water still continues. The pipe throws up a fountain of
brine 50ft. in height, which shows no sign of diminishing.
Fern leaves, old boots, branches, etc., left 24 hours in the
spray, become prettily encrusted with a hard and brittle
mixture of salt and silica. All vegetation within a radius
of 200 yards has been destroyed, and streams of salty
bitterness wander in ail directions. A little oil collects
nightly in the bore, and burns with much crackling. One
of the two companies at work has recently borrowed the
Grey Harbor Board’s boring plant. Shares are dormant,
and general opinion tends towards cats.
* * * *
The Trans - Pacific, a N.Q.-owned Brazilian
dredging venture, is no bonanza, according to reports.
Last cable reported 50oz. for 205 hours’ work. Recent
half-yearly meeting figures were 6840 z. for 1793 hours
(gold value, £3 195.). A 6d. call follows. A private
advice states that the dredge was averaging only 30 to
35 hours’ work a week, owing to lack of firewood. Still,
“ paids ” (£1) are said to have been bought in by the right
oi owd at £1 ss. As the stock is not listed the market
cannot talk. Rumors arrive that matters are not as they
should be in Brazil, and one prominent director declined
to continue on the hoard. 'Shareholders send an expert
over in September to report, at a cost of i 500. A Maori-
land party, headed by one of the Outtens, is in the same
territory to try another dredging proposition.
Apropos "miners’disease” (due to the accumu-
lation of particles of drill-dust on the lungs), the
Transvaal Government mining engineer mentions in his
report for 1902 that, of 1177 rock-drill men who went to
Englaud when the war started, they had proof of death
of 225 before it finished ; and no doubt many others died
without the news getting back to Africa.

An immediate result of the “ swop ” of
complimentary visits between King Ned and
President Lon bet is the stoppage by the French
Government of the Somali Mollah’s rifle supplies,
and a disclosure of the fact that an English firm

In a moment of thoughtlessness Thomas
Bent published a return showing the money
that the Victorian municipal bodies had col-
lared from the Treasury during the last six
years. The total amount reaches to £1,631,689.
Of that sum £233,000 came out of loans; the
remainder was taken from the consolidated
revenue. The whole of this money has gone to
improve the landed property of the men who
daily cry out about the burden of taxation, and
who want to make up all deficiencies in the
revenue and expenditure account by cutting down
the salaries of public servants.
The Gaud - Mayoralty of Melbourne
was delayed just 23 years. In March, 1878,
William Collins, Lord Provost of Glasgow, ad-
dressed a letter to “The Right Honorable the
Lord Mayor of Melbourne, Australia, ” drawing
attention to an appalling calamity which had
occurred at Blantyre Colliery, and asking for aid.
The then Mayor, John Pigdon, thought that
“ next Queen’s Birthday ” would bring the even
then coveted distinction—but it didn’t. The
address wasn’t a premonition ; it was only a
blunder.
Richardson, a Westralian M.L.C., lately
struck a worse bit of luck than usual. He moved
in favor of some pet fad. On the voices the
chairman declared it carried. But Richardson
mistook the chairman, and called for a division.
Having called for a division when a motion was
declared carried, Richardson’s vote was, by the
rules of the House, counted with the Noes against
iiis own motion ; and this transferred vote shifted
the balance—the Noes had it now. So Richardson
killed his own pet lamb, and the House enjoyed it
almost as much as N.S. W. Assembly would if Dr.
Ross could be inveigled into slaying his Bill for
putting down the baneful cigarette.
The resistance offered to N.S.W. Board
pf Health by such bodies as the councils of Parkes,
Tamworth, Balmain, and Redfern proves that,
with regard to sanitary precautions which cost
money, the local alderman is certainly unfit to
ha.ve the final decision. If the councils are to
continue to represent the landlords it is obviously
necessary for the protection of the public gener-
ally, that the Board of Health should have more
authority. What is required is legislation which
will empower the Board to levy and enforce a
special sanitary rate in the event of the local
council declining to take proper steps to safeguard
the health of the people it generally misre-
presents.
“ A an ” on N.S.W. land legislation :
IL 'h' e recently become possessed of a small area of
N.S.W. The means I adopted to become a “landed pro-
prietor are strictly honorable so far as the legislation of
this country is concerned, but, when the question of
equity between individual and nation is introduced, seem
to me nothing short of a scandal. Originally selected as a
non-residential conditional purchase, it is steadily becom-
ing-freehold. This alienation of land, which should be
for ever the common .property of the nation, is rendered
all the more simple by small annual instalments being all
that is expected from the purchaser. (Certain improve-
ments are, of course, compulsory, but, as they pass from
the Crown with the freehold, they do not enter into the
question.) This land in question is now let by me at
ah almost nominal rental, but, even then, returns me
50 per cent, on the amount annually payable by me to
the Crown. I might have made it ioo per cent., but I
didn fc have the heart to ask the Government to forego
the rent on account of the drought. Thus, through
practically no fault of my own, I am becoming absolute
ovvner of portion of the country—not by paying for it
but by getting it for nothing, and at the same time
deriving an income from it 1 If the Crown demanded the
amount annually payable by me in lespect of the above-
mentioned piece of land as a perpetual rental instead of
a time-payment purchase, it would still be a most profit-
able investment for me.
