POLITICAL
POINTS
Maffra anti-Federal capital movement is
palpably a dead-beet one.
Premier See of N.S.W. says that he will
never consent to transfer the N. SAV. railways
to Federal control. But after all, John See will
be here such a very short time now that his
opinions hardly matter.
The longest thing in the Victorian State
Parliament when the news came that the House
of Reps, had voted to bring railway servants
under the Arbitration law was the countenance of
Samuel Lancaster of Kyabram.
In the troubled land of Servia the people
have already begun to throw road metal at the
new king, and a loyal subject in Nish is reported
as having slapped the monarch’s open countenance
—with impunity, so far as the cable intelligence
goes.
Ex-Treasurer Illingworth, of Westralia,
says that unless the transcontinental railway is
built he will vote for secession from the Common-
wealth. Poor, excited Shillingworth might just
about as well- vote, for secession from the solar
system.
It is said that the shifting of See-Owe’Sul-
livan means the prompt stoppage of ornamental
work at the new Sydney railway station at Devon-
shire-street. Accommodation sheds will be com-
pleted, but Carruthers, it is believed, will go
direct for a line to the harbor.
FREEDOM OF CONTRACT.
The S.M. HERALD and Sydney Telegraph
have informed their agents that they will not he
allowed to sell any other Sydney morning news-
paper that may he issued, under penally of losing
the agency for the Herald and Telegraph
aforesaid.
N.S.W. Treasurer Waddell says there is
no case on record where the Government
borrowed' money for the improvement of Crown
lands, and then sold the lands, the proceeds
going into the general revenue. It is a pity
Waddell doesn’t know his subject a little better.
Alleged Senator Reid recently :
With our small community of 3f millions of people,
spread over a territory as big as Europe, we cannot go
on providing a vast army of men with snug billets, to
walk about and do nothing.
Do nothing ! Haven’t they to watch for Reid’s
firm’s fraudulent invoices ?
The most remarkable thing about the
speech at the opening of Vic. Parliament was the
thing that wasn’t in it. There was no allusion to
the fact that Victoria has a .£5,500,000 loan falling
due in about three months, and that it doesn’t
know yet whether it will be able to get the money
or not.
Position with regard to the proposed
Commonwealth agreement with the Eastern Ex-
tension Co. is that the Senate will not ratify the
bargain .until the Pacific Cable Board has been
given a chance of reviewing it; 20 members are
pledged to this policy, and in a House of 36 that
constitutes a good majority.
Amongst all the rant and blather about the
horrors of the stokehole in the Red Sea, does it
ever occur to practical men that a little money
spent on ventilation and cooling appliances might
enable white men to put up with the “horror”
very conveniently ? A stiff back on the part of
the Commonwealth now might precipitate reform
of the conditions under which stokers labor on all
the seas. Shareholders, guinea pigs and their
paid advocates, will hang on to the horrors till the
last gasp, because horrors are cheaper than reform.
The V ic. Iceberg Government has caused
the Governor of Pentridge gaol to send out price-
lists with specimens of printing done in that
prison to all police officers in charge of suburban
and country stations, with a special request that
they will bring them under the notice of secre-
taries of local cricket clubs with a view to obtain-
ing orders.
George Reid, when he proposed the toast
of “ Australian industries ” at the N.S.W. Cham-
ber of Manufactures shivoo, was taking the first
steps to making himself square with the Protec-
tionists. When he offered to abandon Freetrade
agitation for the rest of his life, provided a Refer-
endum gave a majority against him, he took the
second step.
Senator Drake hasn’t the capacity to lead
the Senate. When he takes charge from O’Connor
the House at once slips away from the Govern-
ment. The other day he tactlessly tried to bring
down a resolution “ by command of the Governor-
General.” The House was taking no commands
from any G.-G., and the Ministry received a
severe snub.
G. H. Reid’s denunciation of the Barton
Government for alleged want of candor in regard
to the dropping of the Arbitration Bill was in that
genial contortionist’s very best style. His own
candor about the measure was characteristic.
Although he made a speech favoring the Bill, and
denounced the Government for abandoning it, yet
upon the two points which have caused all the
trouble—the extension of its provisions to over-
sea shipping and State employees—he carefully
refrained from expressing any opinion at all.
Since the Victorian Iceberg Ministry came
into power 14 months ago there have been four
tumbles. First the pious M'Kenzie, who was in
the top ranks of political elders, went under.
Then the respectable political importer Robert
Reid fell heavily. Next, Shiels the Raven dropped
from the Treasury to the Railway Department.
The last change has brought up that remarkable
political nonentity, Sachse, as Minister for Edu-
cation. J. M. Davies goes back to the Law port-
folios, and Irvine becomes simple Premier and
Treasurer.
A. very proper person by the name of
Thomas Shaw, who was one of those who cheered
the Irvine Government when it engaged in the
joyous task of sitting on the Victorian
Public Service, has gone frothy at the
mouth over its compulsory land pur-
chase proposals. He rampages over half
a column in the daily press on the sub-
ject. “To drive a man out of his hard-
earned possessions,” says Thomas,
“ may be a Russian way of doing things,
but it is not English. As a land-owner
I maintain that to compel a man to
sell his land is unnecessary, unjust,
tyrannical and iniquitous.” Also: “I
contend that com pulsory sale of their
lands is tyrannical, and only fit to be
perpetrated by a Czar of Russia or a
Turkish Sultan.” One would almost
think this was the language of a sacked
engine-driver. How insurrectionary
and disrespectful to their betters these
Tories are at times.
Another official report about the
the awful rottenness of the British
army has been published and, like
the rest, it will lead to nothing.
The disease lies in British
reverence for “ governing
families in the fact that
the selection of officers is
confined, as far as possible,
to men of wealth, pedigree
and “haw,” and there
aren’t enough geniuses with
wealth, pedigree and
“ haw ” to make an army;
that the average British
officer comes from a class
which hasn’t toiled for some generations and
knows more of shooting game than of hard
work; and that too much pedigree and “haw"
have created the idea that the British army,
so far as concerns the officers, is a club
rather than a practical line of defence. And
when the “governing classes” inquire into the
frightful state to which they have brought the
army they can’t be expected to discover that they
are themselves the incurable disease.
Having, as he thought, on Wednesday of
crisis week jumped too far towards the Labor
corner, lieid devoted Thursday to getting back to
the Argus attitude. One tactless remark, that
white stokers were “the scum of the earth,”
should ensure him a hearty reception when the
Woolloomooloo waterside workers see him next
election time.
While Premier Philp returns from a cham-
pagne banquet tour in North Queensland and says
that he could see no unemployed, the Brisbane
press describes how 1280 rations were issued to
nard-up men in Mackay the other day. But it
doesn’t often say things like that. The most
truthful part of the daily press nowadays is the
advertisement column.
Lancaster, the boss ’Pootra, at Echuca
(Vic.) the other day, talking about irrigation, said
the prosperity of the Rodney area was an indica-
tion of the value of water. “ Rodney had sent
away 4030 tons of fruit, which meant £4OOO to the
railways in freight.” Why doesn’t this prosper-
ous Rodney send in as “ conscience money ” the
wealth it swindled out of the State ?
The S’African “ patriot,” on whose behalf
the Empire made war, is about the most bowel-
less brigand of all times. He used the war to
plunder the country that was disgracing itself by
fighting for him ; now he is using the peace just as
unscrupulously. At Pretoria £621,513 has been
spent in connection with the re-stocking of bur-
ghers’ farms, &c. The burghers have got
£303,536; the brigands, for “ administering ” the
funds, have got £317,977. And there are scores
of such cases.
Apropos Premier Irvine’s resumption
scheme. Victoria has already done a little re-
suming of a kind. Certain land in Lonsdale-
street, Melbourne, was bought in the boom time,
and at boom prices, to oblige some boom friend,
and it has lain idle for 14 years, the loss in interest
alone being £44,991. Another block near the
Titles Office, purchased in 1887, has been unoccu-
pied to date; the interest on the capital cost
being now £53,28L. On these two blocks alone,
including the capital cost, the sum of £229,145
has been thrown away.
In the teeth of half-hearted official denials
(Melbourne papers don’t seem to
attach much credence to the word
of Premier Irvine nowadays)
the Age has been persist-
ently declaring for the past
week or two that the Victo-
rian Government is going
to issue debentures for
£500,000 or so to get money
for public- works. The.game
that Bent, as Public Works
Minister, is playing is a beau-
tiful piece of bluff. Money is
needed for the water and
other schemes already in
hand, but there is a very
shadowy chance of the Govern-
ment being able to raise any-
thing like its proposed .£500,000,
on top of the <£5,500,000 loan
conversion a few months hence. When Bent)
the Bluffer fails to get his loan money he
will be in a position to announce that no
works can be done as he hasn’t got the funds.
And the Victorian public, which never heard
of raising money for public works by a gene-
ral land-tax, will hold further meetings at
Kyabram and elsewhere, and deprecate the
exodus and everything else except its own
stupid dishonesty and unwillingness to bear
a due proportion of taxation.

The Chiltern correspondent of Melbourne
Age, in reporting the caper of some Melbourne
produce agents who asked local storekeepers to
forward letters falsely stating that certain con-
signments of chaff were for starving stock, so as
to get special railway rates, remarks gently that
“it is considered that an inquiry is necessary.”
Well, tastes differ. Some people would say that
a police court prosecution, with. 12 months' hard
labor on conviction, is necessary. /
The “ pair ’’ system is getting to be a good
deal abused in the Federal Parliament. When
a member goes away for a long spell, leaving
a general arrangement with another member
to “ pair ” on all divisions, it often turns out that
he is made to vote the wrong way—as he explains
at great length later on. There have been of late
several such incidents, and the whole system
should he abolished. The spectacle of a man
going away and leaving behind him his decision,
without knowing what he is deciding about, is
very mechanical legislation.
The commercial man in this country is a
weepful thing. When the poll-tax (which, pro-
duced £BO,OOO a year by laying a special class tax
on people who were earning as little as ss. a
week) was imposed,(the commercial class was
quite satisfied. But when it was proposed to
raise half that sum by a tax on the rich, the cries
of the Brisbane Chamber of Commerce lifted the
roof, and it demanded that the gap in the revenue
should be made good by extra land sales, and said
that if the land wouldn’t sell for 10s. an acre it
should be thrown away for whatever it would
bring.

years ago it had no debt

of the Royal assent to the Reform Bill. He has cabled,
but can get no satisfaction.”— Age.
Bent, Irvine, Kyabram and Syme draft another
Coercion Bill.
s
Just before meeting Parliament for what
is expected to be its last session, Premier Irvine
calmly appointed fat landlord Sachse an eighth
salaried Minister, thus ignoring all his previous
promises and pretensions of economy. At Nhill,
with snuffling hypocrisy, he openly warned the
careful Commonwealth Government against ex-
travagance. The ’Pootra Government’s finances
are a fake, its railway policy is causing a deader
loss every week, and now even its blessed economy
is shown to be so much bunkum. Eight salaried
ministers ! Peacock was thrown out for proposing
only six.
The United States runs its laws against
the importation of labor under contract in a way
that would raise the hair of the easy-going Barton
Government. The Chartered Bank of India,
Australia and China sent out an accountant under
engagement to its branch at Manila, and the
Yankee authorities ordered him out of the
Philippines as a contract laborer. A Greek clerk
imported by a Greek merchant was similarly sent
back from New York, and a preacher imported
from England to expound to a fashionable
American churchwasn’t allowed to land because of
the same law.

lost brother. Then he said he also knew of large
revenue evasions by another company, and there

Premier Jenkins, of South Australia, has
assumed a fighting attitude over the Federal
Arbitration Bill, and threatens, if the railway
servants are brought under it, to lease the rail-
ways. Some day a strong Federal Government
will remind people like Jenkins that they aren’t
such large pumpkins now as they imagine. Even
in such a matter as the leasing of State railways
the Australian Government can have a large say.
It has powers of taxation which can be applied to
burst up almost anything. An English railway
syndicate, for instance, might run against a
special income-tax on absentees, which would
make it wish it had never heard of J enkins.
Muecke (the’ German Consul) will appar-
ently have a pretty easy win for the Central
District S. A. Legislative Council seat. The Labor
_ party is running a direct candidate in Henry
Adams, and Dick Wood, an ex-Labor member, is
standing on his own. The full weight of the
Conservatives is behind the German gentleman
whose views are of the stereotyped Tory pattern.
He prudently prints but his initials on his
advertisements. The Adams party says that it
would have some hope if the full name “ Heinrich
Carl Emil Muecke ” was presented to the electors.
A popular impression that his surname is pro-
nounced “ Muck ” is being vigorously combatted.
George Reid's proposal to hold a referen-
dum on the fiscal question is as tricky as most of
his other doings. He proposes to ask the people
to vote either for the present tariff or for a purely
revenue one. He doesn’t propose to let the people
vote for a greatly increased tariff, but to so fix the
issue that a Protectionist victory is to be taken to-
mean that the present very inefficient tariff is. to
continue in all its inefficiency. And after preach-
ing Freetrade all these years as the one thing
which will make the country strong, rich, and
self-reliant, he doesn’t propose to even give the
people a chance to vote for that pure and noble
thing at all. It isn’t to be so mueli as mentioned.
New Chief Railway Commissioner Tait is
going the right way to get himself seriously
disliked in influential Melbourne
quarters. One of the side issues
attached to the policy of upsetting the
railway system, forcing on the strike;
and permanently spoiling the excel-
lent suburban service that formerly
existed, was the desire to introduce
electric traction, and especially to
make a start with it between St.
Kilda and Brighton. (Thomas Bent
acquired some property there a few
months ago.) But Tait got up the
other day and declared himself
strongly against tinkering with an
electric system, presumably out of
loan money, at a minimum cost of
over £2,000,000. Also he pointed out
that Tom Bent’s railway would serve
no legitimate purpose, merely estab-
lishing unhealthy competition with
another railway line.
Chamberlain to the British House of
Commons:
When I am told that our prosperity is bound up with
free imports, I ask, in the first place: What is our
prosperity? Is it a fact, as we are told on the high
authority of Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman, that twelve
millions of our people—more than a fourth of the whole
population—are always on the verge of starvation? Ia
that a proof of the blessings of free-trade ? Is it true
that many once profitable industries have disappeared,,
that the whole of the capital invested in them has been
lost, and that the work-people employed in them have
either gone to Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman’s twelve
millions or else have been forced to emigrate where they
are now finding employment in competition with the
comrades whom they have left at home ? I ask, in the
third place, is it, or is it not, a fact that our greatest ana
oldest industries—l would especially name the iron trade
and the textile industries—are threatened as they never
have been before ; that they might at any moment be
overwhelmed by a great importation of goods manufac-
tured abroad, sold here below cost, the product, mini
you, of protected States?

told the House that the pay named in the Bill
would not cover the cost of an imported article, so

which calls for remark j so the people are bearing

last of a third-rate lot of diplomatic hacks.

About a dead politician :
„ Duncan Gillies, ex-Premier of Victoria, ex-Agent
General, Speaker of the Assembly, and many other
thmgs, was ,-mnd dead in his bath at Parliament House
on Saturday. Gillies was 70 years of age, and had lived
on the political frame, m one way and another, for 44 years
His political career started in 1859, and it is 35 years
since he got his first portfolio in the Sladen Government
He sat in seven Ministries, and held the Premiership
for 4 years 8 months—the longest among Victorian
T?,!" llne > bar those of Turner and M'Culloch.
ftilHoa t h l h 0n X S ° od thln 8' to be recorded politically of
Gillies is that he once refused a knighthood. He was
without exception, the worst disaster that ever struck
Victona in a political sense. He was a Tory and a Free-
he W f - a ! y ’ indifferent, selfish, incompetent
and an opportunist; his finance was awful, his bor-
mefhnfl n? • lnsane > and bis utterly unscrupulous
“® tb ° d °/ b W*K support by scattering cockspur rail-
ways has loaded Victoria with a railway deficit that Will
T T nd ltS neck man y years. When Minister for
''' as unseated on the ground that votes had
n-fr P“ rcha ® ed b J’ wholesale land concessions
granted by Gilhes department to Gillies’ constituents dur-
ing a general election. The committee whitewashed Gillies
Dy saying that he wasn’t aware of what was going- on—
department and Gillies’ con-
stituents. The ‘Dictionary of Australian Biography"
recounts the offices he held, but doesn’t mention
one movement that he led, one measure that he
P. a38ed ’ footprint pointing in the right direc-
tion that he left on the political shore. There is
bad in Victorian political life
tnat Gillies didn t support, or originate, or help to
originate ; hardly anything good that he didn’t oppose.
Yet he remained plain Duncan Gillies to the end
which is something in his favor ; and, despite all his
political corruptness, he never was known to loot the cus-
toms or the public estate for the benefit of his own
pocket; nor were there any great scandals charged against
h ' m >u life. A certain dreary respectability hung
about Gillies like a shroud ; he had a considerable
power over his fellow men, and he had enough
courage to be a reformer if he had wanted to reform
anything And the fact that he didn’t found bogus
banks is to his credit. Gillies died poor, and perhaps
for that reason he drew his salary as Speaker
to the bitter end, though long past any work. Even
while he was dying on his feet the pathetic big hat
and the large, shiny, clean-shaven face, that always
looked as if it had been freshly washed with yellow soap
at the kitchen sink, faced Melbourne in public places It
was a strangely vacant face certainly, but it tried so hard
to I°ok like the old face. Gillies was always regarded as
a bachelor, but it now appears that he married when in
London six years ago. Apparently he never lived with
his wife, and the whole thing is a mj^stery.
Postmaster-General Fysh has stated, in a
loud tone of voice, his intention to prosecute the
numerous “embezzlers” brought under his
departmental notice. Unfortunately, the em-
bezzlements referred to by the P.M.G. are mere
shortages in accounts. The prosecution of 50
postal clerks on petty charges relating to- the
books will not stop the leakage of letters, which is
a pretty general cause of complaint in Victoria
and an outrageous scandal in the West. Thou-
sands of letters are destroyed, stolen, or miscar-
ried m the post ” every year, but, as far as the
public can learn, nothing is being done to im-
prove the character of the service.
The last public appearance of Duncan
bnllies wasgruesomely pathetic. It was a Tory job,
m the first place, to appoint Gillies as Stic deer,
and, a month after he was appointed, he had a
seizure which deprived him of speech. That was
in JNovember last. Ever since, he has practically
been dead, but the Government went on paying
j}.!® salary, and Chairman-of-Committees Beazley
did his work. In order to make a pretence of
a square thing, the Government allowed
Gillies to take the chair at the opening of Parlia-
ment. He strutted stiffly into the Chamber
and mounted his seat. \Vhen the messenger
*he Governor was announced, Gillies
Id not even remember the simple formula,
Admit him.” The messenger had to enter
the sacred place without so much as a chilly
invitation of that sort. Then, when members
began to hustle for positions on the notice paper,
Gillies merely stared ahead of him like an image.
Half-a-dozen members rose and shouted “Mr.
Speaker.” Gillies stared at first one and then
another and tapped the floor with his feet. The
babel continued, and Shiels rushed fo. Gillies and
besought him to leave the chair. Apparently
Gillies declined to do so just then. After the
sound of the call for “ Mr. Speaker ’’ had risen to a
roar, Beazley, Chairman of Committees, got to one
side of the chair and began to conduct the business
m something like order. Gillies and Shiels were
still nodding and bobbing to each other. Eventu-
ally Shiels took a grip on the old man on one side,
and with a gentle pressure by Beazley on the
other, Gillies was got out of the chair and Beazley
slid in.


New Vic. Railway boss Tait is treading
heavily on the corns of some of the Viotorian
cookies, who are always crying for more rain,
better roads, more taxes (on other people), more
trains, and more everything. A deputation
waited on him recently from Warragul, protest-
ing against the removal of the loco, depot from
that hub of the universe toTraralgon, and pointed
out that the depot had been at Warragul for
nearly twenty years. Tait, who looks like the
good boy in “Sandford and Merton” grown up,
told the “shareholders,” as they styled them
selves, that they were “darned ” lucky the depot
had been so long at their township, that it was
someone else’s turn now, and—that the door was
open, and he had a cold. The deputation did not
take its dismissal kindly, and expressed its sorrow
that Tait had ever been imported from the home
of the reaper and binder. No doubt Tait by this
time harbors a kindred grief.

almost instantly relieves the pain, and quickly
heals the injured parts. Sold everywhere at

Mention The Bulletin.
Wore a Torture
BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
SULPHURIG TIG ID.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the ,Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free Belt, which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs;
He paid £4 for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained World
Wide; fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen —In answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has‘troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various,
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had my attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, I thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £1 and was very much disappointed-
because I was informed that it was- neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug, treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt, with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
£4. I wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month. You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts.
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself in your
hands. I knew my case was very diffi-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a veiy
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Belt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has
Completely Cured Me, and strongly recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find are
suffering from the distressing malady
called “ Sciatica.”
I have not been wearing the Belt for the
past fortnight, but have passed it over to
my wife, who has received great benefit
for nervous troubles.
I remain, your grateful patient, ’
W. FOLLEY

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A JVC. Derby was a great boil over, and trainer
Tom Pay ten seems fated never to win a blue riband event
despite that he has occasionally got very close up. Duke
of Grafton’s manner of securing Rawson Stakes at
Randwick two weeks before (conceding 101 b. over w f a )
made him look such a good investment that odds of <5
and 7 to 4 on were readily laid until Mo tightened up and
asked twos. . The raucous voices dinned “8 tol Belah” into
the crowd’s ears incessantly without inducing much
business, and 7’s were obtainable about Victoria’s repre-
sentative, Sweet Nell. Belah practically led from jump-
off to judge’s box and Won comfortably, with Sweet Nell
second and the favorite a bad third. Only once did Duke
of Grafton threaten winning, and then at the distance
but he shot his bolt in a few strides. In recent Rawson
Stakes (lm. If.) Duke of Grafton and Belah met at a
difference of 101 b. in favor of latter, and former triumphed
With Belah far in the rear. Weights were equal in the
Derby. Belah, a well-shaped black colt, at once takes
the eye ; he was bred at the Mungie Bundie stud, near
Moree (N.S.W.) by owner, Mr. John M'Donald, and
springs from a family of stayers. He is by Havoc (son of
Musket and Goldsbrough mare Frailty) from Wilo- a by
Epigram from Nellie, by Tim Whiffler from Sappho
Belah’s successes include First Nursery Handicap (lm at
A.J.C. Autumn meet) and recent Lyndhurst Stakes (6if.)
at Rosehill after a hot finish with Marvel Loch and
Whiting. Interesting that it was winning jock’s (W. H.
Smith's) first mount in a Derby. Time 2.39 f has been
beaten—particularly by Hautvilliers’ 2.37*.
* * * *
, Battle between M.L. galloping machine, Cruci-
form, and Australia’s champion, Wakeful, in Spring
Stakes'(l*m.), Saturday, was most exciting duel ever seen
at A.J.C. headquarters, mainly because of unquestionably
representative credentials of the two nags. The Victory
and Postillion were others engaged. Only two first
n'atiied weri thought of in a winning connection, and
though here and there The Victory found support at 100
to 10, backers laid 7 to 4 on Wakeful, and accepted twos,
and occasionally 9 to-4, Cruciform. For verging on a
mile the Maorilander made her own running (a big tax),
then Wakeful shot up inside and drew level, and the pair
moved away from Postillion, travelling at a clink-
ing pace. Bracketed head and head, the two wonders
streaked down the straight, till finally, with excitement at
snapping point, Cruciform’s head was observed clear of
the opposing mare’s, and then a great roar arose when lit
was seen she held her advantage. Away they sped to the
winning-post, riders and nags having every ounce up aiid
straining every nerve till they flashed past the judge
with the yejlow-and-black liverv -of M.L.’s great sport,
Mr. Stead, a short head to the good. Randwick
has seldom known such a burst of cheering (re-
peated thrice over) as greeted the result, the ring of
which was all the more genuine seeing that the favorite
went under. Though Postillion bumped Wakeful at one
stage the.occurrence made no difference, for the pace was
slow then and the mare soon regained her position ; but
-over the last 4*fur. the struggle was something that will
never be effaced from the memories of those who
Witnessed it. Despite that M.L. jockey Hewitt (up on
Cruciform) lost something by letting Wakeful have the
rails, he made up for it in superb horsemanship at the
critical juncture ; his Tod Sloan ” riding stood to him
well. Postillion finished two lengths behind the leading
pair and four lengths in front of The Victory, thus
making his Melbourne Cup prospects look particularly
sweet.
* * * *
A.J.C. big Spring mile (the Epsom) saw 17 line
up, as against 11 more last year. Only elimination on
the course was St. Modan, who had just previously
secured Trial Handicap. Winner turned up in favorite
Fanjous (9 to 2 against), who scored by . a neck
from third choice (Faithful) after a ding-dong, high-
tension tussle down the straight, and then only
through his jockey, Frank Fielder, being stronger
than the other (Bradshaw). Fielder .fairly lifted
his mount past th‘e post at the end, and never showed
better than in this struggle. Famous is a four-year-old,
trained and owned by Sam Fielder, and is by Grafton
from Vanity by Marvellous, but there his pedigree is lost.
At two years a Rosehill Nursery Handicap fell to him,
then came a similar event at Moorefield, and a Flying
Welter at Rosehill. Late last season Famous annexed
the Welter Plate at Sandown (Vic,.), and, since coming
back, appropriated Flying Handicap at Warwick Farm (a
lucky win from Lord Cardigan), and July Stakes at Rose-
hill. Only for the doubt that Famous might be in one of
his bad moods—he is admit tedly unreliable that way—
Mo would have had to unload still more largely.
Opening of A.J.C. Spring meet saw a Derby
Day record attendance of 28,000 at Randwick. Previous
biggest was last year’s 25,000. Results were, on the
whole, disastrous for backers, whereat there was much
joy in Israel. Only twice did favorites succeed. Gatherer
(St. Modan) of Trial Stakes was “any price”—nominally
15 and 20 to 1. Eights were freely offered against Derby
Winner Belah. Prices of three placed nags in Kensington
Handicap were at least 20 to 1 each, and, while 9 to 4 was
available about Maorilander Cruciform’s chance in Spring
Handicap, punters laid 7 to 4 on opposing Wakeful freely.
* * • •'
One (Tatterdemalion) of the three already
weightily invested upon Caulfield Cup favorites was
served up the choicest possible thing for Trial Stakes, at
Randwick, Saturday, and heaps of public money went on
at 6 to 4 (which was about the best price available at any
time up to flag-fall) but he never got within dozens of
lengths of the lead at any stage, and ultimately tailed off
to nowhere towards the finish. Rarely has a heavily sup-
ported nag made such a poor showing. In striking con-
trast the big outsider(St. Modan) of the field, about whom
Mo. almost let the backer write his own odds, led from
start to finish and won easily.
* * * »
Last year final acceptances for A.J.C. Metrop.
totalled 27, this time they were 26, and all sported silk on
Monday. Persistent support and weight of money sent
Faithful out hot at fours, though in places no better than
threes could be had. Differently owned stable chums—
T.M.S. and Famous—were next in request at B’s and 10’s,
as named, while at least a dozen others were heavily
backed, notably Nothos (upon whom a big “commish”
was placed at card-call previous Saturday night, and at
the course early Monday), St. Modan, Cyanide, and
Highflyer. Minister for Lands Crick backed his horse
Ossian to win a goodly pile of thousands, and about
twice as much would have come to a well-known ex-
politician and solicitor (who races as “Mr. T. Marvel”)
had T.M.S. proved successful. Winner turned up in Mar-
vel Loch, who beat T.M.S. by a length, with Ossian a head
behind, third. Though among the outside section, Marvel
Loch was well enough supported to leave many of the
Noses, who had laid hundreds to two and hundreds to
four against, lamenting. At Tatts.’ rooms morning of
race 500 to 5 went begging because the offer made it
appear that the mare wouldn’t be a starter, or had broken
down. One prominent Sydney punter gathered £2OOO
through the result, and his backing of the nag was
prompted by the fact that Marvel Loch got away last in
the Epsom, ran right round her field, yet made up lost
ground and finished fifth. Air Motor was verging on up-
sides with third (Ossian) in the race, and close against
her were Nothos (who should win ere the meeting closes),
Sweet Nell, and Highflyer. Favorite Faithful was never
prominent at any stage, and Cyanide seemed to be buried
in the ruck all the trip. Time (2.3s).equals the l*m. Aus-
tralian record previously shared in by Survivor, Fairy
Prince, San Fran and Patronus. Absolute best for the
continent is M.L. nag. Advance’s 2.34 1-5. In America
Goodrich covered the distance in 2.30 J. Avidity has the
English top figures—2.3o 4-5.
* * * *
Metrop. surprise, Marvel Loch, is by Lochiel
from Marvelette, and consequently well named. She was
bred by Mr. John M'Master in Liverpool Plains district.
Her dam was by Marvellous (imp.) from Contessa, by
Gemma di Vergy (imp.) Marvel Loch (at 3yrs.) scored
her first win, annexing a Park Stakes at Canterbury last
year. Subsequently she ran second to erratic Santoi in a
Moorefield sprint; then Rosehill Railway Stakes fell to
her. Doncaster Handicap saw the mare third to Rose
Petal and Ban Ceontha and, through getting a bad run,
she was beaten by Ambulance in the A.J.C. Flying
Handicap. This season Marvel Loch finished unplaced to
T.M.S. in a 6fur. handicap at Rosehill, and two weeks
afterwards went under narrowly to Derby winner Belah.
Her next appearance supplied evidence of material
improvement—-she got away with Tramway Handicap, at
Tatt’s. August meet. Marvel Loch’s Metrop. success
means a 141bs. penalty for Caulfield Cup, making her
load 8.4.
* * » *
“ Punter ”: Concerning Marvel Loch’s Metrop.
scoop. It did seem strange that stable-mate Faithful
should start a hot pot at 3’s, while t’other was among the
very outside lot, especially as trainer Scully thought the
pair so equal that he took £lOOO to £5O about each for the
Epsom. Surely Ikey Mo must be the favored of Fortune,
for, had second or third horse been in Marvel
Loch’s position, the diamond bespattered’s store of
shekels would have been largely depleted, particularly if
T.M.S. got home. As it is, not one of the big double
books has been struck, though some shops will have to
pay out, for Marvel Loch was occasionally coupled with
Famous, because everything else had been taken. Mares
haven’t been over successful in the Metrop., though races
of 1899,1902 and 1903 have gone to the sex.
* * » *
A.J.C. First Steeple, Monday, induced nine
starters, and though five fell, race was interesting
throughout and run at a speedy bat. Second lap brought
Kaffir and Victorian Wongleburnie down, and further on,
Moravian’s rider was dislodged, while last obstacle (the
logs) proved disastrous to prospects of very tired V.R.C.
Grand National winner Grafnell, who started a warm
favorite, and Queenslander Ringwood, whose neck was
broken. Just here the money looked sure for Blackthorn,
who had been flying his jumps well and making all the
running, but on the flat that consistently fine performer,
Brokerage, smothered him without an effort and won
easily from Grafter. Grafnell’s cropper shook nag and
rider badly, and Ringwood’s jock, was unconscious for
some time.
* » * *
Trainer Fielder’s luck in the Metrop.: Four
years ago his Cranberry was second to Cremona ; last
year The Persian filled a similar position when Queen of
Sheba won ; and this time T.M.S. followed suit.
* * ♦ *
Say the owner’s name was Jilkins and the name
of the fashionable jockey who had just weighed out to
ride his nag was Bilkins. Scene, Caulfield racecourse.
Two or three friends of Jilkins are anxiously appealing to
him for information. Is he going to back his horse ?
Jilkins scratches his head in a troubled way, and replies,
“Back my horse? I can’t tell you until I see what
Bilkins is doing.” That about explains the position of
the average owner to-day. He’s afraid to back his nag
until he has assured himself that the jockey is not backing
something else
* * • *
Only two men have ridden three S. A.J.C.
winners—Simpson, who won the first three (’6O, ’6l, and
’62), and W. H. Ross, who won the last two and that of
1900. Apropos, Wyman, South Australia’s most brilliant
rider of 25 years back, who rode the winner twice in suc-
cession and had bad luck in losing the race the third year,
is now, or was a few months back, caretaker of aMethody
church a mile or two from where his old stables were.
* * » *
Whilom great ’chaser Domino qualified for
first prize in heavyweight hunter’s competition at Vic-
torian Royal Agricultural Show, day or two since. His
lepping was capital, no task troubled him the least bit.
The old grey’s hair has whitened some since he humped
13st. over Caulfield’s four-mile steeple course four years
ago, and won easily, but he looks as well as ever, and
wears an appearance which suggests a possibility of
recovering his best form, provided a thorough prepara-
tion be gone through. Pity that more wasn’t seen of
him. When withdrawn from racing he was sound as a
bell, and everything indicated that much bigger things
were within his scope.
* * 3|( *
The enterprising business man again. A well-
known Sydney city publican pumped beer incessantly for
weeks before Saturday last, through offering every pur-
chaser of a drink at his bar £2O to nothing about picking
the treble—A.J.C. Derby, Epsom, and Metrop.—with the
understanding that if there was more than one successful
guess the money should be whacked among the lot. This
scribe hasn’t been able to find out exactly how many
named those two outsiders for the Derby and Metrop. on
their cards, but has been informed that they totalled
considerably more than a few.
* * * *
‘ ‘ Gun ” : Know one or two people who backed
T.M.S. because those were the initial letters of the name
of the event—The Metropolitan Stakes. Had T.M.S. got
home the ranks of believers in “the office” about the
result of a race would have had several fresh recruits
* * * *
Given out at time of “black’’Bill Forrester’s
death that that fine old son (The Chief) of now defunct
Gozo would be pensioned off for the term of “ his
natural ” at Tucka Tucka, but he’s in training again,
and was among the “ other starters ” in recent Tatt’s.
Spring Handicap, looking as well as ever. It should be
no surprise if he pulls off something big ere long
* * * »
Champion trotter Ribbonwood was brought up
on the milk of the cow, and • his full sister, now nine
months old, is being similarly treated. The young lady’s
drink bill runs to 7s. 6d. a week.
* » * *
First-class entries have been received for the
well-endowed classic events of the Canterbury (M.L.)
Jockey Club for 1904-5-6, a large number of finely-bred
youngsters figuring in the list. Messrs. F. Reynolds,
J. Thompson and T. Payten (N.S.W.) have cast their eyes
across the Tasman sea, and are represented by eight,
sired by Lochiel, Grafton and Medallion respectively.
* . » « •
After vainly spending a sack of money at law in
endeavoring to keep the pencilling fraternity off Riccarton
(M.L.)racecourse (it being ruled to be a public reserve), the
C.J.C. lately went for the tote-odds crowd baldheaded
under the Police Offences Act. As they had contravened
a brand new regulation relating to the conditions of
admission, they were fined. There have been over a hun-
dred convictions for “ trespassing” on private racecourses
in same district, and latterly the fines have been a fiver,
which practically meant that they paid that much in the
shape of a license to bet. A court warning that gaol
without the option will be the punishment to fit the crime
in future has somewhat dampened Mo.’s ardor.
* * * •
There is one place where, for the time, Mo. and
the tote do thrive side by side, and that place is the
Westralian goldfields. Yet, though the tote certainly
makes Mo. more liberal than he would otherwise be,
Mo.’s prices can’t, as a rule, come within cooee of the
tote’s. The biggest difference is when an outsider gets
in. Best books’ price about Brayleigh, who won the big
Boulder Steeplechase money, was 7 to 1; the tote paid
£lllos.
* • * •
Not before it is time, V.R.C. may shortly insist
upon some reforms in connection with Melbourne subur-
ban racing. Increase of stake money to £3OO or £350 is
talked of, and it is also hinted that some of the mid-week
racing will be dropped. Horsey Melbourne will applaud
the step, which is sure to make for the advancement of
the game.
♦ » * *
Inter-State Stock Exchange walk, on the Ade-
laide Oval last Wednesday, may serve to put the finishing
touch to that particular kind of sport. Sparling, of Mel-
bourne, finished the 12 miles in 43 seconds under two
hours, on a close-clipped grass track —three to the
mile. There were very few on the ground who did
not credit him with running occasionally. Anyway,
his was an artificial sort of gait, and of no practical
use. Alfred Roberts, the old footballer and present
tennis expert, finished half a minute behind, W. J. P.
Davies and Twiss being over a lap in the rear. These three,
all Adelaide men, won the match for their side easily.
All three walked with conspicuous fairness, as did North,
the only other Melbourne man to finish. An “open”
walk followed, in w’hich competitors went practically as
they pleased, and Stack (of the Register office) rattled
off eight miles in lh. 3m. 15s. Several running events, for
brokers only, were chiefly noticeable for the fact that Clem
Hill won the hurdles and was second in the hundred ; but
it is hoped they may lead to a revival of amateur athletics,
which have been dead in S.A. for years past.
* * * *
Recently the Sydney manager of one of the
biggest Australasian steamship companies was asked to
delay the departure of a 2000-ton steamer by some hours,
because a suburban golf champion was a passenger, and
if the vessel wasn’t delayed his club would have to do its
best to lick another suburban club without his assistance.
These golf people do take themselves seriously.
» * * *
The 24 hours’ walking championship of S.A.,
• recently decided, showed good men going “in all
shapes,” in accordance with the old proverb. Ultimate
winner Swan (110 m. 568 yards) led for the last 12 hours,
but Young was never a mile behind him. Swan is a
little, nuggety veteran of 50 or thereabouts, Young a
long slim boy of barely 20, out of a draper’s shop 1 Two
others covered over 90 miles in the time, and all would
probably have done better on the grass where the race
started ; but heavy rains drove them indoors to a small
ring and a board floor. The 24 hours were broken up
thus : 2 p.m. to midnight on Friday, 6 a.m. to 2 p.m. and
4 to 10 p.m. on Saturday.
* * * *
The baseball premiership of N.S.W. has ended
in an unexpected win for Paddington. This club and the
Leichhardt team divide all the limelight in the somewhat
restricted local baseball world. Each had been defeated
once, and both sides were anxious to play off for the
premiership. Leichhardt had but one game to play, that
against Woollahra, a weak team. So it was proposed to
Woollahra that it should forfeit the match and let the
giants battle for the first place ; but Woollahra was
anxious for a practice against the invincible Leichhardt
team, and insisted on its right of being eaten up by
the cracks. They met accordingly, but the eating up
was done by Woollahra, which defeated the Leichhardt
team with painful ease. So Paddington wins the com-
petition.
* * » *
N.S.W. pigeon slaying championship this year
“ attracted ” 28 men, who subscribed among them £l4O
for the stakes. Before the prize was awarded they killed
amongst them 820 birds, without counting those which
fell dead or mutilated “ out of bounds.” A few dozen of
the hapless pigeons got away ; but few of them escaped
sound, the majority being “ feathered,” “ tattered,” or
“ plucked ” in the vernacular of the sport, to become the
spoil of the watchers outside the enclosure. The sure
fate of nearly every pigeon raised is the trap of the
shooting club, and the reading of a newspaper report of a
champion shooting event is harrowing to the feelings of
the private individual who has had his pretty pets stolen.
The pigeon thief can always find a safe and sure market.
* * * *
Maoriland Amateur Boxing Association cham-
pionships, decided at Christchurch August 27, 28 and 29,
proved to be fiery comets with flaming tails of gore.
Maoriland has long stigmatised bruising exhibitions as
brutal; but when it was called boxing everyone opined
’twas “ a sport for gentlemen,” and fell over themselves
in their eagerness to get a good seat. Recent champion-
ships were devoid of science, being mostly desperate
encounters between wharf-lumpers and carters. H.
Taylor (Wellington) slogged his way to victory in the
heavy-weights, leaving behind him a wake of mangled
opponent. J. M. Griffin (Invercargill), by mere bruising,
annexed the middle-weight medal and a special cup.
When he got a Mow home it usually lifted his man out
into the audience, and the ambulance dashed in. The
light-weights went to A. Farquharson, and the feather-
weights to A. Parker, both of Dunedin. The various
battles extended over three nights, and drew big houses.
* * ♦ *
Bert Murphy, fourth and youngest of M.L.
fighting family to which once world’s champion feather,
“ Torpedo ” Billy, and sometime Australian premier
middleweight Tim belong, and of which long since
dead Jimmy (S’African lightweight champion) was a
member, had his first real battle at Sydney Golden
Gate A.C. Saturday night. Opposed to one “ Robbo,” a
tough ’un from Toughville, who began by appropriating a
9st. Novice tourney a few months back, and has won
six times since without meeting defeat, Murphy made
a gritty showing, and occasionally looked a winner.
Robbo carried too many guns (though not by much) and
won in the sixth round. Beaten man showed enough to
warrant the opinion that he’ll get to the top yet, for all
the material is there, it only needs training and develop-
ing. Battle bristled with incident, despite that it was
the loser’s first effort in anything like good company.
The bruise wherein Irish cockney Denny
Moloney and clever up-country pug, “ Ted ” Nelson,
were engaged at Sydney National S.O. Monday night,
•filled the house and was vigorous, albeit devoid of inci-
dent and occasionally wearisome because of manner in
which cleverness of Nelson baffled superior force and un-
questionable grit of t’other. Nelson accumulated all the
points in every round. Now and again Moloney scored
but not effectively. Nelson slipped a lot, while 'Moloney
was sent to his knees once with a punch on the ear.
Ultimately the 16th rounp saw Moloney so utterly used-
up that referee M‘Donald declared Nelson the winner.
• • • •
Fight ’tween West Indian black, “Cakewalker ”
Savral, and Victorian feather, Silver, at Queen’s Hall,
Melbourne, last week resulted sensationally. Negro
looked a certainty on performances, and odds of 2 and 3
to 1 on his chance were freely offered but rarely taken.
Pace was furious from moment they shaped. Exchanges
were heavy, with white most in evidence till near bell
sound for the first 3mins. when black copped a frame-
shaking right on the point that made him oscillate and
sag. Another immediately afterwards sent him to
the boards quivering like a chocolate jelly; he rose groggy
to be floored again, and was saved by the gong. The
minute’s spell was not long enough for recuperation, and
Savral moved to the centre, still dazed, to meet a para-
lysing uppercut, which so palpably and decisively beat
him that the referee intervened and gave Silver the
money. Melbourne hasn’t seen a more complete stag-
gerer, for the black was thought almost invincible at his
weight—probably £st. in excess of the other chap’s,
while Silver wasn’t rated near a top-notcher. Savral has
since been suing for another test, alleging he was caught
napping. All the same, Silver will probably win again if
they come together.
» * * *
Harold Smith-“ Psycho ” (unique in pug.
nomenclature) Hansen bruise at Sydney National 5.0.,
last week, was exciting, but not nearly so much as Regan-
Wilson curtain raiser. Each pounded with remarkable vim
and effect, and never expended a punch fruitlessly till the
referee stopped the rough and gave the big end of the
purse to Wilson. Smith, a promising pupil of retired
middleweight premier Mick Dunn, surprised Hansen a
heap, and, after the initial round, punched home a
weighty left frequently. Hansen was busy, too, but not
to the same extent as Smith, and things were lively till
the end of the fifth round, when the towel from Hansen’s
corner indicated he had had enough, and the crowd
cheered both, particularly Smith, who’ll do well against a
better man.

* * * *
The combined Cricket Association’s Union for
nurturing and advancing junior cricket is the latest
Sydney development. Five strongest bodies outside
N.S.W.O.A. have joined forces and represent 2300 players.
Inter-Association matches will be a feature ofthe new
order of things, which ’tis hoped may make materially for
the advancement of the second-rate section.
* * * 3g
The first three bowlers to take 100 wickets in
the present English season were Barnes (who is at last
showing something of his Australian form), Hargreave,
and Rhodes. They all reached three figures within a few
hours of each other. Hargreave shows considerably the
best average. Relf (coming out with Warner) and Albert
Trott each seem, by figures (and in batting too), to be
practically the same value on a side as Barnes, but
had not done so much work. J. T. Hearne, who has
suddenly come back to his best form, was second only to
Hirst in the averages, and it was evidently he, with help
from Bosanquet and Trott, who won the premiership
for Middlesex. Hirst, Bosanquet and John Gunri are
easily the best all-round men in England this year.
Q.-N.S.W. football matches in Brisbane, last
week, weren’t over-exciting and never high-class.
Scores of first contest were 11 to 3 in favor of N.S.W.
Visitors’second engagement was against a Brisbane 15,
and, though play on both sides improved much,;it was
nothing to rave about. Tussle went to the Cornstalks,
who accumulated 15 to t’other’s 7. Third rough (Satur-
day last) was hot from end to end, and game iooked
either side’s till five minutes before time, when two
inexcusable transgressions saw home team penalised, and
opposition scored goals through each, making full totals
12 —5, with N.S.W. best. Wickham gathered every point
for the winners. Operations were very even throughout.
Bananalanders shone at loose play, but scrum-work and
passing of the others was surer and cleaner.

Of the 34 Vic. football Premierships gathered
in since 1870, Geelong has collared 7, Carlton and
Essendon 6 each, South 5, Melbourne 4, Fitzroy 3 and
Collingwood 3— but Collingwood only came into existenco
in 1894.
* » * *
“ Harrier ” : Concerning alleged cross-country
championship of N.S.W. decided t’other day. Where
does “cross country” come in? They run round Ken-
sington race track and jump hurdles. Only a few times
in its history has the contest been what it professes, and
what it should be. Then the spectacle of the thing ! A
long tail early and two or three men (two on latest occa-
sion) shooting away from the others as if they were stand-
ing. Many of those who struggled the 5 miles out were
lapped, which means they were, in some instances, a mile
behind. Result was a repetition of last year’s race, and
apparently it will always be the same until “Wall}'”
York or Smith, or both, depart for fresh scenes or get too
old for the game. There’s no one in sight with anything
like the stamina of the pair named, and theirs is not
remarkable.
• • * *
Died, t’other day, once forefront Victorian oars-
man Sam Gowdie, from pneumonia supervening upon
influenza. He rowed with Vic. crew in inter-State eight-
oar race in ’B5, ’B6, and ’B7, and in ’BB, when there were
two races, he was stroke each time. Peculiar how Gowdie
came to associate himself with rowing ; he began helping
a brother, who had been recommended rowing for a weak
constitution, and then stuck to it.
* » * *
When the Australian rifle team that went to
Bisley last year got back it wasn’t safe to mention the
commanding officer’s name to some of them ; and this
year’s lot seem to be more furious still. A first consign-
ment of the men, who have come back by the Omrah,
promise trouble. They say they weren’t coached or
diligently captained, and that it was by sheer, good luck
that they came out even as well as they did.

Thirty-seven entered for Victorian golf
supremacy last week, but they didn’t all materialise.
W. J. 0. Riddell, who succeeded two years ago, survived
with an aggregate of 323—5 better than that of H.
Mac Neill, the immediately previous winner. Fierce
wind all three days put brilliancy out of the question
altogether, and marred play considerably.

Members of the N.S.W. lady deputation,
which recently tried without result to effect the
release of Ethel Herringe, at present in prison for
having mortally wounded the man who refused to
marry her, are now claiming through the press the
right of woman to shoot her seducer. There is a
good deal to be said against the proposal, though
it must be admitted that it would effectually
settle the problem of what to do with our boys.

Swagman Jones, the crank committed for
trial for the murder at Manila (N.S.W.), is
another instance of the evil effects of Gospelism.
What time he was toying with a tomahawk he
wrote to a friend—or rather got a frightened matron
to write it for him—“I am now about to make a
great sacrifice for the dear Lord. After that I
will get two shillings.” When a police sergeant
came to arrest him he fired a gun at the unwel-
come officer, exclaiming “Christ’s will must be
done, ’ and after his arrest he regretted that he
hadn’t made some more sacrifices for the Lord.
Looks as though Jones was suffering from the
influence of the Salvation Harmy war cries—
Blood and Fire. A Harmy captain in a red
guernsey preaching Blood and Fire to a cranky
Jones ought to be treated as a punishable offence.
Mr. JACOB G. REBUS, 112 Brodway, New

BROUGHT RELIEF AT ONCE.”*
The most important feature of B. R. Wise’s
Habitual Criminals Bill is the power it gives to
the Governor-in-CoUncil to set free a convicted
prisoner. The Gpvernor-in-Council, presumably
in most cases the Minister for Justice, will act on
the report of the prison authorities, and thus,
unless strict safeguards are set up, the wrong
criminal may be frequently set free. Governors
enjoyed a power like this in the early days, and
the records are stained with evil applications of it.
A man, four times convicted of one of the serious
offences, is taken by the Bill out of the hands of
the court and given over to gaol officials who, if
incompetent or corrupt, may hold him a convict
for ever, or recommend release where it should not
be granted.
Few man have a better knowledge of the
working of the Lands Acts than has Land Agent
N. Willis of 21 Bligh-street. Payment by
results is not only reasonable but also novel.*

SPECIALITIES
. The abandonment of the Federal Arbitra-
tion Bill has caused the Federated Seamen’s
Union to contemplate taking a test case to the
High Court to determine whether crews of foreign
ships, not paid Australian rate of wages, are not
frohibited persons within the meaning of the
immigration Restriction Act, and, as such, liable
to exclusion from Commonwealth waters. Union
relies upon sub-section G. of clause III., which
places among prohibited persons :
Any person under a contract or agreement to perform
manual labor within the Commonwealth, provided that
this paragraph shall not apply to workmen exempted by
the Minister for special skill required in Australia, or to
persons under control or agreement to serve as part of
the crew of a vessel engaged in the coasting trade in
Australian waters, if the rate of ivages specified therein
he not lower than the rates ruling in the Commonwealth.
No one interested in art or in Australian
history can afford to miss inspecting to-day the
collection of Phil May’s Bulletin drawings at
324 George-street. These will be dispersed at the
sale to-morrow, Friday; and in all probability
such a fine series will never be brought together
again.
Melbourne Australasian offers its disas-
trous support to Bigjohn Forrest as first Federal
High Commissioner in England :
There can be no doubt that Mr. John Forrest should
have the High Commissionership. He is an ardent
Imperialist, whose every effort would he devoted towards
strengthening the ties between the Motherland and the
Commonwealth. He is a man of wealth, and he has
behind him a career of conspicuous service in several
directions, which entitles him to the goodwill of his
native land, and to the respect and trust of those who
love the empire.
That testimonial, coming from such a quarter,
should lead to the hanging-up of John for further
consideration, and especially for further details.
What does the Australasian mean by “ an ardent
Imperialist”? The Bulletin is an ardent Im-
perialist—it is so anxious for the good of that part
of the empire called Australia, that it would
cheerfully see Chamberlain executed if Australia
would benefit thereby. John Forrest is also an
ardent Imperialist it seems—but which part of
the empire is he ardent about ?
Bent s latest notion is more than comic.
He proposes to help the Victorian fanner to build
his roads by selling a liarrow strip along each side
of existing 2 and 3-chain roads, and reckons to get
about £10 an acre for the strips. It looks simple
and pretty —like Thomas himself. Also, it works
out just as absurdly. Take a farmer with about a
thousand yards of frontage to a main road. This
man is to be offered a strip, five yards wide, along
this frontage—which will make just an acre—and
for this he is to pay £10, and then it will cost him
from £25 to £50 to remove his fence or hedge.
Certainly, he can graze the shorthorn horse and
the bob-tailed cow thereon without going to this
expense, but, as he does that at present for
nothing, where is the use of paying £40 or £60
for it?

Vic. Labor-member Anstey, in a recent
speech at Maryborough (Vic.), said some things
which were carefully not reported in the Mel-
bourne dailies. Extract from report in Mary-
borough Standard:—
Take the M'Gulloch Carrying Company, which practi-
cally belonged to Mr. M'Culloch, M.L.C., and its trans-
actions with the State. A farmer lived, say, between two
lines of railway. The M’CuIloch company went to the
authorities and asked for concessions if it brought that
farmer’s trade to the railway—where else would it go?
Anyhow, the concession was granted, and last year the
company had had £30,000 concessions. Was that business-
like ? A Government which had any business fn il would
have gone straight to the farmer and given him the con-
cession if there was to be any. But, no, the farmer could
pay, and the M‘Culloch company get rich for “ bringing
business. ” There was a syndicate holding Crown lands
known as the Ballarat East Concession, which was com-
posed of two brothers. What did they do ? Held leases,
allowed other people to work them, and drew fat royalties
off Crown lands. That was a lovely state of affairs. This 1
had been going on for years. From one mine alone they I
have had £16,000 in royalty for the right to work ground
which belongs to the Crown. The leases fell in last'
December, and the Irvine Government showed how!
genuine it was in its desire to economise that it renewed

“ Ne’er-do-Weel ” on an undertaker :
I once saw the champion mean man of the district
taken down. He was an undertaker, and though there
is a certain amount of excuse for a man proceeding some
lengths in the endeavor to make business these hard
times, he went altogether too far. I was in a bush hos-
pital. There were only two others in the same ward as
myself, and one of these was a former sergeant of the
R.1.C., the wreck of a once magnificent man, who had
stood fully 6Jft. in his stockings, now dying of consump-
tion. The undertaker, who was a member of the hospital
committee, spotted this man, and scraped acquaintance
with him. On every visit, he asked the wardsman or
matron how O’Hara was, and was always told the end
was very near. At last, he was told O’Hara would die
within the week. He went in and sat down by the,
patient’s bed, and was bending over talking to him
when the glint of brass caught my eye. The ghoul
had his foot-rule in his hand, and was stealthily
measuring the lengthy patient. I sent a note
to the doctor, and he fell on the undertaker. Latter’s
reply wa§ that O’Hara was sure to die, and there
Would be some difficulty' in fitting a man of his length
with a coffin, and he wanted to be prepared for the job.
He prepared accordingly. A day or two later O’Hara
took a turn for the better, and his friends took him down
to the seaside, where he promptly passed away. Then
the maddest undertaker in Christendom was left with a
uselessly long coffin on his hands, and he filled the town
with his cries and lamentations.

Concerning the Site.
The casual scoff of Ernest Eavenc :—
The real trouble concerning the much-vexed question
of the site of the Federal Capital appears to be the diffi-
culty of finding a spot where nature has preeminently
combined all her favors of climate, scenery, soil, water,
and all other essentials. Of course, the idea of our
Federal legislators meeting in a place devoid of all these
pleasant attributes would be intolerable. Therefore, the
general notion is that the most suitable site is the locality
which nature has most lavishly endowed. That is the
popular way of looking at it. Another way is from a
colossally rugged standpoint of pure patriotism and com-
monsense—not the conventional legislative patriotism
that modestly accepts a snug billet and perks, but the
true patriotism that is ready to undergo trouble and dis-
comfort for a national cause. It is an undisputed asser-
tion that the building of the Federal capital will greatly
benefit the district lucky enough to secure its presence ;
but why benefit a district already highly favored
by nature ? Why not benefit a district that
wants artificial benefits bestowed on it; that wants
man to populate it, and devote all his energy to improve
it and make it productive and prosperous? How could it
be better done than by putting the Federal capital there?
All the present rival sites come forward with their special
offers of great natural endowments ; why should not one
site come forward without any such pleas? “ I am only
a poor forlorn site, at the back of nowhere. I possess
neither trade nor railway, and have no inducements to
offer in the shape of climate, soil or scenery. But if the
Federal capital is built here I have any amount of
unalienated land to accommodate it. 1 shall soon have
railway communication with all the States, and rapidly
become a flourishing centre of population, and the country
all around me would benefit with me.”
To this, a patriotic Government would answer : “ Most
certainly, it is the duty of a national Government to help
on and populate a district which nature has done little
for. Never mind the climate or the soil, we will stand all
that—* it is our duty, and we will ! ’ ”
It might strike many people that building a city in a
poorly-populated part with no natural advantages, would
be the height of folly; but that is because such people
have no originality. In fact, the Australian atmosphere
is dead against originality. Look at our nomenclature !
The only original names we have are borrowed from the
Dutch ; and a few that we fbndly look upon as aboriginal.
But let us cite an example : Let us, in fancy, build the
capital at Milparinka, a distant township in the far North-
west, which about, to put it vulgarly, fills the bill. It is
by no means unhealthy, although the climate cannot he
he'd forth as a model one. What would he the result of
putting the capital there ? A railway line would be con-
structed, and important.townships would spring up along
its course. The howling desert, ms the west of the
Darling is considered, would blossom like the prover-
bial rose, and the Paroo and Cobham Lake become
known to the world outside of this continent. In fact,
it would alter the entire conditions of the far Western
country, for all the inventive talent of Australia would
be called on to fight the rigors of nature In that part. In
reality it would add a new province to the continent.
How much better this would be than giving a fillip
to Bombala or Orange. Regarded from a national
Austra'ian standpoint, a glance at the continental
maps will convince every unprejudiced person that
the finger of fate points directly at Milparinka as
the site of the future Federal capital. Not only are
there no vested interests in huge private estates to take
over, but the nature of the country would allow of the
creation of a much larger Federal territory than has been
proposed. Let us then put an end to all rivalry and
jealousy and determine upon Milparinka as the site, and
start the capital on a wood and iron basis until we are
clear of some of our debt. Our legislators may not enjoy
it at first, but it is astonishing how fond one gets of the
far west, when one is used to it.
Members of Parliament, both in the States and
Commonwealth, boast of the personal friendliness
that is becoming more and more a characteristic
of their hearing towards one another outside Par-
liament walls. The boast, when their habits of
speech inside the walls of Parliament are con-
sidered, really demonstrates their political and
moral decadence. If members on the floor of the
House upbraid opponents in disgraceful terms and
embrace them out of doors, there is utter want of
sincerity either in the speech or in the embrace.
G. H. Reid, in addressing the House on the elec-
toral distributions, charged the Government with,
among a lot of similar language, “unparalleled
treachery,” “a fraud upon the Constitution,”
being “inspired clearly by motivesof self-interest,”
“ a combination to secure unworthy ends,” and so
on. If words carry a meaning, there is enough in
those quoted to create between honorable men a
gulf that nothing could bridge. But only a few
weeks before, and even since, the same Reid
assured gatherings that his esteem for members of
the Government, as “men of honor and dis-
tinguished abilities,” is lofty and sincere. The
difference between G. H. Reid in bis screaming
vituperative flights and the same man in his
periods of fawning personal adulation is so great
that his statements can’t both be true. Either
the accusatory bravado style in Parliament should
be put down or the bravo’s cringing adulation
when he accosts the “unparalleled traitor” as a
friend outside the House should be suppressed.
“ Woodsey ” writes :—
I ran against the Salvation Army curse again the other
night—one is always running against the curse of that
loathsome, red-shirted, hook-nosed fraud and hypo aisv.
It was a woman following the oldest profession in the
world, and I dug out her story. It was very short.
There were five orphaned sisters with a speck of money
among them, and they started a laundry. It got on ail
right till “ William Booth, trading as the Salvation
Army ” started a laundry also in the same district.
William Booth didn’t require to pay his hands any wages ;
his laundry was Worked by fallen women .whom the red-
shirted show Lad “rescued ” and their sole remuneration
Was victuals, prayer and tracts. William Booth didn’t
need to feed his girls ; victuals were collected from the
silly charitable “ to aid the noble work.” William Booth,
as a “ charitable institution,” was free from the operation
of the Factories Act, and got concessions as regards
water rates. William Booth got a Government subsidy.
So William Booth was able to cut rates below what any
other establishment could live on, and yet make large
profits for William Booth. His canvassers pervaded the
neighborhood and urged people in the name of piety
and charity to send their “wash” to Booth’s cheap
sweating den, and they did. So the private laundries
which paid wages and didn’t snuffle and didn’t get a
Government subsidy were killed off. Two of the sisters
took to domestic service. One became a barmaid. Two,
when their little business was sold up as the result of
Salvation Army competition, cursed Booth and all his
hypocrisies and took to the streets. The Army rescues a
few fallen women, and makes more.


The great mental struggle which took place in the
N.S.W Assembly over clause XI. of the Land
Bill, represented a manful effort to decide on the
best way of getting a quart out of a pint pot
without damaging the pot or spilling any of the
quart, and owing to the limitations of the pot the
quart wasn’t extracted satisfactorily. The
Assembly wanted to find out a way to do the
impossible. It sought to discover some method
by which it could put a poor man on to a stretch
of poor Jand.in which he would have no interest,
except the right to stay on the land so long as he
paid his rent if he happened to be a leaseholder,
or the right to own the land when he was able to
pay for it if he chanced to be a conditional
purchaser. Then it wanted to discover how
this poor man could borrow money on the
security of the land which he didn’t
own, and how the lender, if that impro-
bable person should be found, could foreclose
in order to recover his money without dis-
turbing the principle that the owner of the land
must live thereon. It was a hopeless problem.
The Advances to Settlers Department lias been
trying to solve the question of lending money to
the poor man on poor land that he doesn’t own,
with the inevitable result; it couldn’t lend nearly
enough money to do the aggregate poor man any
good, and it seems to have lost a lot of the money
it did lend. There were vigorous demands in
some quarters for a State Bank to solve the
difficulty, but 20 State banks wouldn’t solve it,
though 40 State banks might go broke over it.
There is no way under Heaven by which a poor
man can be put on poor land for which he pays
the State, perhaps, £10 as a first instalment, and
then be lent £100 with any certainty of the lender
being repaid. The remedy lies in not having
such an awful number of poor men.

WARNER’S SAFE CURE
THE KIDNEY AND LIVER SPECIFIC.
ITS POWER EXPLAINED.
It has been * source of wonder to many how any one medicine can possibly cure so many
disorders of apparently diverse origin, but to the scientific mind the curative properties of Warner’s
oafe Cure supply no element of mystery. Warner’s Safe Cure is simply the result of years of earnest
research a remedy which possessed the power to restore to health and activity both the kidneys
and the liver. Physicians were aware of the fact that, so long as the kidneys and the liver were
working strongly, nine-tenths of the ailments with which the human race is afflicted could not exist.
The difficulty was the finding of a safe and speedy medium which would act directly upon the kidneys
and liver in such a manner that their health and vigour should be restored when once they had
become either weak or diseased. Patient investigation terminated in the discovery of the medicine
now known throughout the civilised world as Warner’s Safe Cure. This remarkable medicine
possesses a. curious and stimulating effect upon the kidneys and liver. Not only does it re-impart a
healthy action to those vital organs, but the stimulus is permanent. The importance of this great
discovery will be fully realised if the functions of the kidneys and liver are considered.
THE KIDNEYS
filter from the blood of the average person about three pints of water every day. In this water should
be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten or twelve grains, in weight, of uric acid, and other animal and
mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. If the kidneys arc weak or
diseased a portion of this solid refuse matter remains in the system, and becomes actively poisonous.
Minute crystals of uric acid form in the blood and are deposited in the joints and tissues of the body,
causing the acute pain experienced when we have
RHEUMATISM, GOUT,
Lumbago, Neuralgia, Sciatica, Sec. Sometimes the uric crystals aggregate in the kidneys and
form Gravel and Stones, and cause Kidney Colic, and Irritation and Inflan mation of the
Bladder.
THE LIVER.
So intimate is the relation between the work done by the liver and that of the kidneys that it is
almost impossible for either of them to be deranged without the other being directly affected. The
liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made from the
blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood,
converts it into another form and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood as the
latter needs enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into urea, which is com-
pletely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived their life and are
useful nb longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion, Bilious-
ness, Jaundice, Anaemia, Sick Headache, Blood Disorders, and Debility.
Warner’s Safe Cure cures all the disorders named safely, surely, completely, and permanently,
simply because it restores to vigorous health and activity the kidneys and liver, and enables them to
efficiently perform their function of removing from the system the uric and biliary poisons which
cause the sickness.
Mr*. May Edwards, of Joseph-street, Balmain, Sydney, N.S-W., says:—
“I had kidney disease, which was brought on by a cold, for nearly eight years, causing great
Fain across the back and loins and making me sick at the stomach. I could neither eat nor sleep.
had swellings in my ankles and pains in my legs, and was so ill that I had to hire a girl to do
my work. Reading in a book accounts of Warner’s Safe Cure, I decided to try the effects of this
medicine in my own case, and found immediate relief. The swelling left my ankles. I could eat
well and sleep well again. I took altogether eight bottles, which I procured from Mr. G. L.
Harvison, Chemist, Western Road. I never suffer at all now, and am really and truly cured.
The medicine did me all the good in the world.”
The commonest first system of kidney disorder is
PAIN IN THE BACK.
A surer test is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass,
and let it stand until the next morning. If it is then cloudy, shows a brick-dust like sediment,
if particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, then no time must be lost in resorting
to Warner’s Safe Cure for aid, or Bright’* Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more
painful illness will result.
