Interesting reading from Sydney Telegraph,
headed “ Irrigation by Private Enterprise
Before a meeting of the People’s Reform League last
night, Mr; J. H. Cardew read a most interesting paper on
irrigation, in which he urged that the only hope of grap-
pling with this important problem was to permit private
enterprise to step in. He advocated the establishment of
a hydrographical bureau, for the purpose of affording a
complete system of river gauging, rainfall measurement,
evaporation, and other observations ; for the compilation
of maps showing irrigable areas, and to demonstrate the
possibilities of irrigation ; that the State should construct
all reservoirs for the maintenance and regulation of the
flow in the rivers. . . and that the State should pass such
legislation as may be necessary for the purpose of. aiding
the creation of corporations, or co-operative societies, or
trusts, for the organisation of irrigation districts to
undertake schemes of supply.
How the proud Private Enterprise man begs for
State aid, and demands that State Socialism shall
be used for his benefit. He wants the State to d.o
all the profitless work of collecting scientific data
and to go to vast expense to regulate the flow of
rivers, so as to give lazy, shiftless Private Enter-
prise the water and the information to irrigate
with. Then it is to pass legislation to facilitate
Private Enterprise in forming itself into a com-
pany. Then Private Enterprise will distribute
the water supplied to it by the State, and take the
profits, and brag about how much better Private
Enterprise does these tilings than State manage-
ment. Is there anybody who begs more brazenly
for State aid than the Private Enterprise man ?
«—:
Superintendent Larkin, chief of Sydney city
police, tells the Assembly’s select committee on
gambling that, if the disease flourishes in Sydney,
it is mainly because the force cannot get satisfac-
tory backing from the Bench. It is an old com-
plaint, and as good as it is old, that the courts
won’t imprison ; they fine, and the game pays
well enough to make the fine in most cases a
matter of small consequence. But even the fines
under the Betting Houses Suppression Act are
on the down-grade. Ten years ago —in 1893—360
prosecutions landed £l4BB in fines. That was
equal to about £4 3s. a time all round. Next year
the average was just about £4; in 1895 it was
£4 Bs. ;in 1896, =£s 18s.; and in 1897 it went up
to £ll 17s. This increased severity apparently
did what it was intended to do; anyhow, whereas
in the previous three yeajs the prosecutions
averaged 304 a year, in the next three they
averaged only 115. By 1900, in fact, they had got
down to 61. But then a change set in. Instead
of averaging £ll odd, the fines in 1901 averaged
just under £3. From 61 prosecutions in 1900
(following the heavy fines), they jumped to 302 in
1902 (following the light fines). The increase
might reasonably have been expected to have
been followed by severer punishments; but it
wasn’t, and the average fine for the past couple
of years has come down to below £3. This year’s
figure is, indeed, the lowest on record; and,
naturally enough, the year’s prosecutions pro-
mise to be about the highest. The real problem
in connection with gambling suppression seems,
therefore, to be merely how to get magistrates to
do their duty.

9.M. Herald, talking of industrial conditions,
severely lectures the Australian working man on
the sinfulness of rapacity. It regards as of
“supreme importance” what Mr. J. H. Pat-
terson said “at the half-yearly meeting of a
Westralian gold-ming co. in Melbourne on Tues-
day—that, while the men had been well paid all
along, only per cent, had been paid upon the
capital invested over seven years ago.” But if
what happens in most cases happened in this case,
then about one-fourth at most of the “capital
invested ” went into the mine ; the other three-
fourths went to the people who sold it. Mr.
Patterson is chairman also of the Broken Hill
Prop., which has a capital of £384,000, and which
has a present market value of about a million and
a half; yet all the capital put into that
mine was about £15,000. The mine has,
however, to struggle to pay interest on the million
and a half, and not on the £15,000, and wages are
fixed accordingly ; and when somebody points out
that the thing into which £15,000 has been put
isn’t earning fair interest on the million and a half
“ invested in it,” the Herald tells the workman
not to be so rapacious. And the old lady, no doubt,
in her utter inability to see that there is any side
to a case except the rich man’s side, firmly
believes she is talking good Christian doctrine.

“T.5.H.,” from the West, writes:—
It looks as though there were likely to be a movement
of population from the Wentworth district of western
N.S. W. into the Victorian Mallee lands across the river.
The Victorian Mallee leases fall due in December, and
Taverner, Minister for Lands, has declared his intention
of resuming them for such closer settlement as they are
capable of. N.S.W. Minister Crick’s nose is now decidedly
out of joint, in the estimation of the Western District
homestead lessees. Not much more than 12 months ago
Wentworth welcomed him with banners and arches,
and smote the tin dinner plate of daily life
with the waddy of applause. But all that has
changed into angry feelings of resentment. Home-
stead lessees now feel that the squatter has had the
best of them in Western district land matters. Under
the Lands Act of 1884, which was the beginning of the
homestead leasehold system, the squatters were allowed
to throw open for resumption just such portions of the
runs as suited them, and these were naturally not the
best. As a consequence of this many of the homestead
leaseholders have had to settle on land away from a river
frontage and without permanent water. They hoped for
a remedy from Minister Crick ; and have hoped in vain.
The homestead lessee has now no chance in the West.

The reasonably prosperous farmers and pastoral
ists of Charlton (Vic.) are bailing up the Treasury
again for “assistance.” Through their Agricul-
tural and Pastoral Society they are pleading tear-
fully to have their manures brought to them by
the railways free. At their latest begging
demonstration various of them waxed eloquent
oyer their contention that “as manures are
stimulants to cereal .production, and therefore'to
increased railway traffic, the department ought to
carry them free.” The fact that Vic. railways
already shift the cereals at a loss, and pander to
the landlord in many other ways, only seems to
excite him to clamor for more alms, every time a
new way of doing it strikes him. It is confidently
expected that it will soon dawn on some
member of the Charlton A. and P. Society that,
as farmers are stimulants to increased cereal
production, and therefore to increased- rail-
way traffic, the Department ought to carry far-
mers free.” Of course, it’s quite plain to the
Charlton A. and P. Society that, if the Victorian
railways will only go in for enough free carriage of
everything the farmer wants or produces, and if
the Government can be Kyabramnied into cutting
down everybody’s wages to make up for it, and
then, if pressure can be brought on Irvine to
makeit piain to him that the erection of a huge
State laboratory for the manufacture of artificial
manures to be distributed free would be a fine
large, popular national undertaking, and also that
this could easily be done by cutting some more off
everybody*; wages, and sacking as many as pos-
sible, so that the farmers may be able to get
laborers for their mere food and clothes-well
then, there would be really very little for the poor
tarmer to clamor for, unless it were free drinks

A notable development in elevator-con-
struction is the conversion, by Easton and Co. of
London (Sydney agent, R. A. Ilervey, 171
Clarence-street), of the Easton Hydraulic Lift
into the most up-to date type of Electric Lift.
The cost of current used by the new Lift is, on
the testimony of the Guardian Assurance Co., less
than one-fifth of the cost of water used by the
hydraulic arrangement.*
The Federal electors should begin to think
hard about the approaching elections. To help one
in sustained hard thinking—not hard drinking—the
best of all friends is D.C.L. Whisky. If all the
male electors of the Commonwealth put them-
selves on a strict D.C.L regime, and give their
minds to a careful selection of candidates three
months before the event this country would be
well-governed hereafter. D.C.L. for drinking and
thinking purposes.*

Queensland Menzies (W.A.) has been doing well
lately, and this month’s 6d. dividend is the fifth for the
year, and constitutes a record, with the exception of 1897
when the co. paid 4s from gold won from the oxidised
zone. There has lately been a falling off in returns the
figures for the last three months reading 1337, 1248 and
11610 z., but an improvement may be expected soon. The
mine never looked better ; and the directors, who are
h?f nSer wh^ e v£ aVe rec 1 e n t v ! y determined to launch out a
. When the new 10-head of heavy stamps start, a
rise in returns and revenue may be looked for, though
the average will suffer. • s “

Menzies Alpha (W.A.), which adjoins the Lady
the! south, managed to come out with a profit
of £1230, after writing- off £4500 from development
account and allowing for depreciation. Co. is in 120,000
shares, of which 100,002 are ordinary and 19,998 prefer-
ence. The latter are entitled to a non-eumulative div.
j- P. e . r cen k- On this occasion these shares get their
d 'L , Thls absorbs £999 18s., and the small balance of
£230 is carried forward. Since the financial year closed
the mine has been turning out a fair.amount of gold, but
the chutes are so short and the lode so patchy and
irregular that it can never be relied on. The co. has now
been going for upwards of seven years and been once
reconstructed. Ordinary shareholders have got no
return, and are hardly likely to get any. This mine is
run by Rayfield of the Shenton, and the working's of the
two are connected.

Princess Royal, Norseman (W. A-), should absorb
the Central, as proposed ; this would indefinitely lengthen
the life of the mine. Manager Trelogr maintains that the.
reef in the northern portion of the property doesn’t dip
into, the Central till a depth of 1240 ft. and, in the southern
section, of 840 ft., but naturally the manager isn’t going
to cry stinking: fish till he knows the fish is decomposed.
In any case, the reef is so flat that a new shaft will have
to be sunk, as nearly 600 ft. will have to be crosscut at the
500 ft. level before the reef is tapped, and at each
additional level the position will be still worse. The pur-
chase price of 6000 shares for the Central seems very
moderate, all things considered, and the proposed
increase of capital to 120,000 shares ean’t be called exces-
sive. The £21,000, raked 'into the coffers of the co. by
issuing the- 14,000 surplus shares at 30s. each, wiit
enable the co. to sink a new main shaft without stopping
divs., and will place the co. in a splendid position
financially. If the co. were on the London market it
would probably be capitalised at double the amount.
* * * *
Queen Margaret, Bulong (W.A.), according to
half-yearly -report, is avery one-horse show, and, had the
co. not acquired the South Gippsland, at Vosperton,
the outlook would be black indeed. The South Gipps-
land property is developing splendidly, the reef all
through averaging about 2ft. in thickness, though
it varies ~ from a few inches to a few feet. During
the past half-year the co. worked at a loss of £lB6B odd, '
but, in the previous balance-sheet, the cost of wages,
stores, &c., totting up £3742, at the South Gippsland
leases was shown as an asset, and this amount has appar- .
entl.v since been written off. The co. had £2365 cash in
hand on July 1, and, as the South Gippsland concern,
when the mill and cyanide plant get in full swing, should
show a good lhargin, the co.’s next balance-sheet ought
to have a more rosy hue.

Cumberland, Norseman (W.A.), mill should get
going by the end of the month, and it will quickly prove
whether the pessimistic Capt. Pleitner, or thb optimistic
manager Bufkitt is to be top dog. The former says the
reef is barely 2ft. wide, half of this being mullock and
averaging not more than lOdwt. ; the latter asserts that
the reef runs from 2ft. 6in. to 4ft., averging 3ft., and
easily worth an oz. The mine is only being opened up at
150 ft., and as the concern has been going for as long as,
or longer thgn, .most of the Kalgoorlie - mines, the
management must have been suffering from that tired
feeling so prevalent -in the West. From the Benson’
leases 4307 tons have been treated for 42590 z. So, if one
can judge from past performances, Capt. Pleitner’s
jeremiad will get a nasty knock.

E. and 0.E.: Fremantle" (W.A.) is the great
fraud and robbery port of the Commonwealth. The other
day French liner, Armand Behic, : put in ; did not go to
wharf, but tied up to a buoy in the stream ; discharged 14
passengers and five tons of cargo; embarked six passengers
and no cargo ; and for a few hours’ Use of this bit of the
Indian Ocean the Fremantle Harbor Trust charged £930
in fees. That is equal to a duty of nearly Is. Bd. per lb.
on every item of cargo 1 mded. And the greedy mercan-
tile set which levies this stupendous tax on foreign
vessels complains bitterly that if the same ships are made
to pay Australian wages while in Australian waters it will
drive them, away altogether. If they are driven away
they must yearn for a chance to bombard Fremantle
before they go.

“True Bill ”: Prospecting isn’t what it was.
Was out recently prospecting with a new mate, and came
across a gully that had a bit of likely-looking wash in it;
sent mate With a dish of dirt to try at some water lower
do vn. In about a quarter of an hour he came back
crying, “ We’ve struck it'; we’ve struck it.” “ What did-
you get ? ” I asked. “About a quarter of a grain, all nice
coarse gold, too .” ® _ *

Max Beerbohm is not a well-informed person. He
recently stated in the Saturday Review he had never
heard that any “ colonist ” had ever attempted to write a
play. What about Haddon Chambers, George Darrell,
and half-a-dozen other Australian playwrights ? And
doesn’t Dan Barry write his own plays? If he doesn’t,
how does such magnificent badness come about?
“ J-A.0.” : Re Lalla Miranda (B. 10/9/’O3),- Her father,
David Miranda, was a teacher of singing in Melbourne
away back in the ’7o’s. He was a little, man, rather
taller than an envelope, whose carriage gave one the
idea of seven feet. His nephew, a frequent speculator in
more or less successful theatrical enterprises, looked
upon him as closely related to the Deity. He, the
nephew, one day remarked to me that there was a very
good singing part, Francis Osbaldiston, in “ Rob Roy.”
I replied, “Of course there is ! I heard Sims Reeves in
it.” “Sims Reeves!” he exclaimed; “why, iny uncle
has sung it! ” And then, for the first time, I realised
how small a circumstance was Sims Reeves compared
to David Miranda’s nephew’s uncle.
J. A. E. Malone, business manager of the George
Edwardes Co., which visited Australia with “A Gaiety
Girl ”in 1895, has lately been married. When that com-
bination was booming ip these latitudes Malone was
reported to be Grace Palotta’s husband, but on her
second visit to Australia the lady repudiated the impeach-
ment, stating that she never had a husband.

“ Petramos” : It was during the first performance in
Adelaide of “The Face at the Window.” The villain had
(as usual) lured the virtuous female to the Rogues’
Retreat. “Let me go!” shrieked the virtuous female.
Neyaire !” cried the foreign skunk ; “you shall not
leave this place until you and I have been united in the
bonds of matrimony.” Then a bibulous individual in the
two bob stalls shouted, “ Qui’ right,” and cheered enthu-
siastically. Hearing the top-hatted villain offering
matrimony to the virtuous female in his power was some-
thing so unusual that the bibulous one had after that to
be held down to prevent him applauding the villain all
the time.

ber of Frank Thornton’s co. He played a “ Test Match”
as curtain-raiser to farcical comedies. His wife played

Harry Bruce, a South African manager who seems able
to give Dan Barry points, publishes this, among other
quaint statements in his programme :—“ ‘ Snares of
London ’ is a sensational drama built round a simple
human story. To show what pains are taken that every
detail may .be correct, the fish-shells—viz., whelks and
mussels—are real (purified by Jeyes’ Fluid), and have
actually been used in London, and pedestrians in London
have eaten the contents of the shells off a stall similar to
that which we portray.” The spectacle of a simple,
honest manager who wouldn’t deceive anyone even about
the whelks and mussels, naturally appeal’s to the public.

Harry Roberts and Gaston Mervale, well remembered
in Australia, are just now members of the same American
company, playing in “Du Barry” at Belasco’s Theatre,
New York. Our old friend Kyrle Bellew, too, was lately
ip New York playing Romeo and cutting the same ultra-
romantic figure as of yore. But his voice is failing him. '
.That’s the penalty of speaking with a borrowed voice
instead of your own. Bellew imitated the tones and
tricks of elocution that came fairly natural to Irving, and
the time has now arrived when the people at the back of
the gallery exhort'him to “ speak up,” and he can’t speak
up.
•Spring”: Women, like wolves, hunt in packs
Wirths’ Circus recently reared itself in a park close
up to St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney. T’other afternoon,
a pack of matinee women awaited admission when a
murky funeral came, like a black cloud, into the view. The
pack paused irresolute and put its bob back into its purse.
After the coffin had faded away the pack grasped its bob
again and pressed towards the ticket office. Then a
gorgeous wedding, with flowers, footmen, and frock-
coats, and a vision in white drove up to the cathe-
dral. This was too much for the women. One gave a
despairing shriek and started towards the cathedral.
The whole crowd, hot and flurried, scudded after. When
it came pounding back the best circus seats had been
snapped lip by mere men, and the strong man, the fat
lady, and the sore-eared tumbler lost all their charm.
Figaro’s special commissioner in America (and the
Parisians know Something of dancing) reckons that
America is going to re-vitalise the art of dancing. “On
the American stage, everyone knows how to dance. The
American chorus girl has resuscitated the art of move-
ment—of making the whole body speak with a grace, a
charm, a seduction which are incomparable. Tne com-
bination of song and dance has made the American style
a new national art, which possesses the necessary elements
for becoming a classic art, and replacing that which has
reigned for centuries, and which is now becoming com-
monplace. . . . You will see next year an intelligent
director bringing to the boulevards the American chorus
girl of the subtle hands and agile feet.”

Bananaland is just now witnessing the
marvellous spectacle of the Brisbane Courier
barracking for a new party of which the backbone
and a lot of the forefront shall be Labor repre-
sentatives. To such a pitch can hatred of a
coming journalistic rival drive an infuriated
morning paper,
The suburbanites who were attracted into
Melbourne last Friday night by a red glare in the
sky got a sensation. At the wharf, they found
that the fore-hold of the Pilbarra was a mass of
flames, fed by nitre, general merchandise, and a
few cases of explosives. While the crowd looked
on, the head and arm of a trapped pantryman was
thrust out of a forecastle porthole. The crowd
promptly rushed to the point of anticipated crema-
tion, and declined to shift for either firemen or
police—not until a squad of mounted troopers
arrived could they be driven back. Meanwhilg,
a level-headed constable got a couple of axes
and started to hew a way through the deck—
luckily it was a .wooden one. One axe-handle
immediately broke, but the other stood, while
policeman Lang and his mate hacked away for
their lives—and the life of the man inside. He was
just able to grab a lowered rope and hang on till
he was hauled through the ragged hole, then he
fainted away. While the axemen were chopping
with sparks and smoke swirling around them, the
units at the back of the crowd insanely pressed
forward, while those in front fought to get back
from the heat and the Yarra, and occasionally the
booming of Qxplosives .down in the hold started a
fresh pnniip. It was an ideal scene for an
Anderson melodrama, but for the half-fried
pantryman and the singed policemen it was a
crowded hour.
Sale of Phil May’s Drawing's.
To-morrow, Friday, at 11 o’clock, the auctioneers
(Walter Bradley & Co ) will sell The Bulletin’s
splendid series of original drawings by Phil May.
The collection is on view to-day for the last
time at the sale-rooms, 324 George-street, Sydney.
Such a fine set of examples of black-and-white
art, by the most brilliant draughtsman of his
generation, has never before been brought together
in Australia.
Phil May joined the staff of The Bulletin in
1885, at the age of 21, and for two and a-lialf
, years remained in Australia. His style, already
. formed, whs in this period developed and assured.
His eye for a likeness and for character, and the
dexterity and decision with which he set down
his impressions, became remarkable. In technical
quality of draughtsmanship he never surpassed
the drawings made at this time.
Nearly every notable Australian personage of
the ’eighties was a subject for May’s pen. His
drawings might form no inconsiderable contribu-
tion to a National portrait gallery. Numerous
political cartoons represent a class of work which
he did not attempt elsewhere. To his Australian
Eeriod, also, belong some of the best among his
umourous pictures.
Phil May was the founder of a school in black-
and-white, as Sir Joshua Reynolds, for example,
was the founder of a school in painting. His
talent influenced others in a remarkable degree.
Unlike some artists, he had the good fortune to
see his work appreciated in his life-time. He has
been appreciated no higher than his desert. His
original drawings possess a quality which none of
his imitators has matched. As time passes, many
of them will be more and more valued as examples
of modern illustration.
What English and American Critics say of
Phil May’s Work.
Whistler wrote, in a letter printed in the
catalogue of the May exhibition, 1895:—“ The
exhibition you are going to have will certainly be
most interesting, and I wi*h I could see it; for I
take a great delight in Phil May. Certainly his
work interests me far more than that of any man
since Charles Keene, from whom he is quite
distinct. There is a brightness and daintiness in
what he does, combined with knowledge, These,
together with the fact that in his drawings the
wit is the artist’s, makes a vast difference between
him and his contemporaries.”
Hubert Herkomer, R.A., said, in opening an
exhibition of Charles Keene’s drawings:—“ln
his own day Keene stood alone. He has been
followed by a great rival in his own particular
line. Phil May has gifts as great as those of
Keene. He is the greatest living master of ex-
pression in a few lines. I don’t know Phil May—
I have never met him—but I think his art is
marvellous.”
Charles Dana Gibson said : —“ If a man’s bad,
I can tell you why he’s bad ; but when he’s as
good as Phil May, I can’t tell you why he’s good,
because if I could, I could do just what he’s doing,
and I can’t. Alll can say is, that his work is just
sticky with human interest, and that’s all there is
about it! ”
The Athenaeum (8/8/3) says:—“The death, at
the early age of thirty-nine, of the artist universally
known as Phil May was not unexpected, but is
no less a loss to the world, since he was unequalled
in his own style. Mr. May was born in Leeds in
1864, and did cartoons in London for the St.
Stephen’s Review, but made his name first in
Australia on The Bulletin, a well-known centre
■for the work of clever young writers and artists.”
' The Daily Chronicle (6/8/3) calls May “the
most original caricaturist of the age,” and adds
(7/8/3): “Perhaps the most productive period of
the jate Phil May was the three years he spent in
Sydney as the leading artist of The Bulletin. He
joined that most popular and widely-circulated of
Australian weekly journals shortly after its
foundation, and the striking novelty and quaint
originality of his pictorial contributions were no
small factors in spreading the name and fame of
The Bulletin all. over the Commonwealth.”
The Daily Mail (6/8/3) calls May “pro-
bably the greatest black-and-white humorist
since the days of Leech,” and tells these
stories of him“ At the Savoy Hotel two
or three years ago a supper party was being
given in honor of the birthday of Mme. Amy
Sherwin, on whose menu card Mr. May made an
exquisite little drawing. This was seen by a
wealthy woman present, who sent the waiter
with a ten-pound note to the artist asking him to
do a similar drawing for her. Mr. May, dis-
gusted at the woman’s impertinence, took a good
look at her, and then made an appallingly truth-
ful caricature of her features on the back of. the
bank note, which he returned. It was a terrible
vengeance.
“.On one occasion in Romano’s after dinner he
sketched the portrait of each of Ins fellow guests
on their respective shirt-fronts, taking the stud or
studs as a point to work from. These life-like
drawings were the work of very few moments,
but they should be valuable now, if any of the
diners had the forethought to preserve them
instead of sending them to the wash ! ”
The Morning Leader says: “ The world of art
ancj the world of. humor has lost one of its finest
artists and one of its merriest of jesters.”

“ Hyphen s he :
Passingthe Law Courts in Lonsdale-Street (Melbourne),
about 9 a.m., I saw six men with three horses in attend-
ance, trying to shift a stump of telegraph pole out of a
hole in the kerbing. They had a derrick and pulleys
rigged, and a chain passed round the stump. Then they
all threw themselves on to the rope. “ Yaheavoo ” they
yelled, and pulled lustily. Nothing shifted—till the
chain slipped. Then they all sat down in an untidy heap.
After some argument they started again, and I came
away. Passing that way from lunch, observed men,
horses and stump still in the same place. The men were
still “ Yaheavooing” when I repassed at 4 p.m. It was
not till about 5.30, on my way homewards, that they had
all disappeared—all, that is, except the stump. It was
still there, with a slight list to port and a broad grin on
its rugged countenance.

policemen who would be picked as spielers,
bookmakers or capitalists before being taken
for “thief-catchers.” Standing at our street
corner last Sunday were two plain clothes
policemen watching Casey’s pub. 1 heir attitude
was a picture—heels together, toes turned out
at an angle of forty-five degrees, left hand
fumbling with watch chain, no conversation
between them —only the off eye of each on
Casey’s instead of one watching it. Presently
a red-headed youngster rushed up breathless
and told another kid that Bruiser Morgan was
wallopin’ the stuffin’ out of big Gaffer down
the back lane. The two Johns immediately
wended towards the lane, and Casey “unloaded”
seven thirsty citizens. The red-headed kid lives
next to Casey’s, and scouts for him all day Sunday.

Some Doiiurs in Divorce •
oonie Doings in Divorce .
Justice Hood tackled a record list of divorce cases (51)
in Melbourne last month and got through them in busi-
ness-like fashion, wasting no time in Maddenesque plati-
tudes on the iniquity of absent co-respondents and missing
respondents. Mostly they were of the common or “ ordi-
nary ” variety, where the husband fades away through
weariness, or the wife casually resigns the billet of
honorary washer-up and bab.v-minder without pay ; but
a few showed some bright tints One husband had given
his wife but £1 during 13 years of wedded bliss, but
thoughtfully informed her ere finally departing in ’99 that
“ if she wanted him he was to be found every night at
Tattersall’s, playing cards.” Another went to that alleged
hunting ground of missing husbands—Westralia—sent his
wife £1 in four years, then lapsed permanently. A petition-
ing husband, who married at 19 and gave 10s. of his 17s. 6d.
wages to his wife (she staying meanwhile at her mother’s),
seemingly took so little heed of his looser half that she
acquired two irregular infants before he began to be
suspicious. A lady who, at 34, had married a man of 39,
had never lived with him. A month after the wedd’ng,
while she was still in service, he went for a trip to Eng-
land, promising to make a home for heron his return,
That was the last she saw of him. Another extra-sudden
respondent had faded away after a brief honeymoon of

ä
PEOPLE cannot understand why Diamonds do not come down in price.
Unless some new Diamond fields are discovered they will never come
down, and I think that proportionately they will go as much higher
in the next ten years as they have in the last ten years. The reason
is this: Ten years ago it took less than one-third of the effort to produce
Diamonds as it does to-day. In other words, the strata of blue ground in the
Kimberley Fields that were then worked were so prolific that Diamonds
were found practically on the top of the earth. After the very prolific strata
were worked out and it was necessary to go down a little deeper into the
earth Diamonds were not quite so plentiful, and to day it takes at least three
times the effort to produce the same amount of stones it did ten years ago.
Pearls are also going up in value at a rapid rate.

only with the view of selling our Diamond goods, but actual facts which you

The Inky Way.
A unique correspondence has been going on between
two Bulletin contributors who happen to be neighbors.
Some time in the tail of 1901, one of them, “D.H.,’*
wrote from the inside of British Papua quoting prices for
“tucker” in the Yodda Valley. The par. got to the B.
at the end of March, 1902. By the middle of August,
1902, it had got back-in print to the inside of Papua ;
whereupon the other correspondent, “ Shopki,” pro-
ceeded to dance on “D.H.,” and the result reached the
B,on Dec. 20. In April “D.H.” was able to read what
his neighbor had said about him in August the year
before ; and his remarks, defending the statement he had
made away back in 1901, wandered Into The Bulletin
office late in July, 1903 This is, no doubt, what daily-
paper correspqhdents call “rushing into print.”
Bairnsdale, the sleepy’, stone-built capital of Gippsland,
boasts a curiosity in the shape of a half-penny weekly,
entitled Every Week. It is a small sheet, consisting of
twenty columns well studded with “live” advertise-
ments. Every Week has been twice enlarged during its
short existence, and has a guaranteed circulation of 2,300
copies weekly, which knocks the shine out of the other
tri-weeklies published locally'—but though the published
price is nominally ‘ half-a-brown,’ the copies are really
distributed free. They are in great demand on the
morning of publication b.y the local tradesmen, wherein
to wrap country’ orders. Hence it is a common sight to
see the lass from Mossiface sitting aboard the puffing
mud-back scraper, homeward bound, with her parcel of
sausages lying on her lap in all their damp, red naked-
ness, and the cabbage between her feet, while the
wrap is being read ! Bairnsdale’s oldest paper, the
Advertiser, published tri-weekly, is a natty
sheet, of fair dimensions, run entirely by the
Yates family father, sons, and daughter. The
Courier is published on alternate days to its rival.
These two papers devote much of their scanty space to
calling each other a reptile. The Snowy River Mail is
another curiosity, published weekly up where the bunyip
is supposed to have its existence. Syndicate “ ads.”
form the burden of its matter, and out of nine local pars
five will probably refer to pigs, one to onions, and the
remainder to other animals or vegetables. Society
gossip is not expected Snowy River way—but pig
news is the essence of a happy existence up there.

Journalist G. E. Bentley, who died at Ashburton (M.L.),
t’other day, was fairly well-known in Tasmania and Aus-
tralia. Breaking a limb through falling down stairs, he
was operated upon twice. When told that a third opera-
tion was imperative, he recalled that there had been a
dozen deaths in M.L. in 18 months as a result of ansesthe-
tics, so, lest the opportunity might be lost, he wrote an
article giving his dreamy impressions when under the
influence of the drug on the second occasion. Bentley

Some provincial newspapers do* pay. Charters Towers
daily Miner and weekly Reoister f’rinstance. Pro-
prietary is spending about £2OOO on a new building in
place of the old shanty, which dates from about the mid-
seventies. The Miner was once famous as the organ of
Thady O’Kane, whose record for libels transcends John
Snorting’s. Several times after Thady’s severance from
the paper the old building was within an ace of having
a roDe run round it with a mob of miners heaving
thereon. Thady would be uneasy in his grave if he
could see what a tame and respectable rag his old sheet
has degenerated into.

THE WESTERN RAG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You’re only a commonplace Western print—
A dreary sheet—yet I call you friend;
A feeble effort, with scarce a hint
Of decent copy from end to end.
Your leader is dull as it well can be,
Your news is gleaned from the mailman’s bag ;
Yet your dullest page has a charm for me,
For you take me a journey, old Western Rag I
You bear me away to a far-out town
Where the hot sun glares on the hot iron roofs —
Where the drovers rattle their money down
To the merry tune of the flying hoofs;
For they run a race in the main street there,
With a bar-towel used for a starter’s flag;
And—win or lose it—they run it fair ;
Or they did when I knew you, old Western Rag 1
In every par is an old mate’s name—
They’ve a flow, I see, at that bore of Brown’s ;
And I learn that Cameron’s dog is lame,
And that Howe was “the ringer” at Bowen Downs.
“ The Rager’s” been out on a mighty burst —
I’ll wager the bobbies found him a snag ;
He was none too slow when we knew him first,
But, like us, he’s a has-been, old Western Rag.
And her name is there—a discerning pen
Has written her “ Belle of the last race ball.”
Does she queen it now as she queened it then—
The beautiful tyrant who ruled us all ?
Does>he ever think ’mid the voices’ hum
Of a waltz “ sat out ” neath a drooping flag?
Why should she think ? Yet the thought will come ;
And I blame you for it, old Western Rag.
N.s.w.
SPINIFEX.
SAPPHO AND ME.
[For Tub Bulletin.]
Sappho of Lesbos wrote on her isle
Verse by the furlong and verse by the mile ;
Now all her poems are lost in the years
Spilt o’er the rims of the twain hemispheres.
Sappho the Lesbian’s verses are dead,
But mine are still printed, paid for, and read.
There’ll be a day when mine are forgot,
And Sappho and I shall inuse o’er our lot,
Under the shade of the wild olive-tree,
You’ll find there two ghosts, those of Sappho and Me !
GRANT HERVEY.
A LETTER,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear Bulletin, —I’ve tried in vain, and yet somehow 1 do
not tire
Of trying to arouse what you might call the true poetic
fire.
I feel sometimes that you are hard ; then, as I read, I
think you right,
For my poor efforts fail to paint the glorious day, the
wondrous night.
And when from out my camp I see through scrub the
sun-rise gladdening all,
And bird to bird a message sends with thrilling sound
from branches tall,
I take my pen to write a thought—to seize it ere it flits
away—
And sometimes feel that I have caught a book-mark for
the leaves of day.
And when I stoop to raise a stem, crowned by some
fair dew-laden flower,
I think at last I have the theme, and in the theme I have
the power;
But when I try to tell the tale of petals wet with tears of
dew,
A cow-bell in the distance tolls : “ It will not do, it will
not do 1 ”
W.A.
JOHN S. ALLDAY.


“Ses”: During the debate on the Ser-
vants’ Registry Bill, Mr. J. 0. Xj. Fitzpatrick,
one of the three who voted against it, said that
“the necessity of private registry offices was
shown by the fact that, while the office estab-
lished by the State was dealing with hundreds, a
private office would deal with thousands of appli-
cations.” The difference mentioned doesn’t prove
anything of the kind; it merely shows that a
majority of employers, who can’t be fleeced and
have nothing to lo3e, go to the private offices, and
the unfortunate slavey has to follow suit to get an
eariy engagement. Also it shows that private
offices get a vast number of applicants by adver-
tising imaginary billets. Another thing which has
much weight with the majority of servants is the
taint of pauperism that has become inseparable
from State institutions of the kind. Wipe out the
private offices altogether, and this objection would
be in a great measure removed, and Mrs. Potts-
Point would have no alternative but to place her
favors with the State office. The Bill doesn t go
far enough. It should be made illegal for any
person to keep a private registry office. It seems
a strange anomaly that a community that kicks
so vigorously against k< Tattersall s swoops can
permit these registry sharks to live and grow fat
on the misfortunes of others. We want them put
down, not licensed.

file Evangelical Church Association ”
of Victoria, with sectarian Canon Berry for its
mouthpiece, is asking that all charitable institu-
tions which employ labor for profit-making pur-
poses may be brought under the provisions of tße
Factories and Shops Acts. It goes without
saying that Canon Berry’s evangelical optic is
fixed upon the R.C. convent laundry, where the
female of more or less unstable character washes
her sins away in the Faith which maintains a
prosperous opposition to the E.C.A. Also it is
an undeniable fact that the charity of a convent
asylum which takes in washing may be made to
cover a multitude of injustices to the workers. A
home is provided for some women, who do nothing
but eat, sleep, and say their prayers, and for
others who work very little ; but a number of able-
bodied, willing toilers are perhaps sweated for
the glory of God and the good of the institution
as a going concern. This being conceded to the
sectarianism of Canon Berry and co., it remains
for those doubtful, champions of Factories and
bhops legislation'to extend their solicitude to the
inmates of certain Protestant washeries, where the
weary refugee works out her own salvation with-
out any worldly wages. And the industrial
operations of the Salvarmy call for State super-
vision If the Christian endeavors of Canon
Berry and co. can bring Harmy trading under the
control of the Factories Act the Lord’s blessing
will probably rest upon those one-sided agitators.
But perhaps, while the Canon is on the job, he will
hustle his friend Irvine a little and try to make
reasonably sure that there will be any Factories
Act to bring the convent under.
Plain language from W. T. Stead in a
review of “The Failure of Lord Curzon,”byan
ex-civilian official:
The poverty of the people who have to foot the bill run
up by the Russophobists is appalling. In Behar, one of
the richest provinces of Bengal, some twelve millions of
the peasants during many months of the year live or die
on one meal a day. These statements relate to a period
antecedent to Lord Curzon’s advent Later statements
are unprocurable, because Lord Curzon has put the gag
upon his officials. Anyone who so far forgets himself as
to admit that famine is caused by any but natural causes
finds his career ended. “Mum’s the word” in India
to-day.
Privy Council decision in the case B. R.
Wise v. Perpetual Trustee Co. is of some interest
to club members. Mr. Wise, as a member of the
old Cercle Frangais Club, was, with four others,
sued to make good a big liability left by the club
on its dissolution. He was willing to share with
all the other members the liability, but not to
shoulder an undue proportion of it. The case
went against Wise in the N.S.W. courts ; the
Privy Council decided in his favor and ruled—
Clubs are associations of a peculiar nature. They are
societies the members of which are perpetually changing.
They are not partnerships ; they are not associations for
gain ; and the feature which distinguishes them from
other societies is that no member, as such, becomes
liable to pay to the funds of the society or to anyone else
any money beyond the subscriptions required by the
rales of the cluh to be paid so long as he remains a mem-
ber. It is upon this fundamental condition, not usually
expressed, but understood by everyone, that clubs are
formed ; and this distinguishing feature has been often
judicially recognised.
This decision will probably have some effect on
the credit of clubs. The man who gives credit to
a club can only hope to get his money if the sub-
scriptions of the members and anything that may
be raised by a voluntary passing of the hat will
cover the debt. Any attempt at compulsory
Eassingof the hat to two or three solvent mem-
ers justifies these members in kicking said hat —
hard.
Be H. E. Holland’s paragraph on Ethel
Herringe, “John Carew” writes :
I regret that Mr. Holland’s memory is so defective.
He may refresh it by reading the statements on oath in
Oowra Free Press of Nov 28th, 1902, or Oowra Guardian.
(1) Mr. Holland states : “Lee was the only Cohentin-
genter Ethel Herringe had been engaged to.” The word
“ engaged ”is his own. The relations between Ethel and
the first Cohentingenter were such as permitted her to
receive half his pay. In connection with this emolument
the Anglican cleric, Seymour-Smith, signed the cus-
tomary document. Three months intervened between
the death of the first Cohentingenter and the man-
slaughter of Maurice John Lee.
(2) Mr. Holland denies the statement —“Without any
apparent conventional reason, she sprang a marriage
ceremony,” etc. —and retorts : “ When dying, Lee
admitted his promise to marry Ethel Herringe.” Does a
man’s promise to marry constitute an “ apparent con-
ventional reason ” for the woman to fix time, place and
circumstance at a moment’s notice, with the alternative
of sudden death? When dying, Lee, on oath, said : “ I
made no arrangement to marry her.” Another sworn
witness stated he remarked—“ Why did you not give me
a chance ?’’
(3) As to my statement, “ The subsequent Grenfel'i
petition for her release was coolly received in Cowra,”
neither press, clergy, nor influential citizen supported
the movement. Many signatures were given from pity
for the girl’s respectable family, and by persons not
anxious to be pointed out as desiring her detention. As
to the remark—“ The tardy infant provoked no enthu-
siasm it is an unfortunate circumstance that this sort
of infant never does provoke enthusiasm.
“No Prime Minister was sufficiently
imbued with Australian sentiments to be trusted
-with important negotiations such as these in
London, for when he basked in the smile of
duchesses, when he was taken in hand by Mr.
Chamberlain, and when the King held a sword
over his head, and did not let it drop, our experi-
ence was th ’ afc Australian interests were for-
gotten.”—Senator Stewart, on the Naval Tribute.

Wrecked Illusions.
DEDICATED TO LOUIS BECKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You are now in London town,
Louis Becke,
Keeping up your old renown,
Writing yarns of women brown,
Getting yellow money down,
Or a cheque.
That is right enough, maybe—
You are wise;
But your Isles of the South Sea,
Where the life is bold and free,
You may have them all for me—
Dash your eyes !
I am full of you,JJI am,
To the neck;
And I cannot think with calm
Of your tales “ By Reef and Palm ”
But I have to mutter “D n
Louis Becke! ”
You have dined, the press records
(Mot in joke),
At the hospitable boards
Of a lot of dukes and lords,
And beguiled them with your words —
Simple folk !
Yet I would not envy you,
Be it said,
If the tiles you told were true
As they were unique and new—
But you made them all up, Loo,
In your head.
Ne* ;r, as in days of yore,
(You will see)
On your pages shall I pore,
With t .cir yarns of love and gore,
Never, Louis, any more
Becke for me.
I’d rejoice to have you here
, (You might grieve !)
With your pen behind your ear,
In this clammy atmosphere,
Where it rains all round the year,
I believe.
O, you made a fine renown !
Mr. 8.,
With your yarns of women brown,
And the red hibiscus crown
On the black hair hanging down
To the knee.
I have seen in Santa Cruz,
(Bet your life!)
Women browner than tan shoes—
And I’d rather die than choose
Any one of them as Muse,
Or as wife.
They had hair limed freely, but
Wore no wreath;
They had mouths of comic cut—
Mouths that hardly ever shut—
Red with chewing betel-nut,
And black teeth.
And their tank ears hung in loops,
And were well
Loaded down with rings in groups,
Blocks of wood, and things like scoops,
And their noses shone with hoops
Made of shell.
They exhaled a perfume rare
(Potent yet,
Even in this strong sea-air),
Of its name I’m not aware—
But it was not, I can swear,
Mignonette.
Could Romance live there ? Alas,
It took wings!
Louis, you can take the class,
You can have the lot—l pass—
With, their petticoats of grass.
And nose-rings.
And your traders —Grand old Drunks —
Where are they?
I have seen 3ome queer quidnuncs
Who go sober to their bunks,
And are temperate as monks,
Sad to say.
They were clothed in suits of white,
Fresh and neat;
And no marks of rpcpnt fight
Marred their countenances bright,
And they spoke in words polite,
Clean and sweet.
If this Rechabitish crew,
This tame lot,
Are indeed the models true
Of the Traders bold you drew —
Then I really think that you
Should be shot.
You may say in weak excuse—
Being gnawed
By your conscience—that the loose
Stories that you did produce
Dealt with other isles. No use !
You’re a Fraud !
Well, my Last Illusion so
Comes to wreck.
’Tis your fault, as well you know,
Yet I would not wish you woe —
But you know where liars go,
Louis Becke!
GUADALCANAL
V. J. DALEY.
Is the superior competing Scotchman to
disappear ? Stated by an English authority that
the Scottish working clashes are rapidly deteriorat-
ing in consequence of having discarded porridge
in favor of the Cockney diet of boiled tea, bread,
jam, and tinned food.
Bananaland.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Queensland, Chowland, Kanakaland— ifc is all
one, a country with very little evil going to waste.
Its rolling downs are extra-productive ; its moun-
tains are auriferous ; its grass is sweet and pro-
lific, and its crops are always big—that is, if there
are any crops at all. It is a land of pile-raised
wooden houses with iron roofs, the habitude of
perennial thirst, soil of the bottle tree and beauti-
ful bunya-bunya pine, and home of the alligator.
It is the richest of Australian States—a land of
lassitude and of easy-going people. It is, more-
over, the milch cow of political jobbers.
The Southerner mentally associates Bananaland
mainly with heat. The heat of Queensland in the
aggregate is higher than that of the southern
States ; there are far higher temperatures in N.S.
Wales, Victoria, and South Australia than in
Queensland, but the Queensland heat is more con-
stant. It becomes really trying when you begin to
tell yourself how hot it is, and try to get cool places,
and take constant iced drinks, as the Queensland
people usually do. A Winton man tried to feel
cool in dirty Townsville, the Accurst, and from
there sought coolness in Cairns, and then at Barron
Palls, and. then Chillagoe; and then he hurried
back to Winton. He told me his own hell was
the best. There is no town in all Bananaland
that I would leave to live in Adelaide during the
summer-time.
The fault with Queenslanders in the coast towns
is that they give way to the heat. Lassitude is
catered for. Everything is iced that ice will
teinjper; there are electric fans to breathe on
your bald head, or swaying punkahs; there
are deep verandahs and deck chairs and cane
lounges; whisky, claret, sherry, squashes, iced
lager and milk shakes ; delicious tropical fruit—
pine-apples and mangoes, paw-paw (a sort of rock-
melon which grows on a New Guinea tree) and
grenadilla, which is merely a passion-fruit in a
small pie-melon shell. People from the cool parts
endure the heat better than Queenslanders—that
is for a time. Swagmen, stockmen, teamsters,
fettlers, cane-cutters do not think about the heat.
They do not get catered for—that is why.
When the Victorian first visits Queensland he is
all restlessness ; he wants to get about. “Come
on,” he says, full of enthusiasm, “I know where
there is a mango tree with ripe fruit.” The limp
Kanakaland er gloomily looks up from his lounge.
“It won’t run away,” he says. “Well, let’s get
the gun and see if we can’t knock over a duck.”
“Don’t think we will see any game,” says the
somnolent one. “ Oh, well, for goodness sake,
let’s go fishing, or do something.” “ Oh, what’s
the good?” says the man of Bananaland.
The houses of these enervated, tired, iced and
fanned people are built of wood, have iron
roofs, and when you see a big house it has
probably as many legs as a centipede. These
piles are sometimes twenty steps high, and
are used to hold the dwelling above white ants.
Between each pile and the house is a tin
plate, which projects; past this the earth-trail
of the ant can’t go. Every house has its verandah,,
and every verandah its lounge and easy chairs,
and even its bed or two. When thb tenant is not
at work, or enjoying the breeze of an electric fan,,
or taking his wife out for a drive (it is the am-
bition of every Queenslander to own a pony and
sulky), he is to be found on his verandah, like the
Transvaaler on his stoep. ■»
The first impression one takes of Queensland is
the various shading and castes of faces. All races
are busy mixing up the breeds and trying to cross
the Anglo-Saxon out of all recognition. First and
foremost there is the Chinaman. Every town
large enough to have a store has a Chinaman
keeper. Next you notice the Kanakas, who
usually go in droves. And right on top of that
you rub shoulders with the Assyrian hawker, who
is übiquitous. You hear the whine of the Hindu,,
and see the agile Japanese everywhere. There are
also our own derelicts there, Sydney’s overflow,
and Queensland’s own victims of iced whisky and
soda.
I like the young Queenslander even better than
the Maoriland fellow. He is chivalrous and
totally free from “side,” the bane of the Sydney
Johnny. His manners are simple, and he is a.
good thinker (languor is conducive to thought),
and his conversation is interesting. He is an
omnivorous reader (when he can read, and it is
marvellous how many young folks in Queensland
use a oross for a signature), and he is generally
eager to know what you have read. Unfor-
tunately, as he grows older, he loses his charm,
takes to nipping, and lets lassitude pervade his
thinking part. Decent fellows that they are,,
they nevertheless hold first place for obscenity.
T'h" “ blanky ” and the banana grow together up'
North.
_..... girls tend to grow pasty faced. They are 1
invariably plain, but have a queenly bearing. The
graceful carriage of the Italian women is imputed
to carrying pitchers of water on the head from thn
public fountains. The Queensland girls do not
carry pitchers of water on the head, and yet these-
slim girls have all the stateliness of the Dago-
races. You would think those hot climates
would have a tendency to bow the shoulders.
Queensland people are all more or less naked.
It is the land of pyjamas. The way men go about
hotels in pyjamas shocked me the first time I
visited Bananaland. In. Townsville, Cairns, and
Cooktown the leading citizens dress only in white
duck trousers and silk shirts. It is almost a mark
of distinction to dress like that, and take a cab if
you want to go to the next block. Some of these
towns have an astonishing number of cabs. I’ve
seen a dealer in Brisbane going about in bathing-
pants and a blazer. Women have shamed me
with their little-on-ness. The black gins in Cook-
town go about clothed in a skirt buttoned over
one shoulder, and the arm through the placket-
hole. Sometimes they just wear a chemise. But
in the Northern Territory, male blacks often
come into town dressed in a two-pound jam-tin.
The general dress is kharki, white duck, Assam,
or China silk suits. Hats are helmets, panama,
tuscan, or wide-leafed sombreros. I’ve seen boxers
marked up at Is. each, and the man who wears a
silk hat is fair game for the first boy he meets.
In fact, employers of boys don’t count knocking
off a tall hat with a wet dollop of rag as an
offence ; they say they feel tempted to do it them-
selves. But then, the man in proper Queensland
rig who goes South is just as attractive. People
in Sydney stare twice at his white helmet and
white green-lined umbrella and silk suit. In
Melbourne they stare him out of sight, and in
Ballarat hilarious small boys chase him.
As in Maoriland, barefooted children do not
denote poverty. Most Queensland children go
barefooted. t One boy confided to me that he
wouldn't wear his boots more than half-an hour
on Sunday.
T 1 le further north you go the plainer the women
become. This is true from Melbourne upwards;-

Everyone knows the purpose of X
a water filter.—to filter the im-
purities from water.
Have you ever reflected for a.
moment on the fact that you have
filters in your system a thousand
times more intricate and delicate
than the water filter—a thousand
times more important, too 1
These filters are the kidneys.
It is their duty to filter from the
blood the impurities which cause disease,
and to pass them from the system.
But sometimes the kidneys get sick through a fall or
strain, exposure, excessive use of liquor, perhaps, or one
of many other causes. Then they become clogged.
It’s no use to try to filter water with a clogged filter ; before the water
can be pui'ified the filter must be cleansed. It should always be kept clean.
The same, exactly, with your kidneys. When they are clogged they
cannot filter the blood properly ; then certain impurities are left in the system
that poison every nerve and organ. These poisons, this unfiltered blood,
cause Anaemia, Jaundice, Eczema, Pimples, Boils, Blotches, Piles, Varicose
Veins, Sallow Complexion, as well as Female Troubles, Neuralgia, Headaches,
Melancholia, and a hundred other ills.
A tonic or purgative medicine will often give temporary relief in cases of
this kind, but the trouble soon returns. It is NO USE to doctor the symp-
toms if you want a lasting cure—you must go to the root of the trouble and
doctor that.
A gardener who is weeding does not clip off the body of the weed, but
pulls it out by the root. The kidneys are the root of troubles brought about
by impure blood, and
loan’s gackache fines Pills
are a medicine specially prepared for Kidney Disorders. They will never
fail to gently aid and soothe the kidneys, and to cleanse them from im-
purities. They cure the root of your trouble, not merely the symptoms.
Entirely vegetable in their composition, they do not injure the system, nor
purge.
You are not asked to believe our statement alone, nor the word of a
stranger living in a distant town, but you are asked to place confidence
in the word of a fellow-resident, who frankly speaks out for the public
benefit.
SYDNEY PROOF.
Farrier-sergeant Grace, Ist Regiment of Australian Bushmen of N.S.W.,
addresses the following letter : “ ‘ Klip Drift,’ March Ist, 1901. Dear Sirs,—l am
very pleased to state that vve have your famous Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills in
the midst of our regiment. I was very much troubled in the early part of the
campaign with pains all over me, especially in the back. You see I was always
trekking about in bad weather, and sleeping on the damp ground, and one would
want an iron constitution to.withstand the inroads on one’s system. Many morn-
ings I have tried to get up, but failed until I obtained assistance. My agony
was often so bad that I felt inclined to rush into the zone of the enemy’s fire and
put an end to my sufferings. All the military doctors who treated me failed to
do me any good. One day-a comrade gave me three boxes of Doan s Backache
Kidney Pills, and told me to try them. The first box eased me greatly, and the
third box cured me. Many of my comrades used them, and they swear by this
wonderful medicine. Indeed, this testimony expresses their gratitude to Doan s
Backache Kidney Pills as much as it does mine. You may use this in any way
you like.”
Anyone wishing to investigate this case now may do so at 18 Glebe-street,
Glebe, where Sergeant Grace lives ; he will give full particulars of his cure, and
of the way Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills earned a good name in S’Africa, as
good a name as they have in Sydney to-day.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are for sale by all Chemists and Store-
keepers at 2s. 9d. per box (six boxes, 15s. 3d.), or will be posted, on receipt of
price, by Foster-M'Clellan Co., 76 Pitt-street, Sydney.
BUT BE SURE THEY ARE DOAN’S-
only the girls on the highlands look fresher as you
go. In consequence of her sallowness the Queens-
land girl admires the man of meat (as the Maori-
lander), and the man with red cheeks (as the young
Englishman). The Queenslanders wno go South
succumb to the Melbourne girls, but this is only
proper. Victorian girls, with their freshness and
vitality, are the fit mothers of Queenslanders.
The wild Irish girl and the gazelle-eyed Lancashire
witch, who have emigrated to Queensland, may
pick and choose ; but beauty does not last long—
the round on the cheek falls in and the dainty
curves puff out, and the pasty pallor usurps the
sanguine color, and only dialect remains.
The police are as easy-going as the people they
keep m order. They seemed to me to be afraid of
stringent action. Shutting the hotel’s bar-door
on Sundays in Queensland is a farce. Inside, the
business goes on just the same. Everybody
walks in as on a week-day. Sometimes a
sergeant gets himself disliked. “Are these 6ona
fide travellers, Mrs. Bung?” he asks. “Can’t
y°u see for yourself they are not?” she snaps.
“Well, I’ll have to caution you,” he says. People
think he is too officious.
Towns are progressive. They get hard knocks
and recuperate like a miracle. They are dear
towns, but business people are very candid.
Might as well live down south if we don’t get
lug profits.” Longreach had years of drought,
and then a fire came along and burnt up the
best part of the town. It was to be expected
that the whole of Longreach would wrap its
last lone garment round its loins and “get.”
There was nothing to look forward to, only rain,
and, as in Bourke, the rain came not. Yet Long-
reach built up the burnt-out spaces and built
better than before. They tell you one good season
will pay for fi ve bad ones. But there are towns
like Bowen which are stagnant enough.
Hotels in the coastal towns are very good. A
great feature of the table is fruit. Almost every
hotel has a Chinese cook and a Chinese offsider.
Unfortunately, the Chinaman is a good cook.
Out back the hotels rate with the N.S.W. pubs. If
you used the butter supplied by some of these
hotels to the boarders, for axle-grease, the wheels
would run hot.
Cairns seems to have most beauty spots about
it. It is a moist, tropical town, which a visiting
parson described as a “ steaming hell. ” Shut in
with volcano-like mountains, there is only one
breeze can cool it. Tiiey tell you that a river once
ran into the estuary, but a volcano burst up in the
channel and diverted the waters into another out-
let. The railway runs through awfully wild, grand
scenery, spoken of as the most beautiful in Aus-
tralia. For my part, the best Bananaland scenery
1 saw was in the vicinity of the Glass Mountain,
near Gympje. Numbers of nun-like rocks tower
above Australian vegetation, delicately-traced,
white-butted gum-trees, with staghorns growing
on them like the gigi of Maoriland; slim, tall
palms and blossoming bauhinia. It had a won-
derfully peaceful effect on one; everything was so
bright, graceful, delicate, cool-looking, and luxu-
riant. As the elegant butts of the white gums
caught the glint of the sunset they seemed like
filigree work of gold. It is strange the metamor-
phosis of the butts of the white gums, so beautiful
and chaste in the sunshine, so weird and spectral
in the shadows, and often so gilded and enchased
between the lights. _ And they never seemed so
lovely as they did about the Glass Mountain.
If I were an artist I would paint a characteristic
bit of scenery between Charters Towers and
Hughenden. Folks would call me an impres-
sionist, but I would paint true to nature. The
ground would be pale saffron, growing bunches of
pale yellow spinifex. There would be colonnades
of yellow jackets, not the yellow jacket familiar
with the western shed of the Dividing Range, but
more of a native apple tree with a rough, mustard-
colored bark and scanty leaves. Then there would
be a shallow tree fringed thickly with pandanus
growing big, pineapple-like cones, and right above
it a sky like bright steel. I can see it now,
always ; but I can’t paint.
I will also remember the tidal inlets of Banana-
land—broad, mangrove-fringed, backed by fig,
beech and bauhinia scrub, and belts of banana
and cocoa-nut, cultivated by Chinese. Here
and there are clusters of palm-leaf gunyahs—the
houses of the aborigines who live by catching
turtle, or who are employed by the Japanese.
On the beach are cranes larger than any native
companions, and sometimes an alligator suns
itself on a half-submerged log. Maybe a China-
man has mysteriously disappeared, when there is
a more than usually peaceful repose about the
alligator.
The track sometimes runs through miles of ant-
heap country, country that is thick with minia-
ture Mount Egmonts, all with a lean to the west-
ward. The new-chum is usually shown this
country as a blackfellows’ necropolis.
There are parts of Queensland outside the juris-
diction of the,law. For instance, I heard it said
when the Governors were out, and later-on the
Kenniffs, that if they once reached Camooweal
district they would be safe. In this place they
tell the yarn of a teamster whose mate had cleared
out with his wife and a goat. He went after the
runaways and first demanded the wife, and when
she was refused he said, “ Well, I’ll summons you
for the goat.” I remember once, when the. engine
broke down, standing and admiring a rich, orange
sunset. Just as I was thinking the sun looked
like a huge pawnbroker’s ball with the other two
set, a enthusiastic fellow came up and started
to tell me incidents of his travels. He was an un-
usual little man for Queensland ; when he told you
anything interesting he would run back two steps
and spread out his hands; when he told you anything
funny he would run back five yards, and wave his
arms as if he were going to do club exercise. He
said he was travelling on the train, with a P.M.,
and the P.M. had arranged with the guard to keep
the train back at eacli station so that he could hold
court on the bare earth—there are very few plat-
forms to.the stations in out-back Queensland. At
one station the constable had a ease—drunken-
ness. “Ye are foined ten bob,” repeated the
constable, bawling into the man’s ear, as he was
deaf. “I’ll punch you on the jaw,’’ said the
prisoner, who had not understood the nature of
the proceedings.
In politics the average Queenslander feels he is
between the devil and the deep sea. He has lost
faith in political honesty altogether. Politically,
he is the most apathetic man in the whole of the
Commonwealth, and he has need of the most
energy. At bottom the electors are Democrats,
but delayed education and immigration, and doc-
tored rolls have been against democracy. Between
Chinese, drought, and cronk Government, it is a
marvel Queensland is as prosperous as it is.
Queenslanders who go to Maoriland to better
themselves, invariably return and tell, “After all,
Queensland is the better place.” There is little
hope for Queensland outside Commonweath
legislation, unless the agricultural people throw
prejudices aside and support the Labor party,
and by supporting it compel it to broaden its
platform.
Bananaland soil raises big crops, but poor
quality grain. An expert told me the soil
lacks lime. Want of lime spoils the horse-
breeding, also. A horse breeder told me that
noraes raised in Queensland lacked stamina. H«
saia a New England horse, or one from Monarc,
will wear out a Queensland horse if the two are
run together. I don’t believe this to be true (f
North Queensland, however, as I was particularly
struck by the splendid type of riding hacks used
up there, in appearance better than anything I’ve
seen in N.S.W. Talking of horses reminds me of
the bushman. The true type of bushman is in
Queensland; the great, soft, shy, silent fellow
who seems to be afraid of the towns. I’ve met
{V ni ih Longreach where he has seen a motor for
the first time. Fine simple fellows when you get
through their reserve; then you learn things you
did not know before.
Queensland is a vast country of many changing
scenes. There is Toowoomba with its green
pastures and red roads, a town which could be
beautified out .of recognition if only the right
people lived in it; Cairns, embowered in tropical
verdure, and Cooktown with its equable climate
and great sport and easy living. I always re-
member Cooktown first, maybe because it is
where the aborigines .flourish better than in any
°^‘* er P ar f of Australia I’ve been in. Men, women
and children flock into town every day, the women
carrying babies swathed in sheets of soft bark, or
carrying naked youngsters, which cling by the
legs round the neck and hang on to the hair.
They carry water, chop wood, scrub floors, wash
clothes and save householders keeping servants.
I went for a walk in the bush, and two young
aborigines approached me. They were armed
with spears and woomeras, and I thought that
™ might be very near me then. I said,
“Show me how you throw your spears.” I
thought it might be at me. Instead, they made a
target ,of a bloodwood, and, more aborigines
appearing,, the spear-throwing became almost a
tribal affair, each one aiming to show me how
good a marksman he was.
I wouldn’t mind living in Queensland, only for
thirst.. Thirst causes me to drink too many iced
soft drinks, and too many iced drinks cause me to
see frozen snakes. . Thirst is the ruin of nearly all
who go to the dogs in Queensland. I know dozens
of hopeless wrecks, all of them spoiled by the
Queensland, thirst. The heat is right till your
great aim is to get cool; brave the sun and it
loses. its terrors. When the heat has captured
you, it is time to come south. Don’t live naked
in a cellar with a man to throw cold water over
you and bring you iced whiskies (as a squatter
actually did), but fly, because what is prosperity
to the man whose thirst constant iced fire-watfer
will not quench ?
ROBERT BROTHERS.
The Little Bush Girl,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was a little, tired bush girl with pathetio
grey eyes and a warm, brown skin, and she had
the weary little droop of the mouth that most bush
women possess. It was after the glare of the day
had died to the more sullen heat of the night that
we left the house, the little bush girl and I, to
wait for the mail at the white gate. So deadly
still was everything as we passed through the
dusty garden that the trickle of water at the weir
and a locust shrilling in the pepper-trees were the
only sounds.
“Hush!” said the little bush girl suddenly—
“ can you hear the voices of the treeß ? Often and
often I come to this gate at dusk and listen. Just
as it grows dark I hear them. First it is a tiny
little whisper away at the foot of the hills. It
grows louder and sweeps on, moaning over the
plain, coming nearer and nearer, till you grow
afraid, for it is the strangest sound ! Then the
trees round you and behind in the dark begin to
stir and sigh till they too have caught it and are
crying—crying brokenly. Then it all dies away
in distance and in the long, grey grass.
Hush ! ” The little voice died to silence, and the
stillness grew and grew till all nature seemed
drowning in it. Then a bird cried hysterically—
and far, very far away, I heard the voice of the
trees.
“Have you ever seen a storm rise?” went on
the low voice intensely. “ Sometimes it beats up
from the south across those miles of trees. You
can hear it coming a great way off; to me it
al ways seems like the ocean warring on its beaches.
I love to watch it crossing the plain, driving the
dust in eddies before it. If they whirl high and
eddy skywards, I know it is a gale of wind, but
when the dust creeps low, and drives right and
left, there is rain—heavy rain—behind.
“It is lonely, O so lonely, this bushland, and
yet it can be very beautiful ! When the flowers
are out, if the heat has not killed the buds
beforehand, they bring a swift loveliness to the
land. The swamps are wonderful, blue and gold
with wild iris and buttercups that hide the dead
logs and broken ground, throwing sheaves of
petals on the dirty water. Often I ride out on
the plains till I am hemmed in by the sky and the
hazy hills, and the earth shimmers away from me
right and left, in clouds of white and pink—and
faint, pale blue colonies of tiny, plain-daisies.
And there are others as if a sunset cloud had
wandered, a gleaming patch of gold; where the
‘little gold pins’ cluster so thickly. And then
the wattles are out in the hollow behind the
house, and the bees drone there the long, lazy
day.”
There was a new lilt in her voice as she stopped,
and in the thin silence a night bird cried from the
swamp.
A faint breeze moved between us and she went
on. “Sometimes lam so lonely lam afraid. A
loneliness that makes you ache for the sound of a
crowd; the feel of people near you ! It is the
night that brings the fear, when you wake and
hear nothing but the birds along the river. I
long for the crowds, who have never seen them.”
She spoke so wearily and sadly. “ Streets I have
never been in. Sometimes I can almost feel them,
the passing of hundreds of people, the rustle of
many women. I can see their lips moving, their
eyes questioning, but I could never hear what
they say.”
Her head dropped on the gate bar. There was
along, long silence. Very far away in the dark
I heard the rumble of the mail; the jingling of
the chains, and the voice of the driver. Then a
swaying top light became visible, lurching through
the trees.

H. M. Mack (Manila) writes: “ Pollard’s English-
American co. (so-called) is praying at the Zorrilla Theatre,
a large barn of a place that used to house Spanish and
Italian opera in the old days. The * lilliputs ' were an
enormous success, but the ‘ grown ups ’ don’t seem to
have the same luck. Yet they do such things as ‘ My
Friend from India,’ ‘ Charley’s Aunt,’ ‘ What Happened
to Jones,’ ‘My Soldier Boy,’ and ‘Tom,’Dick and Harry’
extremely well. All the Pollard people were vaccinated
on the voyage up, and are only just recovering. A dance
was given at the Hotel de Oriente the night of their
arrival, and Mayor Brown, from Nebraska, who weighs
27st., danced a ‘two step’ (Americans only dance two
steps and waltzes) with Miss Tempest. Thermometer,
. Fahrenheit! There are only six resident Australians
m Manila who dance, and 8000 Americans ! Rain set in
as soon as the co. opened—such rain that it drowned all
dialogue on the stage. Little Jimmy M‘Mahon, of Sydney
Lyceum remembrance, is general manager, as bright as
ever, though shaved clean, so that he appears another
P°™n. After Manila the co. goes to Hongkong, Shanghai,
and then India. Miss Ada Lawrence gets the best press
notices, and her photo, appears in the papers. Mr. C. A.
Pollard is with his ‘children,’ and enjoying cigars wrapped
m gold paper at lid. each. The same cigars in Sydney
would be Is. 6d. Think of that and weep 1"
i-iA. corres P onc * en k rec kons it something of a coincidence
that on one page of a recent Bulletin appeared the
portraits of Miss Fitzmaurice Gill, actress, and Miss
Maggie Papakura, native guide at Whakarewarewa
(Rotorua, M.L.) Miss Gill was the first actress to pro-
fessmnally pourtray a Maori heroine (Kura she was
called) in a Maori drama—“ The Land of the Moa ’’—and
dressed the part exactly like Maggie in the portrait. The
scene of the play was mostly laid at Rotorua where the
geysers and the sulphur and the Sheol are. It was first
produced at Wellington Opera House under the manage-
ment of George Leitch, and after a tour of Maoriland was
taken over to Sydney, where it killed the Dacres —the
final straw which made Arthur Dacre’s ill-balanced mind
tip up was the spectacle of his beloved wife reduced to
playing the part of a brown savage and talking silly
bunkum to other savages. The drama, a hash-up of
Besant s ‘ Over the Sea with a Sailor,” was execrably
written, but Clint’s scenery depicting the eruption at
Xarawera and the Pink Terraces was as good as the best.
A Wellington syndicate ran the show and lost everything,
the scenery and properties being ultimately auctioned in
Sydney. Told of this show that it was the first and last
db record where the management hung this notice on
the office door : “ Members of the company wishing an
advance on their salaries can obtain the same on applica-
to the secretary.” Half the company was trodden under
foot m the rush to the office, and nearly every male over-
drew his salary for two or three weeks and a jiggity jag-
some time ensued. Then the notice was removed. Sophia,
Maggie Papakura’s aged confrere at Rotorua, was
specially engaged for the Sydney season of the “ Land of
the Moa.”

“O.K.”: Touching the comparative merits of the
George Rignold company of 1892 and the imported
George Musgrove co. in “ A Midsummer Night’s Dream,”
the first question that arises is, “ Does Tiib Bulletin
belittler of the present co. remember the 1892 produc-
tion ? ” I will give some of the principal names of
Rignold’s “ Dream ” company, comparing them with
present players of the same parts. Hilda Spong, then
a slim damsel of about 17, was the Fairy Queen.
Granting that she was pretty enough for anything,
the youthful Hilda was certainly not prettier
than Miss Nora Kerin, nor quite so sweetly spoken, nor so
capable of acting the woman Titania striving for her
“ rights.” Miss Watts-Phillips, now the comic “ old
woman” of the Willoughby-Geach co., spoke the beauti-
ful lines of Oberon, which Miss Dora Rignold declaims so
melodiously. Miss Watts-Phillips didn’t look the Fairy
King to perfection, and her elocution suffered from an
utter absence of the letter “r.” Miss Blanche Lewis,
never very distinguished as a Shakspearian or any other
sort of actress, took the part that Miss Maud Milton
adorns- in the Musgrove show. Miss Athena Claudius,
now a minor member of the “Sweet Nell” co., played
Helena. Melbourne people who saw Miss Cynthia Brooke
in the character will not be easily convinced that she
couldn’t look or act it better than the previous lady. Miss
Mabel Russell was the Puck in 1892. J. Cosgrove and
Cyril Keightley (the latter a raw, but very promising,
actor at the time) were the two young men lovers.
J. W. Hazlitt and Charles Hall played the Duke and Egeus
now taken by Alfred Bucklaw and Croker King. The six
mechanics of Athens were George Rignold, Stirling
Whyte, J. W. Sweeny, Joe Tolano, A. J, Ivimy and
Frank llarcourt. It goes without saying that Rignold’s
conception of Bottom the Weaver could. challenge com-
parison at any time with the more aggressive, more
playing-to-the-gallery-person that Denny presents. As
to the lesser five comedian parts there is no opportunity
for Athol Forde, Stanley Lathbury, Arthur Coe and the
others to prove their ability. “ Twelfth Night ” and “As
You Like It ” will show what they are made of. In
Melbourne the revelation of youhg Lathbury’s versa-
tility was a surprise, and both he and Athol Forde are,
to my way of thinking, much superior to any of the
comedians who supported George Rignold.”
Melba, to a New York interviewer on the subject of her
Australian triumphs : “ After I had sung crowds of people
used to wait for me outside, and the women and girls
kissed my hands and skirt and threw flowers to me. An
old lady gave me a handful of opals, saying she had
nothing else ; and the blackfellows presented me with
boomerangs. They were all delightfully familiar.” The
“ blackfellows with the boomerangs ” is a delightfully
familiar fiction, anyhow. It has been so often used that
newspaper readers in the other hemisphere think of
Australia as a place where the distinguished visitor is
beset by naked aboriginals, bringing tributes of boomer-
angs, spears, bananas, and corroborees.
